# WAYFINDING,  
# CONSUMPTION, AND  
# AIR TERMINAL DESIGN  
# MENNO HUBREGTSE  
- --- ROUTLEDGE RESEARCH IN DESIGN STUDIES -- Wayfinding, Consumption, and  
# Air Terminal Design  
This book investigates how international air terminals organize passenger movement  
and generate spending. It offers a new understanding of how their architecture and  
artworks operate visually to guide people through the space and affect their behaviour.  
Menno Hubregtse’s research draws upon numerous airport visits and interviews  
with architects and planners, as well as documents and articles that address these  
terminals’ development, construction, and renovations. The book establishes the  
main concerns of architects with respect to wayfinding strategies and analyzes how  
air terminal architecture, artworks, and interior design contribute to the airport’s  
operations.  
The book will be of interest to art historians, architectural historians, practising  
architects, urban planners, airport specialists, and geographers.  
Menno Hubregtse is an instructor at the University of Victoria where he teaches art  
and architectural history.Routledge Research in Design Studies  
Routledge Research in Design Studies is a new series focusing on the study of design  
and its effects using analytical and practical methods of inquiry. Proposals for mono-  
graphs and edited collections on this topic are welcomed.

This book contains material from two previously published journal articles\:  
Hubregtse, Menno. 2016. “Passenger Movement and Air Terminal Design\: Artworks,  
Wayfinding, Commerce, and Kinaesthesia.” Interiors\: Design/Architecture/Culture 7  
\(2–3\)\: 155–79. DOI\:10.1080/20419112.2016.1215678 \(reprinted by permission of  
Taylor & Francis Ltd, www.tandfonline.com\); and Hubregtse, Menno. 2017. “Art  
Before and Beyond the International Air Terminal’s Border Zone\: The Placement of  
First Nations Artworks in the Disparate Political Spaces at Vancouver’s YVR.” Jour-  
nal of the Society for the Study of Architecture in Canada 42 \(1\)\: 7–19 \(reprinted by  
permission of the Journal of the Society for the Study of Architecture in Canada\). Introduction  
The contemporary international air terminal is a quintessential space of circulation  
defined by security, spectacle, and spending. This building’s main function is to move  
passengers and their baggage from ground transportation to the check-in, through  
security, and onto their plane. While terminals are planned to order passengers’ move-  
ments into directional flows, they are also designed to maximize profits from shops  
and restaurants. As airports around the world began to be privatized during the 1980s,  
they started to rely on retail profits to pay for operating costs and were redesigned as  
shopping spaces \(Edwards 2005\). Many of today’s international airports recoup more  
money from retail spending and parking than the landing fees paid by airlines. On the  
one hand, planners try to design routes through the terminal that can be easily navi-  
gated, while on the other hand, they try to direct travellers to walk past retail outlets  
and services. The air terminal moves people using a stop-and-go process—passengers  
are forwarded along pathways such as corridors and escalators and are contained in  
spaces such as departure lounges and observation decks \(Adey 2007; Fuller 2009\).  
Within these architectural interiors, planners typically install visual media such as  
advertisements and artworks. Although the types of imagery represented vary, many  
of the artworks convey themes of place, flight, and other kinds of movement.  
This book illustrates how the terminal’s interior design is integral to its operation.  
While the design of the terminal’s footprint and elevation are important in terms of  
how aircraft can access its gates and how the building links to ground transportation  
networks, the terminal’s interior is the area that processes the airport’s main user, the  
air passenger.1 I concentrate on how architects and planners design the air terminal  
interior both to facilitate passenger movement and to encourage spending. Corridors,  
escalators, and moving walkways are all obvious examples of the types of things that  
are used to direct travellers along particular routes. However, there are many other  
more subtle examples of interior design that organize passenger movement and are as  
important as signage in directing passenger streams. I examine how ceilings, flooring  
patterns, and lighting are designed to entice travellers to move towards their departing  
gates. Architects designing new terminal buildings adjust the footprint such that there  
are unobstructed sightlines of the windows facing the apron; a view of the aircraft  
parked at the air bridges helps orient passengers since it clearly indicates where the  
gates are located. Interior designs also direct travellers to walk towards commercial  
spaces. In some airports, they are funnelled into a walkthrough duty-free store upon  
exiting the pre-boarding security area. These retail spaces often send travellers along a  
serpentine route to increase their exposure to the various goods for sale.  
While this book addresses a number of interior design strategies that manage pas-  
senger movement and entice spending in the terminal, it also examines how planners 2 Introduction  
deploy artworks to fulfil these functions. I argue that air terminal artworks, which  
have received little attention in the scholarship on airports, have the capacity to con-  
tribute to the terminal’s operation and should not be considered as simply decorative  
additions to the interior.2 I examine how some air terminals have installed artworks  
as landmarks to help with wayfinding and to draw passengers towards retail stores  
and restaurants. I also discuss how air terminal planners assume that certain types of  
artworks will encourage spending.  
Drawing upon actor-network theory \(Latour 2000, 2005; Latour and Yaneva  
2008; Yaneva 2013, 2017\), I consider how architecture and artworks are active  
agents that produce the terminal’s social space. I address how these things’ material  
aspects are able to influence passenger behaviour, and I discuss how airport planners  
and architects expect that their designs will perform in this capacity. For instance,  
Westfield uses artificial lighting to direct travellers towards the departure gates at  
Los Angeles’ Terminal 6 \(Herman 2016; Westfield Airports 2016\). The long, thin  
strips of LED lighting in the terminal’s ceiling emphasize the building’s main route  
\(Figure I.1\).  
At Heathrow Terminal 5, Richard Rogers Partnership has incorporated skylights  
that accentuate the required directional flow \(Figure I.2\). They run laterally across the  
building’s vast ceiling from the landside, which abuts the access road, to the airside,  
which faces the aircraft on the apron.3 For passengers in the landside departures area,  
these bands of light draw one’s eye towards the pre-boarding security area.  
Figure I.1 Westfield, Terminal 6, Los Angeles International Airport, United States, 2016.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). Introduction 3  
Figure I.2 Richard Rogers Partnership, Terminal 5, London Heathrow Airport, United King-  
dom, 2008.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\).  
In some air terminals, architects deploy artworks to support passenger navigation.  
At Vancouver’s YVR, planners installed Bill Reid’s Spirit of Haida Gwaii\: The Jade  
Canoe to operate as a way-post for orientation \(see Plate 1\). This sculpture is one of  
many artworks that the airport has installed for this purpose; a number of these pieces  
appear as icons on YVR’s terminal maps.  
I discuss how these types of wayfinding designs constitute a functional aesthetic.  
Moreover, I consider how they have an aesthetic quality that stems from their capac-  
ity to organize passenger movement. Heathrow Terminal 5’s skylights and Reid’s The  
Jade Canoe operate on a visual register, and in order for them to be effective, they  
need to clearly accentuate the directional flow or be conspicuous enough to operate as  
a landmark. My analysis of their functional aesthetic, however, does not aim to pro-  
vide an aesthetic judgement; rather, it offers a theoretical insight into how wayfinding  
designs can be useful for enabling passenger circulation.  
While air terminals are designed to organize passengers’ movements along a general  
directional flow, travellers transiting through the terminal do not all move in identi-  
cal streams. Travellers are able and even encouraged to deviate from the main routes,  
whether to shop or use the washroom. Airport planners use simulation software to  
estimate how quickly and how many passengers can be processed, and they use this 4 Introduction  
information to adjust terminal designs, operations, and scheduling \(Adey 2004\).4  
Although these programs account for variations in behaviour and attempt to recreate  
actual conditions, passenger movements in these simulations are defined by complex  
algorithms and are quantified as vectors, albeit different for each type of virtual travel-  
ler. But neither passenger simulation software nor the mathematical formulae used to  
optimize terminal designs figure in this study beyond how passenger behaviour is con-  
ceptualized using these design practices. In most cases, they do move through the ter-  
minal more or less along the intended routes and within the expected timeframes \(i.e.  
the time allotted between check-in and departure\), and that phenomenon is central  
to my analysis. However, I do consider one instance of unexpected passenger behav-  
iour where the air terminal’s design malfunctioned. On October 13, 2007, Robert  
Dziekański, a Polish national who did not speak English, became lost en route in the  
arrivals area at Vancouver’s International Terminal. After he emerged from customs  
nearly nine hours after landing, Dziekański became agitated and started throwing fur-  
niture. Four police officers, called by the airport to deal with the disturbance, subdued  
Dziekański with a conducted energy weapon which resulted in his death. This incident  
raises serious questions about design strategy and illustrates some of the implications  
of airport aesthetics. I concentrate on how the airport improved its terminal’s design  
and operations in response to this tragic event.  
Aside from my consideration of this affair, my analysis is based on a general notion  
of the air passenger\: an individual who is flying commercially, accesses the terminal by  
ground transportation, then checks in, drops off luggage, passes through airport secu-  
rity, locates their gate, and waits for their boarding call. Whatever their purpose, from  
work to tourism or family visits, all passengers need to follow this general process  
irrespective of class, income, ethnicity, and gender. Consequently, my investigation  
of air terminal design concentrates on the areas that are accessible to all passengers,  
excluding, for example, lounges limited to business and first-class travellers.  
Aside from walkthrough duty-free stores, I do not concentrate on the design of indi-  
vidual shops and restaurants in these terminals’ spaces.5 I address walkthrough shops  
since all passengers are directed through this space. Moreover, my attention to the  
commercial aspects of air terminal architecture is focused on how airport architects  
design corridors and concourses such that passengers are drawn to move towards or  
through areas that offer shopping and food services.  
I also discuss why architects and planners situate artworks near these commercial  
areas to encourage spending, and I consider how these pieces operate materially as  
well as symbolically to fulfil this purpose. Architects and planners often choose eye-  
catching artworks to entice passengers to move towards shops and restaurants. How-  
ever, they also select artworks that convey themes which they believe will stimulate  
consumption. In many cases, these artworks refer to the region where the airport is  
located. These objects are suspected to lend to the terminal’s ‘sense of place,’ and  
airport authorities presume that this manufactured ambiance places passengers in the  
mood to spend. Other prevalent themes among air terminal artworks are movement  
and flight. These works often convey a type of movement that contrasts with the  
ordered mobility that passengers typically experience while transiting through the ter-  
minal. I consider how these pieces, like those conveying themes of place, affect pas-  
sengers’ behaviour and whether they serve a commercial purpose.  
Sometimes these pieces are major expenditures. When the Vancouver International  
Airport Authority purchased Reid’s The Jade Canoe for three million Canadian Introduction 5  
dollars in 1993 \(Globe and Mail 1993; Lamb 1994; O’Hara 2003\), it set the record  
for the highest amount paid for a Canadian artwork \(see Plate 1\). The airport’s  
authority argued that these costs would be recouped since this place-themed artwork  
would stimulate the passenger’s desire to spend \(Bell 1996\). Reid’s sculpture is one  
of many Northwest Coast Indigenous artworks installed at Vancouver’s YVR that  
are intended to elicit a ‘sense of place.’6 London Heathrow paid 2.5 million pounds  
to build Richard Wilson’s seventy-eight-metre-long Slipstream \(O. Wainwright 2014;  
J. Wainwright 2014\); it was Europe’s longest sculpture when it was installed in Termi-  
nal 2’s forecourt in 2014 \(Figure I.3\).  
Wilson’s rendering of an aircraft’s supposed trajectory exemplifies how flight- and  
movement-themed artworks often connote unrestricted types of movement. Slipstream  
depicts the path of a small stunt plane engaging in rapid twists and turns, and it evokes  
a freedom of movement that contrasts with the ordered mobility that defines com-  
mercial flight. Passengers spend their flights buckled in an allotted seat and aboard  
a large airliner that usually follows a less anarchic route. I argue that works such as  
Slipstream, which convey unconstrained forms of movement, stimulate sensations of  
kinaesthesia as well as an aesthetic experience—one that resembles the Ancient Greek  
notion of aesthetics, aisth*ē*tikos, which means “perceptive by feeling” \(Susan Buck-  
Morss cited in Kester 1997, 43\).  
Slipstream’s depiction of unrestricted movement also contrasts with the passenger’s  
experience of control and containment in the airport. This is most amplified in the  
pre-boarding security zone. Zigzagging rows of retractable cordon barriers confine  
travellers in a queue leading to stations where personnel scan their bodies with metal  
detectors and their baggage with X-rays. In some instances, planners install artworks  
near these security zones to calm passengers before and after this intrusive process.  
Artworks placed at other locations are also intended to buoy one’s disposition. Slip-  
stream, for example, is intended to instil a sense of excitement for those about to  
depart on their flights \(J. Wainwright 2014\). I draw upon theories of affect \(Massumi  
2002; Thrift 2004; Anderson 2006; McCormack 2008; Pile 2010\) to consider how  
air terminal artworks can stir passengers’ moods, and I examine why diverting their  
attention away from their experience of being controlled matters with regard to the  
airport’s operation.  
Slipstream also illustrates how air terminal artworks typically emerge out of a col-  
laborative process between artists, architects, planners, and public art consultants.  
Heathrow’s design team worked with Futurecity, a “placemaking” firm, to establish  
the sculpture’s parameters and to run the commissioning process.7 The brief asked  
artists to engage with themes of “travel, movement, communication and global links  
on history and heritage” \(J. Wainwright 2014, 11\). The proposed piece had to extend  
through Terminal 2’s ‘Covered Court,’ a sprawling space situated between the termi-  
nal building and the parking garage. Wilson’s winning submission met these criteria.  
It, however, required a host of design professionals to realize the work. For instance,  
the architectural engineering firm, Price & Myers Geometrics, used film animation  
software and computer-numerical-control \(CNC\) machines to manufacture the sculp-  
ture’s 30,000 unique components \(Parker and Lucas 2014\).  
This book examines how architects influence the placement and production of air  
terminal artworks.8 They often decide where works are installed in the building, which  
themes are exhibited, and even how they should be constructed. At Toronto Pearson’s  
Terminal 1, Moshe Safdie helped determine the ideal sites for the commissioned works 6 Introduction  
Figure I.3 Richard Wilson, Slipstream, London Heathrow Airport, United Kingdom, 2014.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\).  
and, in some cases, suggested how the artist could improve their piece’s relationship  
with his design \(Hiller 2004\). While Katharina Grosse was spray painting Untitled in  
2004, he asked her to expand her work such that it touched the ceiling; Safdie wanted  
the work to appear as though the strokes would extend to the sky above \(see Plate 2\). Introduction 7  
Noel Best, an architect with Stantec, works with artists to develop pieces that are  
suitable for the terminal \(pers. comm., December 17, 2018\).9 These works need to be  
durable, appropriately scaled, and—most importantly—visually compelling. During  
the commissioning process, Stantec distributes guidelines that show images of the  
planned interior as well as types of artworks that might fit with the space. It outlines  
“challenges and opportunities” for each site and how the work should be integrated  
into the architectural design. The guidelines also define the proposed themes, which  
often refer to the culture and environment of the airport’s greater region.  
In short, artists creating pieces for airports must usually follow a detailed set of  
recommendations. Their works are vetted in advance and assessed whether they can  
meet the airport’s requirements. In addition, artists are discouraged from depicting  
any overtly political themes since airport authorities do not want to upset passengers  
transiting through the terminal.10 This, however, does not mean that all air terminal  
artworks are devoid of content pertaining to political issues. At a number of airports,  
place-themed installations highlight only one or some of the region’s cultural groups.  
At Vancouver’s YVR, airport authorities have chosen to display primarily Northwest  
Coast First Nations artworks. At Delhi Indira Gandhi’s Terminal 3, planners have  
installed a number of pieces that refer to a Hindu identity. Ayush Kasliwal’s wall  
installation, which consists of twelve enormous hands depicting mudras, welcomes  
passengers arriving at the terminal \(see Plate 3\). My analysis of these designs considers  
why airport authorities feel that a particular identity appeals to travellers and why this  
is usually driven by commercial imperatives. I also discuss why some of these place-  
themed installations matter in terms of their placement in the terminal. Schiphol’s  
Holland Boulevard contains a number of stereotypical Dutch designs such as instal-  
lations modelled after Delft Blue pottery; this corridor in the post-security airside is  
intended to appeal to transferring passengers who are spending a layover in Amster-  
dam’s airport and not entering the country. Some airports install place-themed designs  
near the airport’s border zone. These are especially notable with regard to cultural  
identity and state governance. Kasliwal’s gigantic mudras, for example, are situated  
above the screening area’s immigration officers; these hand gestures assert a Hindu  
identity in a space that vets travellers entering a country where Hindu nationalism has  
affected domestic politics and intercultural relations.  
Air terminal artworks that refer to themes of place and movement such as Kasli-  
wal’s wall installation and Wilson’s Slipstream tend to celebrate their subject matter  
and are generally not intended to provoke a critical response. There are some site-  
specific works that are exceptions to this norm. Dennis Adams’s Coda, Jenny Holzer’s  
Untitled, Niek Kemps’s Closed View, and Marc Ruygrok’s So go on at Schiphol and  
Mark Bradford’s Bell Tower at Los Angeles International Airport reflect upon the  
traveller’s experience of moving through the terminal—a building that is designed to  
organize passengers into regulated flows. These five works, which I examine in detail  
in Chapter 4, ask the viewer to contemplate some of the central themes explored in  
this book; they address how air terminals use material design and symbolic imagery to  
affect passengers’ behaviour, and they offer a critical interpretation of how air termi-  
nal artworks are deployed to serve this purpose.  
Research Methodology  
This investigation is based upon my observations from visits to forty-two international  
airports between 2011 and 2019. These include the world’s fifteen busiest airports  
in terms of international passenger traffic\: Dubai, London Heathrow, Hong Kong, 8 Introduction  
Amsterdam Schiphol, Seoul Incheon, Paris Charles de Gaulle, Singapore Changi,  
Frankfurt, Bangkok Suvarnabhumi, Istanbul Atatürk, Taipei Taoyuan, Kuala Lum-  
pur, London Gatwick, Madrid-Barajas, and Barcelona-El Prat.11 The twenty-seven  
other international airports include\: Shanghai Pudong, Delhi Indira Gandhi, Mum-  
bai Chhatrapati Shivaji, London Stansted, Paris Orly, EuroAirport Basel-Mulhouse-  
Freiburg, Prague, Vienna, Venice, Dubrovnik, Athens, New York John F. Kennedy,  
Hartsfield-Jackson Atlanta, New Orleans, Dallas/Fort Worth, Las Vegas McCarran,  
Los Angeles, San Francisco, SeaTac, Victoria, Vancouver, Calgary, Toronto Pearson,  
Ottawa, Montréal-Pierre Elliott Trudeau, Quebec City, and Halifax. I have flown in  
and out of these airports’ terminals as a passenger. In most cases, I spent approxi-  
mately two eight-hour days in each airport examining their landside and airside areas.  
This allowed me to discover the types of artworks and interior designs installed at  
each airport. My analysis of their construction and development draws upon press  
releases, the architects’ and airports’ websites, and newspaper and journal articles.  
While my investigation draws upon my research into each of these airports, I focus  
on some sites more than others—particularly, Amsterdam’s Schiphol, London’s Heath-  
row, Vancouver’s YVR, and Hong Kong International Airport \(HKIA\). These airports  
are indicative of a number of larger trends pertaining to air terminal design. Heathrow  
and HKIA typify the global airport; some of their terminals are designed by world-  
renowned ‘starchitects,’ and they are among the world’s busiest in terms of inter-  
national passengers. For instance, Richard Rogers Partnership designed Heathrow’s  
Terminal 5, which opened in 2008, and Foster + Partners designed HKIA’s Terminal  
1, which opened in 1998. Schiphol, also a major international hub for travellers,  
exemplifies how airports are designed as urban spaces and shopping destinations for  
passengers and local visitors; Schiphol Group \(2019\) has branded its design as an  
“AirportCity” \(see also Cresswell 2006\). Although YVR has less traffic, the Vancou-  
ver International Airport Authority planned its International Terminal in order to  
compete with other North American Pacific Rim transfer hubs such as Seattle, San  
Francisco, and Los Angeles \(Vancouver International Airport Authority 1995; Binney  
1999\). In addition, YVR’s International Terminal is a significant example of a ‘glocal’  
design. Its Indigenous Northwest Coast artworks assert a place-based identity in a  
transfer hub intended to operate on a global scale.  
I have flown in and out of Schiphol, Heathrow, YVR, and HKIA several times as  
a passenger in order to examine all of the accessible landside and airside spaces. My  
research into their designs is based upon planning documents, media releases, corpo-  
rate websites, and newspaper and journal articles that documented the planning and  
development of these terminals during their construction and any subsequent renova-  
tions. I also interviewed architects, planners, and wayfinding experts involved with  
these buildings’ designs. My analysis does not include passenger interviews. It does,  
however, rely on some general observations of how passengers engage with the space.  
Chapter Overview  
Chapter 1 concentrates on how architects design air terminal interiors with mate-  
rial cues that support intuitive passenger wayfinding. It outlines key principles of air  
terminal design before examining three airports—Schiphol, Heathrow, and HKIA—  
in detail. They exemplify three different types of planning approaches with respect  
to air terminal development. Schiphol’s terminal has been renovated and expanded Introduction 9  
repeatedly to account for increasing passenger traffic since it began operating in 1967.  
Heathrow has constructed new buildings to meet demands for increased space. I con-  
centrate on Terminal 5, which opened in 2008. Rather than expanding an existing ter-  
minal or constructing a second stand-alone structure, Hong Kong’s airport authority  
built a new airport on a separate site to replace the city’s former airport, Kai Tak. This  
overview of these airports’ histories and current designs illustrates how wayfinding  
solutions differ among air terminals. This chapter also examines how interior designs  
direct passengers towards retail and restaurant spaces. It provides a brief overview of  
why air terminals need to operate as shopping centres before examining walkthrough  
duty-free stores. This summary of wayfinding designs and commerce in air terminals  
lays the groundwork for the subsequent analyses.  
Chapter 2 focuses on how architecture and interior design can actively influence  
passengers’ practices in the terminal. It discusses how architects consider their design’s  
materiality and its potential to mediate passengers’ behaviour and movement. Fur-  
thermore, I argue that air terminal design consists of a functional aesthetic in relation  
to how it regulates passenger movement, and I discuss how wayfinding designs need  
to look fit for their purpose in order to be effective. This chapter also considers how  
architects assess their air terminal designs’ actual performance. A design’s intended  
function does not necessarily correlate with its operation in ‘real world’ conditions.  
I discuss this issue in light of theories of proper function and scripts which concentrate  
on how function emerges out of a design’s relation with its users. I illustrate how some  
architects examine the terminal’s actual operation and use this evaluation of its func-  
tion to refine their designs.  
Chapters 3, 4, and 5 concentrate primarily on air terminal artworks. Chapter 3  
considers these installations in terms of the theoretical framework employed in the  
previous chapter. Moreover, it focuses on how artworks are material things that medi-  
ate passengers’ movements and how they serve wayfinding and commercial purposes.  
I examine how some planners install eye-catching artworks that are intended to oper-  
ate as guides for navigation. I also discuss why they are often placed near retail spaces  
and how planners generally believe that artworks can create an atmosphere that  
encourages spending. My analysis concentrates on artworks integrated into the build-  
ing’s design and located near commercial areas. Usually, artists need to design their  
pieces to mesh with the terminal’s interior. In some cases, architects design the inte-  
rior to accommodate a sculpture acquired during the terminal’s construction phase.  
For instance, Waisman Dewar Grout Carter Inc. designed a theatre-like space around  
Reid’s The Jade Canoe at Vancouver’s International Terminal. In addition, they placed  
a food court, a café, and a gift shop within steps of the sculpture; the airport’s author-  
ity believed that Reid’s work would evoke a ‘sense of place’ and motivate passengers  
to spend. Richard Serra’s Tilted Spheres at Toronto Pearson’s Terminal 1 influenced  
Safdie’s design for the building. It is also located next to a shopping space. However,  
it runs counter to the airport planner’s central aims—its curved steel plates form an  
enclosure that blocks one’s view of the surrounding stores and signage.  
While Chapter 3 concentrates on artworks with regard to their materiality, Chap-  
ters 4 and 5 focus on what these installations depict in terms of subject matter and how  
this influences the traveller’s experience in the air terminal. Chapter 4 considers how  
an artwork’s representational content can affect passengers’ moods. After a cursory  
overview of theories of affect, I establish how artworks located near security zones can  
operate on an affective register to calm anxious passengers entering and exiting these 10 Introduction  
areas. This chapter’s remaining sections focus on movement-themed sculptures. The  
chapter illustrates how these installations usually depict forms of unrestricted move-  
ment or harmonious flows that contrast with the controlled movement experienced  
in the terminal. I discuss how they operate affectively to spur bodily sensations of  
motion, and I argue that this capacity to stimulate kinaesthesia constitutes an aes-  
thetic experience. I also address how these types of works might generate passenger  
spending. This chapter’s final section considers site-specific artworks that call one’s  
attention to technologies that organize passenger movement and that reflect upon the  
experience of being filtered and funnelled into directional flows. I argue that these  
pieces depart from the usual forms of air terminal installations and that they offer a  
critical interpretation of the functions these works are intended to perform.  
Chapter 5 examines artworks as well as interior designs that refer to the airport’s  
region. It considers these installations with regard to their symbolic content, and it  
examines the types of meanings planners wish to convey via these representations of  
place. As noted, planners install place-themed designs with the aim to increase spend-  
ing. This chapter addresses why they often choose to highlight only certain cultural  
groups living within these areas, and it discusses why these types of artworks and inte-  
riors appeal to passengers. It also concentrates on the political connotations of works  
placed near the terminal’s border screening areas. I focus on Kasliwal’s depictions of  
mudras at Delhi Indira Gandhi’s Terminal 3 as well as a series of Musqueam artworks  
that Vancouver’s YVR has commissioned to welcome international travellers. I exam-  
ine how the representation of these identities relates to the politics of the terminal’s  
border zone as well as national identity and Indigenous land rights. In the latter part  
of the chapter, I turn to natural and pseudo-naturalistic installations that aim to evoke  
a sense of place via the region’s flora and fauna.  
Other Analyses of Air Terminal Design  
This investigation expands on the existing literature on air terminal design.12 Brian  
Edwards’s The Modern Airport Terminal \(2005\) is the most comprehensive study of  
contemporary air terminal architecture. He considers all aspects of terminal construc-  
tion, including how the building fits in with the rest of the airport infrastructure,  
technical requirements such as lighting, soundproofing, and safety, and how termi-  
nals are designed to facilitate passenger movement. He supports his findings with  
numerous case studies from recently built terminals across the world. Steve Thomas-  
Emberson \(2007\) also discusses the design and layout of recent air terminal interi-  
ors, but he focuses on commercial operations and shop designs. He also examines  
how stores featuring luxury brands have increased in air terminals since the early  
2000s. Thomas-Emberson briefly addresses air terminal artworks; he discusses how  
Cesar Pelli established the parameters for the artworks commissioned for Washing-  
ton National Airport such that they could be incorporated into the building’s design.  
Marcus Binney \(1999\) provides an overview of terminal designs, though his analysis  
is not as extensive as Edwards’s \(2005\) or Thomas-Emberson’s \(2007\) books. Binney,  
who focuses on terminals built during the 1990s or under construction at the time  
of writing, examines numerous sites and offers brief overviews of the general layout  
of each terminal and details regarding their construction. Christopher Blow’s \(1991\)  
investigation of air terminal design offers a detailed discussion of the building’s overall  
structure, from connection to ground transportation to aircraft docking requirements,  
but only briefly addresses aspects of interior design such as signage and lighting. Introduction 11  
Contemporary air terminal architecture also receives some attention in historical  
overviews of airport and air terminal layout and design. Alistair Gordon’s \(2008\) and  
Hugh Pearman’s \(2004\) investigations of airport architecture since the early twentieth  
century both include a discussion of how these structures have changed in response  
to changes in aircraft design, the shift towards mass air travel, and safety and security  
requirements. Like Gordon and Pearman, Gillian Fuller and Ross Harley \(2004\) also  
examine air terminal design since the advent of flight, but they focus on the various  
systems that order mobilities in airport spaces and across space more generally—in the  
sense that the world’s airports form one global network. John Zukowsky and Koos  
Bosma \(1996\) and Alexander von Vegesack and Jochen Eisenbrand \(2004\) address the  
history of air terminal architecture in their edited volumes on aviation design. These two  
volumes both contain essays by Bosma \(1996, 2004\) on air terminal architecture since  
the Second World War, and they both examine broader issues such as aircraft construc-  
tion and their internal arrangement, airline branding, and flight attendants’ uniforms.  
Air terminal design has also received some attention in studies of airports as social  
spaces. Mark Gottdiener \(2001\), whose aim is to investigate air spaces in the same  
fashion as other theorists of everyday urban life, briefly looks at terminal design and  
signage. David Pascoe \(2001\) considers the airport as a large infrastructural space  
both on the ground and up to a few thousand metres in the air. His attention to this  
broad space, which considers how it is unique to modernity and how it affects the  
lives of those passing through them, examines air terminal architecture during the past  
century and how airports have been represented in literature, film, and art. Pascoe  
addresses a few air terminal artworks, but he does not examine why planners install  
these works and how they contribute to the air terminal’s function.13 In a similar vein  
to Pascoe’s book, Christopher Schaberg’s The Textual Life of Airports \(2012\) exam-  
ines literary representations of air terminals. His analysis of these texts incorporates  
a discussion of how the air terminal is a space defined by textuality. Schaberg draws  
in some examples of airport artworks; the most notable are those pertaining to avian  
imagery, which he discusses in his chapter, “Bird Citing” \(2012, 117–39\). He com-  
pares artworks modelled after birds and flight to how actual birds pose a threat to  
commercial air travel as “bird strikes.” He also considers how passengers, like birds,  
are offered an aerial view of the earth—a view that is now widely available when one is  
not in flight via computer applications such as Google Earth. In Airportness, Schaberg  
\(2017\) discusses everyday experiences in the terminal and aboard commercial aircraft.  
His analysis is largely based on observations from US airports and contextualized in  
relation to literature, film, and contemporary American politics. His investigation,  
which considers how a common notion of the airport permeates our collective con-  
sciousness, does not address differences between air terminals and implies that their  
designs are relatively the same. Schaberg’s The End of Airports \(2016\) includes anec-  
dotes of his experience as an airport employee. Like his aforementioned books, this  
text includes his observations of air travel and how it is represented in literature and  
film. With regard to air terminal design, he addresses jet bridges and seating, particu-  
larly Charles and Ray Eames’s modular Tandem Sling furniture.  
Airports have been a particularly salient topic for geographers, sociologists, and  
other theorists studying mobility.14 However, only few analyze air terminal design in  
their investigations. In Tim Cresswell’s \(2006\) analysis of mobility, he examines how  
movement is facilitated at various levels at Schiphol. He considers how the airport reg-  
ulates broader movements such as a passenger’s ability to cross international borders  
and how it aims to manage travellers’ movements within the terminal space. He briefly 12 Introduction  
discusses how Jan Benthem has added structural and design elements that operate on  
a visual register to order passenger flows; I address these designs as well as other way-  
finding strategies at Schiphol in the following chapter. Peter Adey \(2008\) considers  
the terminal’s spatial ordering in terms of theories of affect. He examines how plan-  
ners design locations such as security zones to trigger specific emotional responses. In  
Chapter 4, I draw upon his analysis to consider how air terminal artworks can affect  
passenger behaviour. In a separate article, Adey \(2007\) addresses how passengers are  
active observers regulated by the spectacle produced in airports. He notes that these  
strategies to contain air travellers in particular locations illustrate that airports should  
be considered as spaces of both mobility and immobility. Similarly, Fuller \(2009, 2012\)  
argues that the airport is defined by a process of interrupted movements where passen-  
gers are both stored and forwarded.15 She observes that as much as the social spaces  
of airports and air travel are marked by a stop-and-go process, the general aesthetic of  
airports and aircraft “works to inculcate a sense of smoothness and transparency in  
movement” \(2009, 72\). While my analysis of air terminal design also investigates how  
air terminals organize passenger streams into stop-and-go movements, it departs from  
Fuller’s study by examining the visual media in air terminals that convey relatively free  
movements. Furthermore, I consider how these representations of unrestricted move-  
ment can evoke kinaesthesia—the sensation of movement.  
None of the aforementioned analyses of air terminal design in relation to mobili-  
ties are book-length studies. Max Hirsh’s \(2016\) book on airport urbanism in Asia  
addresses this topic, but it does not concentrate on passenger flows in the terminal.  
He largely focuses on ground and sea transportation infrastructure that accesses Asian  
airports and their effect on urban form.16 One chapter is devoted to Asian terminals  
that service low-cost airlines; it illustrates how they have led to the growth of a new  
class of air passengers in the region. In the following chapters, I examine these termi-  
nals’ artworks, place-themed interiors, and intuitive wayfinding designs.  
My analysis expands on the literature of air terminal architecture since it investigates  
and critically assesses wayfinding strategies and artworks at the world’s busiest interna-  
tional terminals. Unlike the existing monographs on air terminal design, I build upon  
actor-network theory to consider how various design solutions operate on a material  
level to mediate passenger behaviour, and I draw upon theories of affect to consider how  
artworks and interior designs can buoy passengers’ moods. Furthermore, I offer a new  
insight into air terminal design with respect to aesthetics. My assessment of its archi-  
tecture and artworks in terms of a functional aesthetic offers an understanding of how  
these things can effectively contribute to the airport’s operations. This, however, is only  
one of a myriad of aesthetic types at play in the terminal. Movement-themed artworks,  
for instance, elicit an entirely different affective and aesthetic experience. Finally, my  
attention to air terminal artworks consists of a significant contribution to the literature  
since it is a rarely studied topic. My analysis not only offers a broad account of the types  
of works found around the world, but it critically assesses their functional purpose and  
the political connotations of their subject matter.  
Notes  
1\. A number of factors determine the terminal’s footprint and elevations \(Horonjeff et al. 2010;  
Hart 1985\). Chief are the airport’s runway layout, road and rail access, the types of aircraft  
to be serviced, and the number of gates required—all dependent on the anticipated volume  
of air traffic. Besides these requirements, terminals need to be engineered to maximize the  
efficiency of passenger flows—from ground transportation access to the aircraft. Introduction 13  
My investigation of the terminal’s interior spaces is not centered on the calculation of  
specific functional attributes. It instead concentrates on the types of materials and archi-  
tectural strategies used to order flows. See Horonjeff et al. \(2010\) for an overview of the  
strategies and formulae used to estimate these dimensions of corridors, lounges, and gates.  
2\. This book expands upon my earlier investigations into air terminal design \(Hubregtse  
2016, 2017\).  
Aside from my publications on airport artworks, there are only a few scholarly analyses  
on this topic. Bernard Flaman \(2009\) examines pieces installed in Canadian airports during  
the 1950s and 1960s. Jo Ankor \(2013\) briefly addresses an artwork installed at Adelaide’s  
airport before considering the experience of seeing an airport’s place-themed imagery in  
light of recent theories of tourism and travel. Christopher Schaberg \(2012\) addresses air-  
port artworks in his analysis of airports and textuality. I discuss his book in further detail  
in Chapters 2 and 4.  
3\. The term ‘landside’ refers to the terminal’s publicly accessible area; ‘airside’ refers to the  
post-security zone.  
4\. See Mike Lazzaroni’s \(2012\) and A. Beck’s \(2011\) reports on how simulation software has  
been used at Vancouver’s YVR and London Heathrow. Horonjeff et al.’s \(2010\) investiga-  
tion of air terminal design examines simulation models and overviews some of the formu-  
lae used to estimate passengers’ wait times and queuing lengths.  
5\. See Steve Thomas-Emberson’s \(2007\) investigation of air terminal interiors for an overview  
of shop and restaurant designs.  
6\. Throughout this book, I refer to the Vancouver International Airport as YVR since it is the  
name the airport uses to identify itself on its website and in its marketing materials.  
7\. Grimshaw Architects’ Jolyon Brewis contacted Mark Davey at Futurecity in 2010 regard-  
ing the project at Heathrow \(J. Wainwright 2014\). Grimshaw Architects designed the ter-  
minal’s satellite pier, Concourse B. Luis Vidal designed Terminal 2’s new main structure,  
which opened in 2014; it replaced the former Terminal 2 building which began its opera-  
tions in 1955. See Grimshaw Architects \(n.d.\) and Luis Vidal + Architects \(n.d\) for more  
details regarding this terminal’s design.  
8\. See Senie’s \(1992\); Senie and Webster’s \(1992\); Miles’s \(1997\); Knight’s \(2008\); Cartiere  
and Willis \(2008\); Lossau and Stevens \(2015\); and Cartiere and Zebracki’s \(2016\) inves-  
tigations into public art for critical analyses of these types of works, their commissioning  
process, and their reception among their publics.  
9\. 10. Stantec has designed a number of airports in North and South America.  
Greg Curnoe’s Homage to the R 34, installed at Montreal’s Dorval airport in 1968, is a nota-  
ble exception. Transport Canada, who commissioned the mural, removed it almost immedi-  
ately after its installation since it critiqued the United States’ role in the Vietnam War \(Sloan  
2010; Rodger 2016; King 2017\). The mural depicted a figure resembling President Lyn-  
don Johnson being struck by an airship’s propeller. Johnson drastically increased American  
involvement in the war. The work included text referring to Muhammed Ali, an American  
boxer who protested the Vietnam War. These two panels in particular led to its removal since  
the work greeted US travellers arriving in Canada. Curnoe did not limit his political critique  
to US foreign policy. He included excerpts from the Freedom Anarchists’ Weekly and text  
referring to Paul Joseph Chartier, an Albertan who died trying to bomb the Canadian Par-  
liament in 1966. The work also mentioned Louis Riel, a Métis revolutionary who led two  
rebellions against the Canadian government during the late nineteenth century. In addition,  
the mural addressed how aeronautic technology used for mass air travel often derives from  
military applications. Curnoe chose to depict the R 34, a German airship developed during  
the First World War. While it was meant to serve a military purpose, the airship was used pri-  
marily for civilian air travel following the war. One of the brightly coloured panels includes  
text of an eyewitness account of a German air raid on a London school in 1917.  
11\. This list is ordered with regard to how they ranked in terms of international passenger traf-  
fic in 2018 \(Airports Council International 2019\).  
12\. My account of this literature concentrates on book-length studies and excludes those that  
are primarily pictorial overviews \(e.g., Arai 1996; van Uffelen 2012; Eiselin, Frommberg,  
and Gutzmer 2016\).  
13\. These pieces include\: Castor Solano’s Vagamundo, 1996, at Madrid-Barajas Terminal  
1; Julian Opie, Imagine That You’re Moving, 1997, at Heathrow’s Flight Connections 14 Introduction  
Centre; and Michael Hayden’s The Sky’s the Limit, 1989, at Chicago O’Hare’s United Ter-  
minal. Pascoe also briefly refers to the works Pelli commissioned for Washington National  
Airport.  
14\. John Urry \(2007\) theorizes this paradigm in the social sciences in terms of mobility-  
systems—the infrastructures that enable people, objects, and information to move from  
place to place—and the effects of these systems on social life. These types of effects have  
been investigated in rail station literature \(Richards and MacKenzie 1986\). Urry, however,  
considers a broad scope of mobility-systems including roadways, pedestrian paths, and  
railways as well as the virtual spaces of the Internet. He argues that aeromobility is the  
main determinant of globalization. Without this mobility-system and the numerous inter-  
national social relations and links it has forged, the current global order would not have  
developed to the degree that it has.  
Ole B. Jensen’s \(2013, 2014\) contributions on ‘mobilities design’ offer a theoretical  
framework for investigating how people engage with the material aspects of various mobil-  
ity-systems \(see also Jensen and Lanng 2017\). These books do not include analyses of air  
terminal design. Jensen’s research methodology involves an assessment of planners’ design  
intentions as well as detailed observations of people’s practices within urban spaces. These  
include quantitative assessments of people’s movements. My investigation concentrates on  
airport architects’ and planners’ design strategies for managing passenger flows and offers  
only general observations of how design affects passenger behaviour.  
15\. Fuller \(2002\) argues that the directional signage in airports does more than simply order  
the space for passengers on a cognitive level. She argues that signs produce the space and  
become part of the airport’s materiality through the passengers’ embodiment of the sig-  
nage’s navigational directions.  
16\. See Janet R. Bednarek \(2016\) for a historical overview of how airports have affected cities  
in the United States.
