# Passenger Circulation  
This chapter examines contemporary international air terminals that include ‘intuitive’  
designs to facilitate wayfinding. While I address these buildings’ structural supports, eleva-  
tions, and footprints, I concentrate on how architects design their interiors with mate-  
rial cues intended to aid with passenger orientation. For instance, they often design the  
concourse with high ceilings such that travellers can see where they are in the building.  
Sometimes planners design these ceilings with skylights or other materials that give the  
sense of the required directional flow. Carpets, colour schemes, and artificial lighting are  
also used for wayfinding purposes. I begin my analysis by outlining some of the central  
aspects of air terminal design before examining three airports in greater detail. Schiphol,  
Heathrow, and Hong Kong International Airport \(HKIA\) are three of the busiest airports  
in the world with respect to international air travel. Their terminals illustrate three differ-  
ent models of development to account for increasing passenger traffic. Schiphol’s terminal  
has been renovated and expanded repeatedly since it first opened in 1967. Rather than  
creating one large terminal building, Heathrow’s planners have built an agglomeration of  
free-standing structures since the airport started its operations in 1946. I focus on Richard  
Rogers Partnership’s Terminal 5, an architectural design subjected to numerous revisions  
before it finally opened in 2008. It illustrates how architects have to adjust their designs  
to account for existing airport buildings and infrastructure. At HKIA, on the other hand,  
architects designed the terminal on an entirely new site. The airport opened in 1998 on  
reclaimed land in the water west of the city’s downtown and replaced Kai Tak, the city’s  
former airport in Kowloon, Hong Kong. I briefly discuss each of these airports’ histories  
before examining their current terminal designs. Although I address the ground transpor-  
tation networks that connect these airports with surrounding cities and regions, I omit a  
description of the rail transportation buildings and parking garages. Instead, I account for  
how the terminals are linked to these transfer points such that the passenger can move  
from the automobile or train to the check-in counter as efficiently as possible. My aim for  
this overview of Schiphol, Heathrow, and HKIA is to provide the reader with an under-  
standing of air terminal design and wayfinding, how this differs from site to site, and some  
of the reasons behind the design choices.  
While architects aim to create terminals that facilitate smooth circulation, they also  
try to design these structures as consumer spaces that generate passenger spending. The  
latter half of this chapter considers how architects incorporate retail and food services  
into their plans and how this sometimes hampers their ability to provide transparent  
designs with regard to wayfinding and navigation. Typically, the main routes travelled  
by departing and arriving passengers are lined with shops and restaurants in order to  
maximize financial return.1 Some interior designs require travellers to walk past retail  
areas, while others, particularly walkthrough duty-free stores, force passengers to Facilitating Passenger Circulation 19  
transit through a commercial space before reaching their gate. Moments of immobility,  
or dwell-time, are seen as a potential commercial boon for airport authorities. In most  
airports, the majority of retail stores and restaurants are located on the post-security  
airside; people are encouraged to loiter in or around shops and restaurants and spend  
their money as they spend their time waiting. Terminals often include a few shops and  
restaurants on the landside for people awaiting arriving friends and family members. In  
my analysis, I focus on walkthrough duty-free stores since these designs exemplify how  
planners use materials to organize passengers’ movement and encourage them to spend.  
In short, this chapter concentrates on air terminal interiors with regard to circula-  
tion, and it aims to provide the reader with an overview of various types of air termi-  
nal layouts. This summary of the material aspects of these complex structures sets the  
stage, so to speak, for the more detailed critical analyses of air terminal architecture,  
artworks, and design in this book.  
Air Terminals as Spaces of Circulation  
airport terminals are the cathedrals of our age—a huge public space where people  
gather, wait, eat, sometimes sleep, and usually shop. These are truly twenty-first-  
century buildings—fluid space for fluid functions using high technology architec-  
ture for spatial containment and cultural expression.  
\(Edwards 2005, x\)  
The twenty-first-century air terminal, Brian Edwards argues, is akin to a cathedral  
architecturally in relation to its structure and manipulation of light. This comparison  
between a transportation building and an ecclesiastical structure is not entirely unu-  
sual. G.K. Chesterton made a comparable pronouncement with respect to railway sta-  
tions \(Richards and MacKenzie 1986\). While air terminals and cathedrals share some  
material similarities, these buildings differ markedly in terms of symbolic and spiritual  
value. Air terminals and railway stations, on the other hand, are very alike in struc-  
ture and in purpose. Edwards notes that in both of these buildings the paths people  
follow in their interiors “are processional and linear” \(2005, x\). They are planned as  
transit hubs that move passengers through the space as efficiently as possible as well  
as marketplaces where travellers are encouraged to dwell and spend their money at air  
terminal retailers. Not only do airport architects need to design a space that functions  
as a passenger-processing machine and a consumption centre, they need to account  
for technological requirements that support aviation and security. While function is  
a major determinant of the design, architects also need to consider the passenger’s  
experience within these structures. Architectural historian Koos Bosma \(1996, 2004\)  
argues that the ideal airport is not simply a transfer point that moves people efficiently  
through the space but is also a place where travellers are kept calm while they wait  
and move through the terminal.  
In his essay “In Search of the Perfect Airport,” Bosma concedes that planners and  
architects have been unable to achieve “typological or architectural purity” in their  
designs since their plans need to account for a vast number of functions integral to an  
airport’s smooth operation \(2004, 64\).2 The strategies used in airports to circulate peo-  
ple, cargo, and aircraft constantly change due to new logistical and security demands,  
technological developments, and shifts in socio-political conditions. This constant  
push for upgrades to improve the speed, efficiency, and security of how passengers  
and freight are processed necessitates renovations, reconstruction, and expansions. 20 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
Foster + Partners’ design for London’s Stansted anticipated future expansions \(Pow-  
ell 1992\). It is a large single terminal using a modular construction that allows new  
modules to be added without dramatically disrupting the appearance of the whole  
\(Figure 1.1\).  
Figure 1.1 Foster + Partners, London Stansted Airport, United Kingdom, 1991.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). Facilitating Passenger Circulation 21  
Its open-spaced design contrasted with the majority of airports in use when it  
was built in 1991. Many airports, notably Heathrow, had undergone numerous  
reconstructions and renovations and were experienced as a maze of confusing cor-  
ridors \(Gottdiener 2001\). Airports built during the 1950s and 1960s needed to be  
restructured to handle increased passenger loads due to the mass popularization of  
air travel, cheaper airfares, and the introduction of the jumbo jet in 1970 \(Gordon  
2008; Pearman 2004\). Air terminal interiors were also redesigned in response to the  
dramatic increase in hijackings and terrorist attacks during the late 1960s and early  
1970s. New airports constructed during the 1970s, such as Paul Andreu’s Charles  
de Gaulle in Paris, accounted for these increased risks and were designed as heavy  
and solid structures that funnelled passengers through tightly regulated and artifi-  
cially lit corridors.  
3 Stansted is one of many air terminals built after 1990 that are  
designed as large unified structures with lightweight roofs and floor-to-ceiling glass  
walls that allow the passenger to see the terminal from end to end. From the outside,  
these glassy buildings often appear “to float in the air or to be poised to take wing”  
\(Gottdiener 2001, 70\). These spectacular air terminals have been constructed for  
new airports such as Foster + Partners’ Terminal 1 at Chek Lap Kok in Hong Kong  
or for existing airports such as Richard Rogers Partnership’s Terminal 5 at Heath-  
row \(Figure I.2, 1.2\).  
Whether it is one large open space or a solidly built structure with a maze of corri-  
dors, passengers predominantly experience the air terminal by walking and navigating  
Figure 1.2 Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\).  
Foster + Partners, Terminal 1, Hong Kong International Airport, Hong Kong, 1998. 22 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
themselves through the building’s interior. Signage and other visual cues help lead  
them through the space. At Schiphol, Paul Mijksenaar has carefully designed a signage  
system consisting of nested categories that directs people to their destination \(Cress-  
well 2006; Mijksenaar 2012\). Mijksenaar colour-coded and sized Schiphol’s signage  
according to three categories\: signs directing flows, which indicate where travellers  
need to go in the process; signs referring to fixed locations such as toilets, lounges, and  
chapels; and signs identifying specific shops and restaurants. His design has become an  
“international aesthetic of air mobility” since numerous other airports have adopted  
Schiphol’s signage system \(Cresswell 2006, 246\).  
Planners are trying to design airports where passengers rely less on signage and nav-  
igate the terminal primarily on an intuitive level \(Gordon 2008\). This is intended to  
reduce the stress experienced by travellers and mitigate the increasing number of ‘air  
rage’ incidents. Architects incorporate specific visual cues into their terminal designs  
that are intended to help push passengers into specific directions \(Edwards 2005\). For  
example, columns used to support a roof are often aligned parallel to major routes  
as are other structural elements like beams. Daylight is manipulated and is sometimes  
projected onto passageways to help guide travellers. The size of a space is determined  
by its relative importance as a route; major transit points and thoroughfares like  
departures halls are often built as large concourses while minor routes like emergency  
exits and pathways to private areas are designed as smaller corridors.  
While wayfinding is a significant aspect of air terminal design, the main deter-  
minant of the building’s overall form is the airport’s runways. These vast expanses  
of tarmac occupy most of the airport’s landmass, and they are relatively fixed once  
they have been developed. Airport planning firms such as NACO \(Netherlands Air-  
port Consultants\) determine the ground plan based on the area between the runways  
and how aircraft can best access the terminal’s gates \(Hein Baijer, Piet Ringersma,  
and Toon Stallaart, pers. comm., April 10, 2018\). NACO designed Beijing Capital  
International Airport’s Terminal 3 with a Y-shaped footprint since this configuration  
works well for long, narrow segments of land between two parallel runways. Hong  
Kong International Airport’s Terminal 1 has a comparable shape since it is also placed  
between two parallel runways. Amsterdam Airport Schiphol’s terminal is situated in  
an area enclosed by three runways and a highway.4 It consists of seven piers, each with  
a different footprint, radiating out from the main terminal structure. Unlike HKIA  
Terminal 1 and Beijing Terminal 3, Schiphol’s terminal does not have a symmetrical  
footprint—largely because it has been substantially reconstructed and expanded since  
it opened in 1967. Beijing Terminal 3 and HKIA Terminal 1, on the other hand, have  
undergone few renovations since they were opened in 2008 and 1996 respectively.5  
HKIA has added separate satellite terminals.6  
Once NACO has determined the ideal shape of a terminal’s footprint with  
respect to the runways, apron, and required gates, they modify the design such  
that it is easier for passengers to navigate \(Hein Baijer, Piet Ringersma, and Toon  
Stallaart, pers. comm., April 10, 2018\). NACO tries to limit the number of ‘deci-  
sion points’ that travellers encounter during their transit through the terminal.  
Often, they adjust the footprint such that a terminal’s piers only converge at one  
point. Furthermore, the firm tries to keep sightlines in the direction one needs  
to travel unobscured, especially if it allows for a view of the exterior apron and  
aircraft through a window. In their design for Mexico City’s new international  
airport, NACO decided to deploy an X-shaped footprint where four piers intersect Facilitating Passenger Circulation 23  
at one central point instead of an H-shaped footprint with two parallel piers con-  
nected by a central perpendicular corridor.7 The check-in area is located between  
two of the piers. NACO has decided to direct passengers to a balcony immediately  
after the check-in such that they can look down upon the terminal’s key decision  
point—the intersection where the four piers meet. HKIA has a similar configura-  
tion. Once passengers have passed through security, they enter an area that over-  
looks an important intersection. Figure 1.3 shows the view from the escalator that  
leads down to a decision point where travellers need to choose the route to their  
gate. I discuss this terminal’s layout in further detail in the following section. Stan-  
tec \(formerly Architectura\) employed an analogous design principle in its plans  
for the Ottawa Macdonald-Cartier International Airport \(Noel Best, pers. comm.,  
December 17, 2018\).  
8 The firm designed the building such that the apron and  
aircraft are visible from the check-in area on the departures floor. In this respect,  
Ottawa’s airport is an exception. In most terminals, passengers are not able to see  
the apron until after they have cleared pre-boarding security.  
Like NACO’s plans for the new terminal in Mexico City, Stantec designed a new  
terminal for Ottawa’s airport. Its existing terminal remained operational until the new  
terminal opened in October 2003 \(Campion-Smith 2003\). Clearly, when architects  
develop a new terminal from scratch, they can incorporate unobstructed sightlines  
and limited decision points early in the design process. These wayfinding solutions  
Figure 1.3 Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\).  
Foster + Partners, Terminal 1, Hong Kong International Airport, Hong Kong, 1998. 24 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
are certainly more difficult to implement when renovating an existing terminal. Next,  
I discuss the different types of wayfinding strategies Jan Benthem has incorporated  
into his renovations of Amsterdam’s Schiphol Airport.  
Schiphol, Heathrow, and HKIA\: Accretion, Agglomeration,  
and Relocation  
When planners need to expand an airport’s terminal due to increasing passenger traf-  
fic, they can apply three different approaches\: accretion, in which the existing terminal  
building is expanded; agglomeration, where a new distinct terminal is built adjacent  
to the existing terminal structure; or relocation, when an entirely new airport and air  
terminal are constructed on a larger area of land. This section describes how architects  
have applied these three respective planning types at Schiphol, Heathrow, and HKIA.  
I will briefly discuss these airports’ origins before addressing how they have grown  
over time and how their terminals are designed to enable passenger movement. This  
description of their layouts illustrates the complexity of the terminal’s interior space  
and why wayfinding design matters.  
Schiphol’s terminal has been expanded numerous times since it opened in 1967 and  
is an example of accretion. Benthem’s master plan from 1997, which depicts Schiphol  
in 2015, illustrates how the terminal consists of a number of piers radiating from a  
central structure \(see Plate 4\). The airport has gradually added more piers to accom-  
modate increasing passenger traffic. Benthem’s master plan outlined his strategy for  
further growth—some piers needed to be expanded, and new ones, such as the H-pier,  
had to be built. I describe the terminal’s layout in further detail below.  
Schiphol, located eleven kilometres southwest from Amsterdam’s city centre, was  
first used for military purposes during the First World War \(Dierikx and Bouwens  
1997\).9 In 1920, the Koninklijke Luchtvaart Maatschappij voor Nederland en Koloniën  
\(KLM\), the Netherlands’ national airline founded a year earlier, was awarded a con-  
tract to fly scheduled flights carrying mail from the airport to London. During the  
next few years, Schiphol’s operations shifted towards civilian use, becoming KLM’s  
main airport. The airport’s first passenger terminal, a one-storey building made of red  
brick, was completed in time for the city’s hosting of the 1928 Olympic Games. The  
terminal was substantially redeveloped during 1936 and 1937. The check-in hall was  
expanded and a second storey was built to house a restaurant and other amenities for  
visitors, many of whom came to the airport to see what went on outside on the apron  
and to watch the aircraft come and go.10 During the Second World War, the German  
Luftwaffe appropriated the airport. In 1943, Allied forces bombed Schiphol, causing  
the Luftwaffe to cease operations. After the war ended, the Dutch government began  
to rebuild the ruined airport \(Versleijen 1999\). Soon afterwards, it built a new longer  
runway plus additional terminal facilities with the introduction of jet aircraft in the  
late 1950s.  
In 1961, the Dutch Government approved the development of an entirely new ter-  
minal \(Kloos and de Maar 1999; Berkers and Burgers 2013a\). This building is still  
being used today, although it has expanded extensively since its opening in 1967.11  
The terminal was designed by Marius Duintjer, the project’s chief architect; Frans de  
Weger, an engineer; and NACO, which was responsible for planning the landside and  
airside operations and organizing the development’s finances, contracts, and staff. In  
1962, Kho Liang Ie Associates was hired to design the terminal’s interior. The new Facilitating Passenger Circulation 25  
terminal’s layout was based on Chicago’s O’Hare Airport. Duintjer placed the arrivals  
area on the ground floor and the departures area on the first level above.12 Depart-  
ing passengers were afforded with views of the aprons and aircraft. Despite numer-  
ous renovations, this configuration has been retained. Kho Liang Ie devised a colour  
scheme for the 1967 terminal that has remained relatively unchanged. In his design he  
only used muted colours throughout the terminal so that the signs, which were green  
and yellow, could clearly stand out from the surrounding architecture \(see Plate 5\).  
He argued that the passengers’ clothing and luggage would animate the terminal with  
additional colours. In the subsequent renovations and expansions, the tiling, walls,  
and ceilings have been predominantly neutral colours such as white, grey, and black,  
and the signage currently uses yellow with black text, blue with white text, green with  
white text, or dark grey with yellow or white text.  
Passengers access their aircraft via piers that expand out from the main terminal  
area \(Plate 4\). All airport gates are located on the piers, which are designated by let-  
ters. When it first opened, the terminal had three piers\: C, D, and E \(Kloos and de  
Maar 1999\). The C-pier was expanded in 1970. A new F-pier was completed in 1975.  
During the same year, the terminal was expanded. In 1983, Schiphol decided that the  
original E-pier needed to be larger so that it could accommodate wide-body jet air-  
craft. The new E-pier opened in 1987. In 1989, construction began on an extension to  
the D-pier which opened the following year. The G-pier was built in 1992.  
In 1985 some of the states within the European Union signed the Schengen Agree-  
ment, intended to eliminate border controls between the signatory states \(Cresswell  
2006; Berkers and Burgers 2013a\). Ten years later, the Schengen Area was established,  
and European airports had to be renovated to separate Schengen flights from non-  
Schengen flights. Most airports in the Schengen Area rearranged their terminals so  
that passengers flying to and from other Schengen states would be paired with travel-  
lers flying domestically. Schiphol, on the other hand, had no existing space that was  
allocated to domestic flights since the airport’s traffic was mostly international, so the  
air terminal had to be rearranged to account for the two different passenger streams.  
The C-pier along with a new B-pier, which opened in 1994, are for travellers flying to  
and from Schengen countries and who do not have to pass through passport control  
\(Kloos and de Maar 1999; Bosma 2013\). The E-, F-, and G-piers are for passengers  
flying to and from non-Schengen countries and who do need to be screened by border  
officials. The D-pier became the border zone between the Schengen and non-Schengen  
spaces, and it services flights to both areas.13  
At the time of writing, the B-, C-, D-, E-, F-, and G-pier all appear fairly similar.  
Each pier is a relatively long corridor with gates on each side \(see Plate 5\). Floor-to-  
ceiling windows offer views out to the aprons on both sides of the piers. They all have  
light grey floor tiling and use neutral colours for the ceilings and walls. The gates  
are indicated by signs with a yellow background and black lettering with the same  
typeface. One exceptional difference is that the wall tiling around the entrance to the  
restrooms and in their interiors are different colours for each pier. The piers extend  
from Schiphol’s lounges, areas that are in the post-security zone that contain numer-  
ous shops, restaurants, cafés, and other amenities \(Figure 1.4\). In the non-Schengen  
area, there is a corridor connecting its two lounges called Holland Boulevard, which  
I discuss in more detail in Chapter 5.  
In November 2005, Schiphol opened a new pier that is dedicated to low-cost carri-  
ers such as EasyJet \(NRC Handelsblad 2005; Brandsma 2005\). It was built with seven Figure 1.4 Benthem Crouwel Architects NACO, Terminal-West, Amsterdam Airport Schiphol,  
Netherlands, 1993.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\). Facilitating Passenger Circulation 27  
gates and no aerobridges to access the planes. The gates, which are on the ground  
level, are approached by escalators from the corridor above and have waiting areas  
with only a few seats. Passengers enter and exit the plane by a movable staircase and  
need to walk across the tarmac to and from the gate. This loading procedure mark-  
edly reduces the aircraft’s turnover time between landing and take-off—a key strategy  
employed by discount carriers to reduce their operation costs. The pier’s gates can  
either be designated as H for non-Schengen flights or M for Schengen flights, and these  
passenger streams are separated by a vertical glass wall on the upper corridor. Unlike  
the other piers, the H/M-pier does not have any shops or restaurants. Nevertheless,  
passengers do have access to shops and cafés between security and the pier itself.14  
Unlike the other piers at Schiphol, the H/M-pier has only one wall with floor-to-ceiling  
windows looking out to the aprons, and its flooring is much darker than the light grey  
tiles used throughout the rest of the terminal.  
The most substantial shift in how Schiphol managed its terminal design and devel-  
opment occurred during the 1980s. The company wanted to have an architect that  
could organize the airport’s design as one cohesive whole rather than a cluster of  
developments in differing styles \(Kloos and de Maar 1999\). The architectural firm,  
Benthem Crouwel Architects, which had designed projects for the airport including a  
bicycle shed in 1982 and a bus shelter in 1986, was approached for the job.15 In 1988,  
Benthem Crouwel Architects began a partnership with NACO.  
The partnership’s first major expansion, Terminal-West, was completed in 1993  
\(Kloos and de Maar 1999\). Like Duintjer’s 1967 terminal, its arrivals area is on the  
ground level and the departures area is on the level above. Terminal-West is built out  
of three fifty-metre modules and can be extended up to 350 metres. The new expan-  
sion and the existing terminal, which are intended to be perceived as one unified build-  
ing, are connected by an eighty-metre-long corridor. Terminal-West’s ceiling, which  
has skylights and a more lightweight appearance than the 1967 terminal’s ceiling, is  
supported by V-shaped columns. The building is encased in glass on both the landside  
and airside. On the landside, the departures level is separated from the exterior wall  
and natural light falls onto the arrivals level below. The lower level does not abut an  
exterior wall since it adjoins Schiphol Plaza, which was completed in 1995 \(discussed  
below\). Schiphol Plaza is adjacent to both Terminal-West’s and the 1967 terminal’s  
arrivals levels. In the latter building, the floor of the departures level on the landside is  
built against the exterior wall; therefore, no natural light shines into the lower arrivals  
area.16  
In 1990, Nel Verschuuren from Kho Liang Ie Associates was appointed to work  
with Benthem on Schiphol’s interior design \(Kloos and de Maar 1999; Berkers and  
Burgers 2013b\). Verschuuren had worked with Kho on the original 1967 terminal and  
its expansion in 1975. Like Kho’s design for the original 1967 terminal, Verschuuren  
used neutral colours for Terminal-West’s tiling, ceiling, and walls. She also tried to  
retain Duintjer and Kho’s design principle that views to the aircraft outside should be  
as unrestricted as possible. Verschuuren considered how eye-catching objects might be  
used as landmarks for wayfinding. On the airside of the terminal, Benthem and Ver-  
schuuren designed a bar housed within a golden dome that was also intended as a land-  
mark for navigation. Similarly, Verschuuren used artworks as locating devices. This  
included Mark Brusse’s I meet you, a pair of oversize Dutch clogs carved out of red  
stone and placed on top of a tall black plinth \(Figure 1.5\). It was placed in Arrival 3’s Figure 1.5 Mark Brusse, I meet you, Amsterdam Airport Schiphol, Netherlands, 1993.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\). Facilitating Passenger Circulation 29  
landside area. Meeters and greeters can sit at the base of the work while they await  
arriving passengers.  
In 1995, the airport opened Schiphol Plaza, a triangular building that abuts both  
terminals and contains the Netherlands’ national rail company’s station \(Benthem  
Crouwel Architects and Schipper 2010\).17 Schiphol Plaza’s ground level has a large  
central area where passengers and visitors enter from the railway concourse below, the  
passageways that lead to parking garages, or the entrances that face the Jan Dallaert-  
plein, an outdoor square adjacent to a road accessed by buses and taxis \(Figure 1.6\).  
The ticketing desks for Nederlandse Spoorwegen are in this central area along with a  
few cafés and bars. A ring around the central area forms a shopping mall that abuts  
the arrivals areas of both terminals. Skylights allow natural light to shine into the  
shopping mall corridor.  
The ceiling covering the central area in Schiphol Plaza is twelve metres high \(Kloos  
and de Maar 1999\). Benthem Crouwel Architects deliberately chose to build a high  
ceiling because they wanted to maximize the visibility of the directional signage since  
it is a complex space in terms of wayfinding—a number of different passenger streams  
converge in the central area. The firm use tinted glass for the building’s façade to allow  
for views to the landside exterior and to create a darker interior, which would make  
electronic signs easier to read. The designers assumed that people are compelled to  
Figure 1.6 Benthem Crouwel Architects NACO, Schiphol Plaza, Amsterdam Airport Schiphol,  
Netherlands, 1995.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\). 30 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
move towards lighter spaces and that passengers would be drawn to walk to the more  
brightly lit terminals from the dim Schiphol Plaza. They also used another technique  
to guide people without relying on signage; the railway concourse below was not  
entirely soundproofed so that passengers and visitors on the ground level could hear  
the trains.  
Dennis Adams’s Coda was installed at the time of the plaza’s opening. This cubic  
structure painted with red and white checkers is one of Schiphol’s official meeting  
points \(see Plate 6\). Coda’s coloured pattern is used in aviation to demarcate obsta-  
cles and hazards. On each side there is a ledge that visitors can sit on and an opening  
to the cube’s interior. This conspicuous structure contrasts with the muted colours  
of the surrounding space and is included on the airport’s maps as a landmark for  
wayfinding. Coda is one of many artworks installed at the airport which are intended  
to operate as navigational aids. I address a number of these works in the subsequent  
chapters.  
Heathrow is an example of growth by agglomeration. The airport has gradually  
added new distinct terminal structures in response to increasing passenger traffic. This  
massive complex, which is one of the world’s busiest airports, is located twenty-five  
kilometres west of London’s core. Heathrow Airport, when officially opened as a  
civilian airport in 1946, replaced Croydon as the nation’s principal airport soon after  
it was built \(Dierikx and Bouwens 1997; Gallop 2005\).18 It was originally a private  
aerodrome developed during the early 1930s. The Air Ministry obtained the land in  
1944 and claimed that it would be converted into a Royal Air Force airport. However,  
the Air Ministry’s actual plans were to convert this site into London’s main civilian  
airport once the Second World War ended. Heathrow Airport, which was renamed  
London Airport, began its operations with tented passenger terminals. Inside one of  
the tents was a W.H. Smith & Sons shop, which sold newspapers, magazines, and  
cigarettes \(this shop has proven to be rather successful at Heathrow—WHSmith shops  
are situated before and after security at each of the terminals\). By the end of the year,  
the airport had erected passenger terminals built out of the same type of construction  
materials used in prefabricated homes built after the war. In 1955, London Airport  
opened a more permanent steel-framed passenger terminal encased with glass, red  
brick, and stone. Frederick Gibberd, a British architect, designed the new terminal  
as well as a new control tower and an office building with a public observation deck  
on its roof. These buildings, which were located in a central area inside a triangle of  
runways, were accessed by a newly built 628-metre-long tunnel that passed under one  
of the runways. Many of the airlines flying out of London Airport moved into the  
new terminal, which contained numerous restaurants, lounges, and shops. The main  
part of the terminal was named the Europa Building, and it serviced flights to destina-  
tions across Europe. A western section of the building, named the Britannic Building,  
serviced domestic flights. The airlines that maintained their operations in the older  
terminal moved out of the building in 1961, when London Airport opened its Oceanic  
Building also designed by Gibberd.  
In 1966, the British Airport Authority \(BAA\) assumed the operations of London  
Airport \(Gallop 2005\). The British government handed the airport, which had received  
massive amounts of investment and had continually failed to generate profits, to this  
independent body along with Stansted, Gatwick, and Prestwick Airport in Glasgow.  
Shortly after it acquired London Airport, the BAA renamed it Heathrow Airport,  
and its three-letter code was changed from LAP to LHR. In 1969, Heathrow Airport Facilitating Passenger Circulation 31  
opened its third Gibberd-designed passenger terminal, Terminal 1, and the Europa  
Building and Oceanic Building were renamed Terminal 2 and Terminal 3, respectively.  
With the advent of the jumbo jet and the subsequent spike in air travel during the  
1970s, more and more passengers departed, landed, and transferred at Heathrow, and  
its three terminals became more and more crowded. In the late 1970s, BAA started  
developing its plans for a fourth terminal. This new structure had to be situated away  
from the other three terminals because the central area between the runways had  
reached its capacity. BAA decided that Terminal 4 would be built across the runway to  
the south of the central area, and it commissioned the architectural firm Scott, Brown-  
rigg & Turner to design the building. Before BAA could bring this massive project to  
fruition, it had to await the approval of a public inquiry. It was approved, and the new  
terminal officially opened in 1986.19  
Although the public inquiry slowed the development of Terminal 4, it was nowhere  
near as long as the public inquiry into the development of Terminal 5, which was the  
longest in UK history \(Doherty 2008\). Even before Terminal 4 was completed, BAA  
was looking to build a fifth terminal at the western edge of the land between the two  
runways. The construction of this new terminal required a major redevelopment of  
the existing site. A sewage treatment plant had to be moved, two rivers had to be  
diverted, a new roadway had to be built, and the rail and London Underground lines  
accessing Heathrow had to be extended. As expected, the proposal for this massive  
development was subject to a public inquiry. BAA spent two years preparing its case,  
and the inquiry began in 1995. The main stakeholders in the inquiry were BAA, Brit-  
ish Airways, a number of local community councils, and the Highways Agency. The  
inquiry, which ran for 525 days and closed in 1999, mainly addressed the reasoning  
behind Heathrow’s proposed expansion, the impact of a new motorway leading to the  
terminal, the airport’s noise levels, and environmental and air quality concerns.  
In 2001, the British government announced the outcome of the inquiry—BAA was  
officially approved to build the new terminal. More than a decade had passed since  
the first architectural plans were drawn for Terminal 5.20 Richard Rogers Partnership  
had won the competition for Terminal 5 in 1989, and the firm’s original plans were  
substantially altered by the time the terminal opened in 2008.21 Following the public  
inquiry, the partnership had to make some concessions such as reducing the site’s  
footprint \(Pearman 2008a\). A number of the design choices that the firm had wanted  
to employ at Terminal 5 were incorporated into Madrid-Barajas Airport Terminal 4,  
which the firm designed in conjunction with Estudio Lamela and opened in 2006  
\(Powell 2003\). The terminal at Madrid-Barajas was designed with a wave-like roof  
with multiple undulations which resemble the original plans for Terminal 5’s roof \(see  
Plate 7\). But the roof that was ultimately built over the main concourse at Terminal 5  
has one simple curve \(Figure I.2\). The parking garage, which has six levels, is a sepa-  
rate structure thirty-five metres to the west of the main terminal \(Macnair 2007; Pear-  
man 2008a\). Cars, taxis, and buses approach the airport via a large curved ramp that  
leads to the top level of the parking garage where there is a passenger pick-up and  
drop-off zone. Passengers can enter the terminal via bridges connecting the top floor  
of the car park to the departures level or through covered passageways under these  
bridges on the ground level, which is the arrivals level in the terminal. The decision to  
build the car park and the land transportation access point as a separate structure was  
partly driven by security concerns—the thirty-five-metre separation could mitigate the  
potential damage caused by a car bomb. The terminal’s main structure is essentially 32 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
the building’s envelope, which is the curved roof supported by a series of structural  
trees on the inside of the building and the four glass curtain walls encasing the space  
\(Figure 1.7\). The interior construction is independent from the exterior shell, which  
allows for changes to the interior spatial arrangement without having to do any reno-  
vations to the support structure.  
Terminal 5’s section and elevation are unconventional with regard to air terminal  
design \(Pearman 2008a\). Because of the building’s limited footprint, Richard Rog-  
ers Partnership needed to organize the space on more vertical levels than most other  
terminals which tend to expand horizontally.22 The interior space is divided into four  
storeys above ground and three below. On the pre-security side, there are two levels  
above ground that are accessible to passengers\: the departures area on the top level  
\(Figure I.2\) and the arrivals area on the ground level \(see Plate 8\). On the post-security  
side, the departures area is split into two levels \(Figure 1.7\). The top level is on the  
same level as the departures area on the pre-security side and accommodates shopping  
and restaurants. The gates are situated one level below, which also has shops, restau-  
rants, and cafés, as well as seating areas. Passengers arriving at the terminal move  
through two levels on the post-security side\: the baggage claim is on the ground level  
and the UK border control is one floor above. On the pre-security side, passengers  
need to descend to an underground level to access the Heathrow Express, a non-stop  
Figure 1.7 Richard Rogers Partnership, Terminal 5, London Heathrow Airport, United King-  
dom, 2008.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\). Facilitating Passenger Circulation 33  
high-speed train to central London, and the London Underground, the city’s public  
transit railway system. On the post-security side, there is also an underground level  
that accesses a rail line—the shuttle that connects to Terminals 5B and 5C. These are  
two satellite buildings east of the main terminal building. Terminal 5B was completed  
at the same time as the main structure, and Terminal 5C opened in 2011 \(Heathrow  
Airport Information 2011\).  
The pre-security departures level spans the length of the building and encom-  
passes a vast space \(Figure I.2\). Even though this space is relatively open compared  
to the more complex layouts at accreted terminals such as Schiphol, Richard Rogers  
Partnership has incorporated some wayfinding strategies into the architecture. This  
includes a different colour scheme for the various access points to the terminal. There  
are four bridges that connect this level to the car park, and at the entrance to each  
one there is an elevator and a set of escalators that lead down to the arrivals area  
below. Each one of these bridge-elevator-escalator modules is designated by a differ-  
ent colour; the structures supporting the elevators and the I-beams supporting the  
bridges are painted in red, orange, green, and purple \(see Plate 8\). The escalators and  
elevators that access the Heathrow Express and London Underground are situated  
in a large blue support structure nestled between the car park and the terminal and  
situated between the green and purple bridges. These are the only the support struc-  
tures on the pre-security side of the building that are painted with distinct colours.  
The columns and beams supporting the roof, curtain glass walls, and other structures  
are painted white or grey. The colour-coded ‘mobility-modules’ are landmarks in this  
expansive space and undoubtedly aid with the passengers’ and visitors’ navigation  
of the terminal. The green and orange modules are used to designate meeting points.  
Each one has a long vertical banner in the same colour that hangs in the arrivals area  
and indicates that it is a meeting point\: green is the north meeting point, and orange  
is the south meeting point.  
During the design process, BAA hired Vancouver-based Architectura, which is now  
Stantec, to conduct analyses of anticipated passenger flows in the terminal and iden-  
tify any wayfinding issues with Richard Rogers Partnership’s terminal planning \(Noel  
Best, pers. comm., December 17, 2018\).23 The firm’s team, led by Noel Best, com-  
pleted its assessments and designs by 2004, four years before the terminal officially  
opened. One of Best’s recommended changes included a reorientation of the escalators  
in the terminal’s satellite building \(Figure 1.8\). Rather than placing the upward and  
downward escalators side by side, Stantec rearranged them such that they were placed  
opposite to one another in the space. The adjusted design separates the passenger  
streams and, according to Best, prevents bottlenecks caused by travellers having to  
decide, on the moving escalator, which direction to take on arrival at the next level.  
Instead, passengers approaching the escalators will have already decided where they  
are heading. In addition to providing recommendations for more intuitive and effec-  
tive passenger flows, Stantec also recommended the location and text for the termi-  
nal’s signage.  
Some intuitive cues were already present in Richard Rogers Partnership’s design for  
Terminal 5 \(Figure I.2\). The ceiling’s materials form distinct lines running parallel to  
the direction that passengers need to travel to get to their plane, and it has rows of  
skylights running in this direction that allow light to be cast on the floor. This flooring,  
made of resin-stone conglomerate tiles, is highly reflective, and it clearly reproduces  
the band of light cast from the skylights above.24 34 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
Figure 1.8 Noel Best, Escalators for London Heathrow Terminal 5’s Concourse B, ca. 2004  
\(redrawn 2019\). Ink Drawing.  
Source\: © Noel Best, Stantec.  
Even though the departures area on the pre-security side at Terminal 5 is vast, it  
is easy to orient oneself within the concourse because of the relatively unobstructed  
views of the ceiling and walls \(Figure I.2\). Three of the glass curtain walls are visible  
from most points in the check-in area. The wall of glass facing the airside is blocked  
by a barrier that contains the security checkpoints. The curved ceiling, which has  
skylights running widthwise, is mostly visible with the exception of what is blocked  
by the barrier. On the post-security airside, the views of the ceiling and glass walls are  
often obstructed because of the two-level installation of retail shops and restaurants.  
In the spaces where there is an upward view, the ceiling’s curvature and striated sky-  
lights offer a sense of which direction one is facing \(Figure 1.9\).  
Passengers do get an expansive view of the space at two points on the top level,  
where there are balconies that offer views of the level below, of the ceiling above,  
and through the wall of glass to the aircraft outside on the apron \(Figure 1.7\). Look-  
ing down from these balconies, passengers can see the seating areas, shops, and res-  
taurants below. These types of views are not available in the arrivals area on the  
terminal’s airside, which are on the two levels below. But unlike the departures area,  
travellers rarely need to orient themselves with respect to the terminal’s layout as their  
options in terms of movement are far more limited. Signage and corridors direct them Figure 1.9 Richard Rogers Partnership, Terminal 5, London Heathrow Airport, United King-  
dom, 2008.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\). 36 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
to the baggage claim and border control. Passengers transferring to other flights are  
directed to corridors leading back into the departures area or to shuttles heading to  
Heathrow’s other terminals. Unlike departing travellers, who are free to move through  
a vast space with numerous consumer choices while they spend time waiting, arriving  
and transferring passengers in the airside arrivals area are expected to follow routes in  
one direction and only stop at the screening points, the baggage claim, and the toilets  
if necessary.  
Departing passengers appear to be encouraged to explore the terminal since they  
are not notified of their gate prior to passing through security. Electronic screens on  
the post-security airside display the gate information. For flights within the UK and  
Europe, gates are posted forty-five to sixty minutes before the departure time. For  
all other destinations, gates are posted seventy-five minutes in advance. For flights  
departing from Terminal 5B and 5C, the gates are posted two hours beforehand to  
give passengers enough time to catch the shuttle to these satellite concourses. From my  
observations at Terminal 5, there are only few travellers that take a seat and wait near  
the gates, which is likely due to the announcement of the gate information only shortly  
before passengers need to board their plane. Most passengers wait in the central seat-  
ing areas, walk through the terminal, shop in the numerous stores, or eat and drink at  
the restaurants and cafés.  
HKIA is an example of an airport that was relocated to a new site. Its architects and  
planners did not need to adjust the air terminal design according to existing build-  
ings and airport infrastructure. HKIA in its entirety—the runways, the terminals, the  
access roads, the train station, etc.—were built on a new site that was reclaimed from  
the water west of Hong Kong \(Figure 1.10\). HKIA, also known as Chek Lap Kok, was  
built to replace the city’s former airport, Kai Tak, in Kowloon, Hong Kong, which  
had been in operation since 1925 \(Government of the Hong Kong Special Administra-  
tive Region n.d.\). Kai Tak, which was located near a dense urban area, was reaching  
capacity and could not be expanded to deal with the expected rises in passenger traf-  
fic. The new airport was built upon two islands and a large portion of reclaimed land  
surrounding them. Lam Chau Island and Chek Lap Kok Island, which had a mountain  
one hundred metres high, were reconfigured to have a flat plane with an elevation of  
ca. 6.5 metres extending 6 kilometres in length and 3.5 kilometres in width \(Pearson  
1998\).25 At the time of the airport’s opening in 1998, HKIA’s Terminal 1 was the  
world’s largest air terminal \(Pearson 1998\). It encompassed ca. 550,000 square metres  
of floor area and was designed to process thirty-five million passengers per year. The  
airport’s planners had already accounted for potential expansions; these would come  
in the form of satellite terminals.26  
HKIA was perceived as the last major undertaking by the British in Hong Kong  
before the former British colony was handed over to China in 1997 \(Glanville 1998;  
MacQueen 1998\). The consortium that won the commission in March 1992—Foster +  
Partners, BAA, and Mott Connell—was largely British, and the design for the most  
impressive structure on the site, Terminal 1, derives from an air terminal on British  
soil. Foster + Partners based their plans on what they had learned while designing  
London’s Stansted Airport, which opened in 1991.27 Pearman \(2008b\) argues that  
when Stansted opened it truly was a “revolutionary” new design, comparable to Eero  
Saarinen’s Dulles Airport in Washington D.C. which opened in 1962. At Stansted,  
Foster + Partners redefined airport design by moving the baggage handling systems  
to the basement and opening up the terminal space under one large roof \(Figure 1.1\). Figure 1.10 Foster + Partners, Terminal 1, Hong Kong International Airport, Hong Kong,  
1998\. Aerial view.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\). 38 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
Like Stansted, HKIA’s Terminal 1 is built with modules that are repeated in space.  
Foster + Partners’ plans for a standardized module at Terminal 1 allowed for an eco-  
nomical and flexible design.28 It differs from the design at Stansted, where each square  
module curves in two directions and has a central skylight. At Terminal 1 the module  
is designed with one longitudinal curve, and the succession of these modules give the  
sense of a directional flow across the length of the airport \(Figure 1.11\). At Stansted,  
on the other hand, the square modules read more like a grid that expands across two  
axes in space. The longitudinal module at HKIA Terminal 1 exemplifies the functional  
aesthetic concerning circulation which I examine in Chapter 2. It operates as a cue to  
entice passengers to move along this axis, which is the general direction they need to  
follow as they walk from the check-in to the gate.  
Foster + Partners used these modules to create a symmetrical design with the north  
side of the terminal mirroring its south side \(Figure 1.10\). The building was designed  
not only with an attention to how it would look on the inside but also from the out-  
side with a view from above \(Davey 1998\). The building’s plan, which arriving and  
departing passengers can see from the window of their jet, “recalls the footprint of a  
primitive aeroplane” \(Edwards 2005, 187\). On the terminal’s east side, a series of nine  
barrel vaults cover the landside departures area, which is a large rectangular space.  
Immediately adjacent to this space on the west is a triangular space also covered by  
Figure 1.11 Foster + Partners, Terminal 1, Hong Kong International Airport, Hong Kong,  
1998.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\). Facilitating Passenger Circulation 39  
nine barrel vaults, which contains the airside shopping spaces and food court. At the  
base of this triangle, there are two rectangular wings each composed of five barrel  
vaults that extend north and south, which contain a few of the terminal’s gates. The  
central barrel vault connected to the central rectangular and triangular spaces projects  
towards the west before forking off into two angled wings each composed of eight  
barrel vaults. This Y-shaped concourse contains most of the terminal’s gates. The long  
central vault, when seen inside the building, exemplifies the sense of the lengthwise  
flow through the terminal \(Figure 1.11\).29  
Hong Kong’s Terminal 1 is arranged such that the arrivals level is below the depar-  
tures level. Passengers arriving at the gates are funnelled through this lower level to  
the Hong Kong border screening area or over to connecting flights. Travellers arriv-  
ing at gates on the terminal’s west side are directed to take a shuttle train that runs  
below the arrivals level along the long central concourse. Passengers departing from  
the gates at the building’s west end can take this shuttle train in the other direction to  
avoid walking the length of the central concourse. This train also accesses the Mid-  
field Concourse, a satellite terminal which opened in 2016. In Terminal 1, departing  
passengers must pass down a series of escalators located shortly after the pre-security  
baggage check on the building’s east side to access the train. Arriving and departing  
travellers do not encounter one another on the shuttle train, as they are directed along  
separate routes and enter and exit the shuttle at different points. Upon exiting the  
train, arriving passengers are ferried up escalators and deposited near the Hong Kong  
border screening zone.  
Arriving passengers remain on this same level as they collect their luggage from the  
baggage claim and pass through the gateway to the landside area. From here they can  
walk through the space where meeters and greeters await and head directly to the  
high-speed train to Hong Kong, Kowloon, and Tsing Yi without needing to change  
levels \(Figure 1.2\).30 This rail station, which was also designed by Foster + Partners,  
accords with the design logic of the air terminal. Trains arriving at HKIA from the  
city stop on a level of the rail station that is connected by a series of bridges to the  
terminal’s check-in area.  
These bridges are one of two sets of bridges that cross an expansive space in Ter-  
minal 1’s landside area \(Figure 1.2\). In this respect, the building parallels Heathrow  
Terminal 5 whose space is likewise defined by bridges leading to the various transport  
systems accessing the airport. Though unlike Heathrow Terminal 5, the bridges are  
angled ramps going both up and down to different levels of access points on the exte-  
rior of the building.31  
The angled ramps that cross through Terminal 1’s concourse on the landside area  
are placed in a symmetrical fashion, as are the escalators in this space. From Terminal  
1’s landside arrivals area there is a central escalator and lift module that leads to the  
departures area above. There are also two sets of escalators leading from the arrivals  
level to the departures level on the north and south sides of the terminal. The angled  
ramps are divided into two sets of four and are situated between the central escala-  
tors and the escalators near the exterior sides. These two sets of ramps are in a mirror  
image of each other. Starting with the building’s centre and moving to the terminal’s  
exterior, they are arranged as follows\: train to check-in, roadway to check-in, train  
to check-in, and roadway to check-in. Under the outermost ramps on both sides of  
the terminal, there are escalators that lead down to the arrivals level from the corri-  
dor connecting to the arriving trains. It appears that these were built for the meeters 40 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
and greeters that come to the airport via the high-speed rail line as these escalators  
are in line with the two points where arriving passengers exit from the airside to the  
landside. In terms of wayfinding, this symmetrical layout of these ramps differs from  
the coloured mobility-modules at Heathrow Terminal 5. Although there is no dif-  
ferentiation between each ramp along the width of the terminal, these structures run  
lengthwise across the terminal’s space and emphasize the same directional flow as the  
ceiling’s longitudinal modules.  
The departures level is also a symmetrical design. There are two security check-  
points, one on the north side and the other on the south. In between these checkpoints  
is a two-level restaurant and shopping area. Restaurants on the second level offer  
views across the concourse’s large expansive space. At the top of the escalators on the  
second level, airport visitors can look through a glass barrier to the food court on the  
terminal’s airside. In addition, they can look down to the movement of passengers  
on the arrivals level before they have passed through the border screening zone and  
picked up their luggage from the baggage claim area. This vantage point also allows  
for a view along the terminal’s long central vault.  
Upon exiting security on either the north or the south side of the terminal, pas-  
sengers enter a shopping space. Between these two shopping spaces is a food court,  
which has seating that offers views down the concourse as well as down to some of  
the shops on the level below \(see Plate 9\). This upper level of the airside departures  
area is situated above an extensive shopping mall on the departures area’s lower floor.  
Passengers must take an escalator down to this lower level as all of the terminal’s  
gates are accessed from the floor below \(Figure 1.3\). While moving down the escala-  
tor, passengers get a clear view of a significant decision point on the lower level. It is a  
three-way intersection between routes leading to gates along the long singular central  
vault and the gates in the north and south wings on the terminal’s east side. This inter-  
section also has escalators that descend to the underground rail line connecting to the  
two wings at the terminal’s western side and another set of escalators that descend to  
the waiting areas for gates 501–530. These waiting areas are situated on the same  
level as the tarmac. Unlike the gates above where travellers board their planes via air  
bridges, passengers are driven from these gates directly to their aircraft by bus or shut-  
tled to the North Satellite Concourse building that houses gates 501–510.  
This tarmac level is one of the few areas in the terminal where the interior archi-  
tecture does not have a symmetrical arrangement. The interior architecture of the  
passenger-accessible areas on the floors above are designed such that the north and  
south sides of the terminal more or less mirror each other along the building’s east-west  
axis. This includes the more permanent architecture in the space such as escalators,  
people movers, bridges, and security check points. As to be expected, the distribution  
of shops, restaurants, and advertisements is not symmetrical along this axis. As noted,  
the barrel vaults are also arranged symmetrically along the building’s east-west axis  
\(Figure 1.10\). This interior space’s symmetry contrasts with air terminals that have  
grown via accretion such as Schiphol. One of the factors that allowed Foster + Part-  
ners to design a terminal with a symmetrical footprint and a symmetrically ordered  
system of passenger routes was the nature of the site. By starting with a vacant site and  
erecting an entire airport from scratch, the planners could integrate the designs for the  
terminal, the high-speed rail station, and the roadways.32  
In June 2007, a second terminal opened at HKIA \(Kang-chung 2007; Skid-  
more, Owings & Merrill LLP n.d.\). Skidmore, Owings & Merrill LLP designed the Facilitating Passenger Circulation 41  
30,000-square-metre building that contains Terminal 2 and SkyPlaza, a shopping mall  
that is part of the terminal’s structure. The new terminal’s purpose was to reduce some  
of the congestion at Terminal 1. Terminal 2, which was expected to process up to fif-  
teen percent of the passengers departing from HKIA, increased the number of check-in  
counters at the airport but not the number of gates. Passengers checking in at Terminal  
2 pass through security in the building and then need to board an underground train  
that travels to the gates at Terminal 1.  
Terminal 2’s design is somewhat different than Terminal 1. Its ceiling does not  
suggest the same type of directional flow as the ceiling at Terminal 1 as it has a series  
of undulating ripples running in multiple directions, similar to the types of waves  
formed on a lake’s surface by a light breeze. As with Terminal 1, it can be accessed  
by the high-speed rail line from Hong Kong, Kowloon, and Tsing Yi. Passengers  
travelling to Terminal 2 exit the train at the same stop as Terminal 1, but instead of  
exiting by the doors facing west, they must exit the doors facing east. Terminal 2  
also has a series of bridges that run from the building’s entrance to the departures  
area \(Figure 5.8\). There are two bridges connecting from the train station that slope  
downwards to the check-in counters, and there are four bridges that slope upwards  
from the roadway to the check-in counters. They are arranged symmetrically in  
the space\: on the terminal’s south side there is a bridge leading to the train situated  
between two bridges leading to the road, and this is repeated on the terminal’s north  
side. Beside each set of bridges, on the side facing the exterior wall, there are esca-  
lators leading to the floor below. This floor, however, is not an arrivals level—all  
passengers arriving at HKIA exit the airport via Terminal 1. This space is reserved  
for coach transport to mainland China. There are ticket counters, waiting areas,  
and bays leading to the buses. On the west-facing side of this floor, there are three  
tunnels that run under the high-speed rail line and connect with the landside area  
of Terminal 1.  
On the floor above, passengers and visitors can visit SkyPlaza. This shopping mall is  
situated to the east of the check-in counters. It has an upper floor which offers views  
to the shops below and westwards to the check-in area and the bridges accessing the  
rail station and road. Passengers checking in at Terminal 2 need to transit through  
SkyPlaza’s lower level as the escalators leading down to the pre-boarding security area  
are behind the mall’s east side.  
The Air Terminal and Commerce\: A Spectacular  
Consumption Centre  
Schiphol’s, Heathrow’s, and HKIA’s terminal buildings include a variety of shops and  
restaurants in their landside and airside areas, and some of these commercial spaces  
resemble contemporary shopping mall interiors \(Figure 1.4, 1.7, 1.9; see Plate 9\).  
Air terminals began to incorporate more stores and services after the introduction of  
wide-bodied jets in the early 1970s \(Gottdiener 2001\). When airports were reconfig-  
ured as larger spaces to account for increased passenger loads, planners realized that  
longer walking distances to and from the aircraft could be used to expose travellers  
to more retail frontage and advertising. The push to maximize consumer spending in  
the terminal increased in the 1980s when airports began to be privatized \(Rau 1996\).  
Dubai International’s Terminal 3, Seoul Incheon’s Terminal 1, Charles De Gaulle’s  
Terminal 2F, HKIA Terminal 1, and Heathrow Terminal 5 are all examples of how air 42 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
terminal design is now focused on creating large open spaces with an array of stores  
and restaurants on the airside.  
Some airports include shopping malls on their landside, and they are meant to serve  
passengers, visitors, and staff. HKIA’s SkyPlaza, for instance, is intended to attract  
passengers departing from both Terminal 1 and 2, local residents, and people travel-  
ling from SkyPier, a ferry pier connected to HKIA \(Ma 2006\). Within its first two  
years of operation, the mall did not attract the number of consumers that Hong  
Kong’s airport authority had hoped. In 2009, Ng Yuk-hang from the South China  
Morning Post reported that eleven out of the one hundred shops at SkyPlaza had  
closed and a number of the businesses were struggling. At Terminal 1, on the other  
hand, all of the available commercial spaces were occupied by shops and restaurants.  
Yuk-hang relayed that one of the shop’s employees thought that Terminal 2 attracted  
only few consumers because most travellers transiting through HKIA did not know  
that the terminal existed and that most passengers tend to shop once they have passed  
through pre-boarding security. During my visits to HKIA in August and Septem-  
ber 2014, I noticed that there were far fewer people in Terminal 2’s shops than those at  
Terminal 1.  
At Schiphol, planners have also added a shopping mall in Schiphol Plaza that is  
intended to serve non-flying consumers visiting the “AirportCity” \(Pearman 2004\).33  
Unlike HKIA’s SkyPlaza, most passengers and visitors pass through this mall since  
it is located between the airport’s primary entrance points and the terminal’s depar-  
tures and arrivals areas. Moreover, Schiphol Plaza’s shops ring around the space that  
includes the travellators leading up from the railway platforms below, the walkway  
that leads to the parking garage, and the building’s ground level entrance which  
serves those who arrive by bus transportation. Departing passengers dropped off  
by automobile, however, do not transit through Schiphol Plaza since they enter the  
building one level above, and they pass through a doorway that leads directly to the  
check-in area.  
On the airside, Schiphol has concentrated its shopping areas in the lounges at the  
base of each pier. Mark Pimlott \(2008\) contends that Schiphol is the ideal realiza-  
tion of a “continuous interior,” a building type consisting of an expansive interior  
space filled with an innumerable amount of consumer goods and advertisements.  
The continuous interior originated in the nineteenth century within the Parisian  
arcades and the 1851 Crystal Palace in London. In his unfinished The Arcades  
Project, Walter Benjamin \(2002\) considers the experience of the nineteenth century  
flâneur meandering through the former’s spectacular glass-covered spaces of con-  
sumption. During the twentieth century, the continuous interior has been expressed  
within other consumer paradises such as shopping malls, casinos, and theme parks.  
Even though these interiors give the “impression of freedom,” they are tightly regu-  
lated spaces engineered to stimulate spending through advertising and spectacle  
\(Pimlott 2008, 76\).  
Drawing from Jonathan Crary’s \(1999\) analyses of the management of attention  
and techniques of observation during the nineteenth century, Peter Adey \(2007\) con-  
siders how spectacular elements are deployed in the terminal to facilitate passenger  
movement.34 He discusses how airport designers try to capture people’s attention  
using information screens, windows, and balconies in order to encourage them to  
dwell in specific airport spaces. Observation points looking out to the landscape and  
air aprons or over the terminal’s interior space are often surrounded by restaurants Facilitating Passenger Circulation 43  
and shops. Information screens are placed close to departure gates to try to engage the  
passengers’ attention and to keep them from wandering off and missing their board-  
ing call. Adey notes that these screens are also strategically placed in spaces next to  
shops and services. Heathrow’s, Gatwick’s, and Stansted’s current operation employs  
this strategy; the flights’ gate numbers are not listed until shortly before the departure  
time, and this information is displayed on screens in corridors and waiting areas adja-  
cent to the terminal’s shopping spaces \(Figures 1.7 and 1.12\). In Chapter 3, I consider  
how eye-catching air terminal artworks are intended to capture the passenger’s atten-  
tion and draw them towards consumer spaces.  
Denis Cosgrove \(1999\) compares the airport’s scopic and kinetic regulation of  
passengers to strategies used in Georgian parks. He argues that the planner’s careful  
engineering of viewpoints in airport spaces resembles techniques used by eighteenth-  
century English landscape designers to direct people’s trajectories such that their sight  
lines were oriented to perceive various manufactured perspectival effects. Cosgrove’s  
essay, “Airport/Landscape,” was published in 1999. Approximately ten years later,  
a number of duty-free shops at international air terminals began to be redesigned  
as ‘walkthrough’ shops with serpentine routes that recall eighteenth-century English  
gardens. Yet, instead of being surprised by a folly after a bend in the footpath, pas-  
sengers encounter a display case with the latest fashions, aromatic perfumes, high-tech  
electronics, or aged spirits.  
Figure 1.12 Retail Street, London Stansted Airport, United Kingdom, 2014.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). 44 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
Walkthrough Duty-Free Stores  
Once passengers pass through security at Heathrow Terminal 5, Schiphol, and HKIA,  
they must walk through an area with restaurants and shops before they can reach their  
gate. In these airports, these areas are designed with passageways and corridors that  
are distinct from the shops and restaurants \(Figures 1.4 and 1.9\). They are demarcated  
with storefront signage and face the passageways. They also have a distinctively differ-  
ent design than the passageways which are part of the larger spaces in the concourse.  
This contrasts with airports such as Gatwick, Stansted, and Vancouver’s YVR where  
passengers exiting security are led along a corridor and funnelled into a walkthrough  
duty-free shop \(Figure 1.13; see Plate 10\).  
World Duty Free Group opened its walkthrough stores at Gatwick Terminal South and  
YVR in 2012 and at Stansted in 2014 \(Amor 2012; Morton 2012; Barras-Hill 2014\).35  
Unlike the corridors and passageways throughout the rest of the terminal, which are gen-  
erally rectilinear, the passageway running through these stores is a curvilinear path lined  
with chocolates, perfumes, alcohol, and jewellery. Furthermore, passengers encounter  
sales attendants offering samples of liquor and spritzes of scents. These worlddutyfree  
shops are an artificially lit low-ceilinged space that contrasts with the adjoining terminal  
spaces, which have high ceilings plus skylights. The passageway in each shop has a slightly  
different design than the rest of the store to demarcate it as the main route to be followed.  
The floor tiling designating the direction is black while the surrounding tiling is white.  
The ceiling designs at these worlddutyfree shops correspondingly accentuate the route  
through the store. At YVR, the ceiling above the passageway has white panels that form  
an undulating wave that runs from the shop’s entrance to the exit. At London Gatwick’s  
South Terminal, the ceiling above the black walkway is flat and painted white. Some of the  
panels above the shopping spaces are white and have a wave-like S-shape. At Stansted, the  
ceiling above the route consists of flat thin black strips. It contrasts with the curvilinear-  
shaped white ceiling that delineates the surrounding retail area.  
In each of these stores, the passenger is sent in a somewhat curvilinear path from  
one end of the shop to the other. At Gatwick Terminal South travellers are sent in a  
long arc where the entrance and exit points are relatively close with respect to the  
terminal’s built space. In each of these worlddutyfree shops passengers are directed  
along a longer route than if the pathway followed a single straight trajectory from the  
entrance to the exit.  
A similar strategy was employed at the Dufry duty-free store at EuroAirport Basel-  
Mulhouse-Freiburg when it opened in 2006.36 Unlike the worlddutyfree shops at  
Stansted, Gatwick, and YVR where passengers are sent through the store in a one-  
directional flow, travellers at EuroAirport’s duty-free store encounter a three-way inter-  
section at its centre; it is the node where three of the terminal’s post-security wings,  
each containing gates, meet. This planning strategy runs counter to NACO’s designs  
for wayfinding discussed earlier. This crucial decision point at EuroAirport does not  
include any views of the wings where passengers ultimately need to go. Instead, they  
are surrounded by advertisements and display stands with spirits, perfumes, candy,  
chocolates, watches, and sunglasses. Travellers must rely on signage to find the way  
to their gate. At Stansted’s, Gatwick’s, and YVR’s worlddutyfree shops, passengers  
do not encounter any decision points—they must simply follow the curvilinear path.  
Kuala Lumpur’s Terminal 2, completed in 2014, funnels passengers departing  
from the P- and Q-gates through the Eraman duty-free store \(Figure 1.14\). Unlike Figure 1.13 worlddutyfree shop, International Terminal, Vancouver International Airport,  
Canada, 2012.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). 46 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
the shops described previously, the passageway is not delineated with a floor design.  
It has a long sinuous light fixture suspended from the ceiling that guides passen-  
gers towards the gates. A series of round signs listing product types hang from this  
fixture. This sign’s shape includes a directional arrow that points to the product’s  
Figure 1.14 Eraman Store, Terminal 2, Kuala Lumpur International Airport, Malaysia, 2014.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). Facilitating Passenger Circulation 47  
location in the store. Below each sign’s text is a colourful image of figures with  
shopping bags, an aircraft, and Kuala Lumpur’s iconic Petronas Towers. Charles  
De Gaulle’s first walkthrough duty-free store, which opened in 2012, also includes  
signs that refer to the airport’s location \(Airport World 2012\).37 Its signage depicts  
landmarks such as the Eiffel Tower, and its ceiling is meant to recall the Parisian  
galleries.  
Walkthrough shop designs are not popular with all airport architects and planners.  
At Schiphol, Benthem has maintained a design ethos that an air terminal should facili-  
tate passenger flows with as little disruption as possible \(de Wijn 2013\). This ideal of  
creating a “zero-friction society” also informed Kho’s interior design for Schiphol’s  
terminal, where the main focus was to create clearly defined passageways, and com-  
mercial operations, which were a secondary concern, were to be located on the periph-  
ery of the routes. In 2006, Schiphol’s management wanted to create routes that forced  
passengers to walk through shops, but Benthem disagreed with this planning initiative  
and decided to resign over the issue. Benthem argues that this strategy runs counter  
to what is acceptable in designing urban spaces\: “in Amsterdam, people walk to the  
Dam past the shops in the Kalverstraat, not through the shops” \(cited in de Wijn  
2013, 131\). These plans, however, were abandoned, and Benthem stayed on board as  
the airport’s architect.  
The installation of a walkthrough shop at Stansted is noteworthy. As discussed  
previously, Norman Foster’s design for this terminal was revolutionary when  
it opened in 1991 since it offered passengers views of the terminal in all direc-  
tions \(Figure 1.1\). This innovative terminal design inspired other open concourses  
that similarly offer passengers an outlook of the interior, which, as noted, aids  
in terms of wayfinding. At Stansted these views are now obscured in the world-  
dutyfree shop, which opened in 2014, as well as in the ‘retail street’ that travellers  
encounter upon exiting the walkthrough duty-free store. In this area, passengers  
are directed along a route with distinctive shopfronts on each side \(Figure 1.12\).  
Unlike the passageway running through the duty-free store, this route is not cov-  
ered by a separate ceiling and the terminal’s modular roof is visible. This retail  
street also directs passengers along a circuitous route—one that follows a long arc  
and ends in a waiting area lined with more shops. This space has limited seating  
and large screens displaying flight information. Passengers are intended to stay  
in this space, the retail street, or the duty-free shop since the gate number is not  
displayed until fifty minutes prior to the flight.38 The actual distance between the  
worlddutyfree store’s exit and the waiting area is much shorter than the length of  
the curvilinear retail street that travellers are required to follow. Clearly, Stansted’s  
planners have chosen to force passengers to walk longer distances with the aim to  
generate profits from retail sales.  
Conclusion  
Today’s international air terminals share the same functions but are defined by dif-  
ferent architectural and interior designs. Their development is affected by multiple  
variables such as existing infrastructure at the airport sites, the anticipated number of  
passengers to be processed, technological requirements, economic and political condi-  
tions, and aesthetic choices made by the architects and interior designers. However,  
their designs are also defined by a few key planning principles. For instance, architects  
and planners aim to design their air terminals such that they are easy to navigate. In 48 Facilitating Passenger Circulation  
some cases, architects try to clarify the dominant routes by incorporating views of the  
apron on the exterior or including material cues such as skylights that emphasize the  
required directional flow. These design strategies illustrate how architects aim to cre-  
ate designs that allow for a smooth circulation of passenger traffic. However, airport  
authorities also aim to interrupt these flows for commercial gain. Moreover, generat-  
ing profits from retail and food sales is a central concern, and planners aim to direct  
passengers to walk past or through retail areas. Walkthrough duty-free stores are  
particularly effective for this purpose since they force travellers to pass through a retail  
space on their way to the gate, and, in some cases, they are directed to move along a  
serpentine route in order to expose them to more consumer goods.  
The overview in this chapter illustrates the key tenets of air terminal design, and it  
underpins the analyses in this book. Moreover, in the subsequent chapters I address  
these types of designs in further detail, and I offer theoretical assessments of how they  
serve wayfinding purposes, how they might entice passengers to spend, and how they  
work in conjunction with artworks to affect the traveller’s experience in the terminal.
