1\. Brian Edwards notes that the British Airport Authority  
earns over £700 million per year from retail-type activities at its UK airports, which  
exceeds by £100 million the amount of money that the company spends each year on  
building operations of various kinds. Viewed in this way retail revenues pay for termi-  
nals without the additional income from landing fees.  
\(2005, 148\)  
During 2000 and 2001, Sydney International generated thirty-one percent of its revenue  
from retail shops and services, an amount that exceeded the profits garnered from its avia-  
tion operations such as landing fees\(Lloyd 2003\).  
2\. The planning of contemporary air terminals also illustrates how architectural designs need  
to account for a number of factors including budget, political pressures, community con-  
cerns, and technological requirements. See Arindam Dutta’s \(2012\) discussion of how the  
planning process involves a number of professional practitioners in order to manage these  
various details.  
3\. Martha Rosler’s \(1998\) photographs of air terminals include a number of these types of cor-  
ridors. See also Anthony Vidler’s \(2000\) consideration of Rosler’s photographs as expres-  
sions of the passenger’s experience of waiting and anxieties associated with air travel.  
4\. Schiphol has six runways in total. In addition to the three runways adjacent to the terminal  
building, there is one to the east and two to the west which are accessed by taxiways that  
cross over a highway.  
5\. Beijing’s Terminal 3 was designed with three sections\: C, D, and E. All three were opera-  
tional when the airport opened in time for the 2008 Olympic Games. Section D was closed  
shortly after the Games. In April 2013, it was reopened to account for increased passenger  
traffic \(China Daily 2013\).  
6\. HKIA’s North Satellite Concourse began operating in 2010, and the Midfield Concourse  
was fully operational in 2016 \(Hong Kong Government News 2011, 2016\).  
7\. Mexico City’s new airport is expected to open in 2020. Foster + Partners and FR-EE, the  
airport’s chief architects, commissioned NACO to design the terminal’s footprint as well  
as the control tower, parking garage, and public transportation access points \(Netherlands  
Airport Consultants n.d.\).  
8\. For a brief overview of Stantec’s design for Ottawa’s airport, see Trevor Boddy \(2017\).  
Stantec acquired Architectura, a Vancouver-based architecture and interior design firm, in  
January 2003 \(Stantec n.d.\).  
9\. In 1916, two fields in the Haarlemmermeer polder near Amsterdam were purchased by  
the Dutch Minister of War, N. Bosboom, and developed into suitable landing strips for a  
military aerodrome. Facilitating Passenger Circulation 49  
10\. In 1936, 322,931 people visited Schiphol, and the airport garnered 68,595 Dutch Guilders  
from entry fees, which accounted for 27.9 percent of the airport’s annual income \(Dierikx  
and Bouwens 1997\). In the early days of air travel, most airports constructed stands or bal-  
conies with views of the airfield. These outdoor spaces are now rare due to security concerns.  
Schiphol, however, does allow visitors to access the terminal’s roof to view aircraft on the  
tarmac. See Adey \(2008\) for an analysis of the balcony at Liverpool Airport and its viewers’  
practices.  
11\. When the new terminal opened, the former terminal site began to be used for cargo \(Bosma

13\. Schiphol needs to have a pier that can service aircraft flying between Schengen and non-  
Schengen zones. For instance, an airplane arriving from Toronto and carrying on to Paris  
needs to have deplaning passengers pass through a non-Schengen zone and embarking pas-  
sengers pass through a Schengen zone. When the Schengen Area came into effect in 1995,  
a glass wall separated the two passenger streams in this pier. The following year the D-pier  
was expanded into two levels in order to separate the two zones.  
14\. Passengers flying to non-Schengen locations access the pier from the same post-security  
area that adjoins the D-, E-, F-, and G-piers. This area has two lounges and the Holland  
Boulevard. Passengers flying to Schengen locations pass through a security zone and a  
lounge that only leads to the M-gates.  
15\. See Benthem Crouwel Architects and Kirsten Schipper \(2010\) for an overview of the design  
of the bike shed and bus shelter.  
16\. On its maps and signage, Schiphol does not refer to these terminals as two separate struc-  
tures. Rather, these terminals are presented as one building, and it is ordered into three  
departure areas on the landside, four lounges on the airside, and four arrivals areas. Depar-  
tures 1 and 2, Lounges 1 and 2, and Arrivals 1 and 2 are in the 1967 terminal, and Depar-  
tures 3, Lounges 3 and 4, and Arrivals 3 and 4 are in Terminal-West.  
17\. The building replaced the railway station built in 1976 that stood on the opposite side of  
the access road.  
18\. Heathrow was not the city’s first civilian airport but the city’s third. It was preceded by  
Croydon and Gatwick. Croydon Airport, a military airfield situated nineteen kilometres  
southwest of the City of London, was repurposed as the city’s civilian airport and opened  
in 1920. The city’s second airport, Gatwick, is located forty-six kilometres south of the city  
centre and first opened in 1936. Its terminal, nicknamed the ‘beehive,’ was a unique circu-  
lar structure with six covered fingers leading to the aircraft. Operations ceased at Gatwick  
the following year because of flooding, and it was not reopened until 1958.  
19\. In the same year, the British government signed the Airports Act, which sought to privatize  
BAA \(Thomas-Emberson 2007\). In July the following year, BAA was listed on the London  
Stock Exchange and valued at 1255 million pounds sterling.  
20\. In November 2000, Norman Foster criticized this planning process at a meeting of the  
Associate Parliamentary Group on Architecture and Planning at the House of Commons,  
and he compared the quick completion of the new airport in Hong Kong with the long  
drawn out process for Terminal 5 \(Taylor 2000\). Foster + Partners had won the com-  
mission for HKIA three years after Richard Rogers Partnership won the commission for  
Terminal 5, and while HKIA was completed two years prior to this meeting, the plans for  
Terminal 5 were still waiting to be approved.  
21\. See Paul Finch \(1996\) and Edwards \(1997\) for descriptions and images of an earlier itera-  
tion of Terminal 5’s design.  
22\. Its footprint measures 400 metres by 176 metres. The curved ceiling’s high point is forty  
metres above ground and runs longitudinally across the rectangular building.  
23\. Stantec compiled a report, “BAA Wayfinding,” which overviews the firm’s designs for  
Heathrow Terminal 5. A copy is kept at the firm’s Vancouver office.  
24\. See Pamela Buxton’s \(2008\) description of how the resin-stone conglomerate tiles were

25\. HKIA was not the first airport to be built on reclaimed land in the sea. Kansai International  
Airport in Osaka Prefecture, Japan, designed by Renzo Piano and completed in 1994, was  
built on artificial island in Osaka Bay \(Buchanan 1994\). At Kansai and HKIA, designers  
were forced to build on reclaimed land because of the surrounding mountainous terrain  
and the existing flat spaces were already allocated for other developments \(Davey 1995\).  
HKIA was part of a larger Airport Core Program, costing twenty billion USD, which  
included a six-lane highway that would connect the airport to Hong Kong, two suspension  
bridges, and a high-speed train that stops in Hong Kong, Kowloon, and Tsing Yi.  
26\. With the addition of the North Satellite Concourse and the Midfield Concourse, HKIA  
has been able to handle more than double the number of passengers per year. In 2017, the  
airport processed 72.9 million passengers \(Hong Kong International Airport n.d.\).  
27\. The lessons learned at Stansted helped expedite the design process for HKIA’s Terminal 1,  
which was completed in a mere six years.  
28\. In conjunction with the engineering firm Ove Arup and Partners, Foster + Partners designed  
a thirty-six-metre-wide module for HKIA Terminal 1 \(Robbins 1998\). Each module has a  
ceiling constructed out of lightweight steel with a row of triangular skylights that runs the  
length of the longitudinal curve. Below the skylights is a gantry, which deflects and diffuses  
the light across the curved ceiling. The gantry is also equipped with artificial lighting and  
has a walkway for maintenance crews. This module is repeated across the entirety of the  
building’s space, creating a series of thirty-six-metre-wide barrel vaults.  
29\. Although these vaults were made from standardized parts, their height varies throughout  
the terminal. The central vault is the highest in the building, and the vaults extending to the  
north and south gradually diminish in height. This is most pronounced in the angled wings  
at the terminal’s west end where the vaults rise as low as four metres, much lower than the  
central vault that hovers twenty-two metres above the check-in counters \(Robbins 1998\).  
The concrete columns that support the vaults vary in height, and each has a steel node  
bolted to the top that allowed for the vaults to be installed at varying angles. The vaults  
also vary in height along their east-west axis. Passengers can see this variation in height  
when looking down the length of the long central vault from the food court on the airport’s  
landside.  
30\. See Max Hirsh’s \(2016\) analysis of the Airport Express line and how low-income travellers  
access the airport using cheaper transportation alternatives.  
31\. The uppermost level on the exterior of HKIA’s Terminal 1 is a roadway where automobiles,  
taxis, and buses can drop off passengers. The ramps connecting from this level run down-  
wards towards the check-in areas on the terminal’s departures level. Below this roadway,  
there is a corridor that runs the length of Terminal 1’s eastern exterior. From this corridor,  
there are four ramps running upwards towards Terminal 1’s check-in area. On the other  
side of this corridor, there are three bridges on the exterior of the terminal that connect to  
the top level of the high-speed rail station.  
32\. As discussed previously, the design for Heathrow Terminal 5 had to accord with existing  
infrastructure on the site. Like HKIA’s Terminal 1, Heathrow Terminal 5 has a symmetrical  
footprint, its outer shell has a symmetrical shape, and its structural nodes are distributed  
evenly throughout the space. But, unlike HKIA’s Terminal 1 with its symmetrical arrange-  
ment of bridges leading to the roadway and train station, the coloured bridges and mobil-  
ity-modules at Heathrow Terminal 5 have an uneven distribution in the space. Between  
each of the coloured mobility-modules, there are two of Rogers’s structural nodes—with  
the exception of the space between the blue mobility-module and the purple module, where  
there is only one node. The blue module, which is the access point for the Heathrow  
Express and London Underground, is a markedly different design from the other coloured  
mobility-modules leading to the parking garage. The red, orange, and green modules are on  
its south side, and the purple module is on its north side. Because the unique blue module  
is not situated at the building’s centre, Heathrow Terminal 5 does not reach the same level  
of symmetrical logic as Hong Kong’s Terminal 1.  
33\. See also David Jarach’s \(2001\) discussion of how airports are trying to maximize their  
revenue via non-aviation business ventures such as commercial, tourist, and conference  
services. Facilitating Passenger Circulation 51  
34\. Crary \(1999\) bases his argument on Guy Debord’s \(1994\) consideration of the spectacle  
in terms of how mass media influences the social conditions that separate and individuate  
subjects in twentieth-century capitalist societies. He notes that although Debord’s soci-  
ety of the spectacle appears markedly different from Michel Foucault’s \(1977\) society of  
discipline and surveillance, the two theories share some similarities. They both suppose  
that a subtle operation of power orders people into docile subjects. The spectacle, Crary  
notes, is less concerned with what is being shown and more with how visual media such as  
television manages the spectator’s attention and positions them as an isolated, separated,  
and controlled individual. Adey \(2007\) extrapolates on Crary’s consideration of how the  
spectacle is used to organize bodies in the terminal’s space.  
35\. When the World Duty Free Group opened its walkthrough shop at Stansted in 2014, it was  
the firm’s largest store in the UK \(Barras-Hill 2014\).  
36\. 37. This store was renovated in 2016 \(Ireland 2016\).  
It is situated between Terminals 2A and 2C.  
38\. The terminal’s gates are in three separate satellite structures. One is accessible via a cor-  
ridor, and the others are linked via a train.
