# Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
The previous chapter illustrated how air terminals organize passenger flows. They  
are directed to move from the building’s entrance to the gate, and they are exposed to  
numerous shops and restaurants along the way. In many terminals architects include  
material cues such as skylights and ceiling vaults that are intended to aid with navi-  
gation. This chapter considers how air terminal architecture and interior design are  
active agents that produce airport spaces. My line of inquiry is in the same vein as  
Bruno Latour’s and Albena Yaneva’s contributions to actor-network theory, a mode of  
investigation that attributes agency to things \(Latour 2000, 2005, 2009; Latour and  
Yaneva 2008; Yaneva 2005, 2009, 2013, 2016, 2017\). I explain key tenets in their  
writing before addressing how the air terminal’s material aspects influence travellers’  
practices. My investigation concentrates on how architects consider airport architec-  
ture in terms of its materiality and its capacity to mediate passengers’ actions in the  
terminal.  
I also argue that air terminal architecture and design constitute a functional aesthetic  
with regard to how their materiality organizes passenger flows. This aesthetic type is  
based upon one’s understanding of how these things support passenger navigation,  
wayfinding, and orientation. I expand upon Glenn Parsons and Allen Carlson’s \(2008\)  
theory of functional aesthetics; they posit that an object can be judged on whether it  
looks appropriate for its function, and they offer a critical assessment of how one can  
determine this type of appeal.1 My analysis does not present an aesthetic judgement;  
rather, it aims to provide a theoretical framework to comprehend an aesthetic aspect  
of effective designs. Moreover, I discuss how wayfinding designs need to look fit for  
their purpose since they usually function on a visual register. This builds upon my  
analysis in the last chapter where I illustrated how architects incorporate cues into the  
terminal’s interior that visually emphasize the routes that passengers need to follow.  
In addition, I consider how air terminal architects assess whether their designs func-  
tion as intended. Sometimes a design’s intended purpose differs from its use in practice.  
I examine how this issue is addressed by theories of proper function and Madeleine  
Akrich’s \(1992\) notion of scripts. These assessments of function assume that a thing’s  
operation in real life does not necessarily mirror the intended function as envisioned  
by the artefact’s designer. Instead, it emerges out of the thing’s relation with its users  
and context. I consider how these conceptions of function pertain to the air terminal  
design process, and I discuss how some airport architects study the building’s func-  
tions in ‘real-time’ in order to improve their own air terminal designs.  
This chapter illustrates how air terminal architects try to create easy-to-navigate  
interiors and how they believe that the terminal’s materiality can effectively facilitate 56 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
passenger flows. I argue that these types of design solutions counter Marc Augé’s  
\(1995\) and Christopher Schaberg’s \(2012\) assertions that the operation of air ter-  
minals is predominately defined by texts. Architects designing today’s international  
air terminals try to create spaces where travellers do not need to rely on signage for  
navigation. Furthermore, signage with text has limitations for wayfinding. I discuss a  
tragic incident at Vancouver’s YVR where a Polish national who did not speak Eng-  
lish died after misunderstanding the process for passing through the airport’s border  
screening zone. Robert Dziekański became agitated after spending hours confused  
in this secured area and was accidentally killed by the Royal Canadian Mounted  
Police \(RCMP\). I examine why Dziekański became lost and what changes the airport’s  
authority made to its terminal design to prevent a similar incident from happening  
again. Some of these improvements to YVR’s screening zone illustrate the functional  
aesthetic of intuitive wayfinding designs.  
Air Terminal Design and Agency  
In Chapter 1, I illustrated how architectural firms such as Foster + Partners and Rich-  
ard Rogers Partnership deploy things like skylights and ceiling vaults to encourage  
passengers to walk in particular directions. This type of design thinking resembles  
Latour’s \(2000, 2005\) attribution of agency to things.2 For Latour, a human, a non-  
human thing, or a group of things and humans are equally important with regard to  
social interactions. His critical writing is part of a larger project, actor-network theory,  
which contrasts with traditional sociology as it accepts entities that operate with-  
out intention or consciousness as actors within networks of social relations.3 Latour  
critiques the general tenet in sociology, introduced by Émile Durkheim, that human  
actions and relations are driven by social forces and “that objects do nothing, at least  
nothing comparable or even connectable to human social action, and that if they can  
sometimes ‘express’ power relations, ‘symbolize’ social hierarchies, ‘reinforce’ social  
inequalities, ‘transport’ social power, ‘objectify’ inequality, and ‘reify’ gender rela-  
tions, they cannot be at the origin of social activity” \(2005, 72\). Non-human things,  
he argues, play an active role in social environments, which consist of numerous  
unpredictable interactions between humans and things\:  
The main reason why objects had no chance to play any role before was not only  
due to the definition of the social used by sociologists, but also to the very defini-  
tion of actors and agencies most often chosen. If action is limited a priori to what  
‘intentional’, ‘meaningful’ humans do, it is hard to see how a hammer, a basket, a  
door closer, a cat, a rug, a mug, a list, or a tag could act. They might exist in the  
domain of ‘material’ ‘causal’ relations, but not in the ‘reflexive’ ‘symbolic’ domain  
of social relations. By contrast, if we stick to our decision to start from the con-  
troversies about actors and agencies, then any thing that does modify a state of  
affairs by making a difference is an actor.  
\(Latour 2005, 71; italics in original\)  
Even though Latour attributes agency to things, he does not suggest that objects  
determine social relations. Rather, things have the capacity to operate as mediators in  
a network of human and non-human actors. In “The Berlin Key or How to Do Words  
with Things,” Latour \(2000\) describes how a unique key with teeth on both ends Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 57  
facilitates social relations among apartment dwellers in Berlin. This key and its cor-  
responding lock require the occupant to secure their door at night but leave the door  
unlocked during the day. The building’s concierge uses a separate key to unlatch the  
lock during the daytime, and the lock is designed such that the occupant’s key cannot  
turn in the keyhole when the lock is unbolted. Latour writes\:  
If I take my key with two bits that authorises me to re-enter my house and obliges  
me to bolt the door at night and forbids me to bolt it during the day, am I not deal-  
ing with social relations, with morality, with laws? Of course, but made of steel.  
\(2000, 19\)  
The key, in this example, does not symbolize the required behaviour; as a material  
entity it acts to limit the occupant’s actions. Latour notes, however, that the key does  
not determine the social interactions in the Berlin apartment building since the key’s  
intended function can be overridden. If the occupant uses a file to make their key thin-  
ner, then it operates in a similar fashion as the concierge’s master key; the occupant  
gains control of the lock and can latch and unlatch the door during the day.  
In “Give Me a Gun and I Will Make All Buildings Move,” Latour and Albena  
Yaneva \(2008\) explain how actor-network theory can be used in analyses of architec-  
ture. The essay’s title refers to Étienne-Jules Marey’s photographic gun which captures  
a series of exposures in quick succession. Marey used this device to analyze how a  
gull flies. Latour and Yaneva note there is no imaging technology that accurately  
represents the multifarious movements of people within buildings. They propose that  
actor-network theory offers a method “to picture a building as a moving modulator  
regulating different intensities of engagement, redirecting users’ attention, mixing and  
putting people together, concentrating flows of actors and distributing them so as to  
compose a productive force in time-space” \(2008, 87; italics in original\).4 They argue  
that investigations of architecture should take a comprehensive view of how design  
mediates the action of the user. Indeed, this type of analysis differs from examina-  
tions of how a building conveys symbolic connotations or displays harmonious ratios  
among its parts.  
Latour and Yaneva, however, are not the first theorists to suggest that analyses of  
architecture should account for how users engage with the building. The philosopher  
that is perhaps most well-known in terms of a conception of space with respect to  
practices is Michel de Certeau. Space for de Certeau \(1984\) is defined by the practices  
enacted within it. He writes, “In short, space is a practiced place. Thus the street geo-  
metrically defined by urban planning is transformed into a space by walkers” \(1984,  
117; italics in original\).5 Architectural theorist Neil Leach \(2002\) examines how con-  
ceptions of practices might contribute to a more nuanced study of architecture and  
cultural identity. He draws from de Certeau’s conception of practices, Judith Butler’s  
notion of performativity, and Christian Metz’s writing on film theory to theorize how  
both architectural form and practices enacted in the space define a building’s meaning.6  
Like Leach, cultural geographers such as Loretta Lees have framed their investiga-  
tions into architecture around the practices performed within buildings. For instance,  
in her investigation of Moshe Safdie’s Vancouver Public Library, Lees \(2001\) exam-  
ines and compares the civic debates regarding the building’s planning processes with  
ethnographic accounts of people using the library’s spaces during an eighteen-month  
period following its opening in 1995. Although she notes that social, economic, and 58 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
political conditions need to be addressed in an architectural analysis, she argues that  
buildings are not merely defined by the productive forces or the symbolic references  
connoted by a structure’s design but also by spatial practices enacted within it. Her  
intention was to offer an analysis that shifted away from the predominant readings  
of the building, which discussed the library’s façade and whether it was appropriate  
for Vancouver.7 In Lees’s article, she recounts some of the practices she witnessed\: for  
example, people perusing books and reading, the elderly using it as a meeting place,  
homeless people using the bathrooms to wash up, and children playing on escalators.8  
Yaneva \(2013, 2017\) also uses an ethnographic method in her studies of architec-  
ture in terms of actor-network theory. However, she concentrates on how a building’s  
materiality mediates the users’ actions. In her \(2017\) investigation of FCBStudios’  
Benzie building at the Manchester School of Arts, she observes her and others’ engage-  
ment with the building’s physical components. The atrium’s open plan, for instance,  
facilitates interactions between the school’s faculty and students. Her account also  
closely examines how the building’s staircases, elevators, doors, and locks mediate the  
user’s movement through the interior. She argues that this type of analysis does not  
merely lend “an understanding of architectural objects as static surfaces where mean-  
ing can be projected” \(2013, 122\). Instead, it offers “a more dynamic understanding  
of the processes that make buildings possible not by asking what a building means,  
but what it does and how it works” \(2013, 122; italics in original\).  
My analysis in this chapter considers air terminal architecture and design in a simi-  
lar manner; it examines how these material things mediate the passengers’ practices  
within the airport. I do not assess the terminal’s architecture as an expression of the  
social relations within the airport and the air transportation industry. Instead, I con-  
sider how it actively produces airport spaces. I investigate how the physical materiality  
of airport architecture enables passenger movement within the terminal, and I concen-  
trate on how architects deploy material cues to aid with passenger navigation. Moreo-  
ver, I examine how architects design air terminal interiors such that their materiality  
actively mediates passenger behaviour. I also discuss how some architects observe  
travellers’ practices in order to improve their terminal designs.  
Without a doubt, airport architects accept that the building’s material design has the  
capacity to facilitate passenger flows. Their designs include things like corridor walls  
and escalators that physically require a traveller to move in a particular direction.  
However, they also include materials that operate on a sensory register, visual and  
even auditory, to draw passengers to move through the terminal. At Schiphol Plaza  
\(Figure 1.6; see Plate 6\), Jan Benthem introduced rows of columns and beams to visu-  
ally emphasize predominant pathways and has designed a grid for the flooring that  
aligns with the major routes that travellers are intended to follow in the space \(Cress-  
well 2006\). He designed Schiphol Plaza with dark-tinted windows as a strategy to  
entice people to walk to the more brightly lit terminals since passengers were expected  
to be drawn towards light \(Kloos and de Maar 1999\). At Beijing’s Terminal 3, Foster +  
Partners designed the ceiling with thin slats that correspond with the main directional  
flow \(Lin-Liu 2008\). They hang below the ceiling and are spaced apart such that they  
do not obscure the light shining through the skylights. Foster + Partners’ Mouzhan  
Majidi notes that\: “the roof becomes a tool to guide the passengers to the gate, wel-  
coming, directing, and orientating them” \(cited in Lin-Liu 2008, 113, 119\). Similarly,  
Heathrow Terminal 5’s ceiling has long and narrow skylights that emphasize the route  
one needs to follow to pass through security and access the gates \(Figure I.2\). Safdie’s Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 59  
Terminal 1 at Toronto Pearson also includes skylights \(Figure 2.1\) that are intended to  
indicate the main passenger routes \(Kuitenbrouwer 2006; Mississauga News 2006\).  
When asked about his design for a pier that opened in 2006, Safdie noted\: “Airports  
are intimidating places and most people associate airports with stress. . . . We’ve tried  
to design this so that it is self-oriented. . . . We’ve used daylight as a major placefinding  
mechanism” \(Mississauga News 2006, 1\). At Paris Charles de Gaulle’s Terminal 2F,  
Paul Andreu placed translucent ceilings over its two piers in order to draw the travel-  
ler’s eyes upwards and to stimulate a sense of flight \(Jodidio and Andreu 2004\). The  
piers’ ceiling design also serves a purpose for wayfinding. When looking towards the  
gates, the ceiling’s framework curves inwards and connects to a long ridgepole, which  
entices one to move forwards \(Figure 2.2\). In its renovation of Los Angeles’s Terminal 6  
building, Westfield incorporated ribbons of LED lights in the ceiling \(Figure I.1\) to  
direct passengers along the main route \(Herman 2016; Westfield Airports 2016\). In  
some areas of the terminal, these winding ribbons are replicated in the terrazzo floor-  
ing as darker bands.  
These material design strategies are used to help passengers navigate themselves  
through the terminal. Unlike airport signage, which requires the traveller’s full atten-  
tion to what is written and signified through pictograms, these architectural features  
are more or less Heideggerian ready-to-hand entities that are generally taken for  
Figure 2.1 Moshe Safdie, Skidmore Owings & Merrill, and Adamson Associates, Terminal 1,  
Toronto Pearson International Airport, Canada, 2004.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). 60 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
Figure 2.2 Paul Andreu, Terminal 2F, Paris Charles de Gaulle Airport, France, 1999.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
granted and are active agents operating in the background of one’s attention.9 These  
material designs are not only helpful for wayfinding but are also conducive to spend-  
ing. An easy-to-navigate interior design lends to a place where passengers are less  
stressed and more willing to shop. In addition, these designs require less directional Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 61  
signage, and this allows the traveller’s attention to drift towards the numerous shop-  
fronts and restaurants within the terminal.  
The Architectural Organization of Movement as a  
Functional Aesthetic  
The aforementioned examples illustrate how air terminal architects believe that  
their designs play an active role in organizing passenger movement. Their aim as  
designers is to create a material environment that is effective for wayfinding. I argue  
that these design strategies constitute a functional aesthetic. This type of appeal  
is defined by what these material designs do. For instance, I argue that Heathrow  
Terminal 5’s skylights are aesthetically appealing since they encourage travellers to  
move along the required directional flow \(Figure I.2\). My aim is to illustrate that  
an architectural design’s capacity to actively mediate passenger flows constitutes an  
aesthetic quality. My analysis, however, is not an estimation of these designs’ aes-  
thetic value; rather, it is an investigation of an aesthetic type determined by the air  
terminal’s function as a transit hub that organizes passenger movement. I am also  
establishing a theoretical framework for understanding how an air terminal design  
can be effective.  
I build upon Parsons and Carlson’s \(2008\) critical assessment of how an artefact’s  
aesthetics can be determined by the knowledge of its utility.10 According to their theory  
the cheetah’s stunning spotted coat, which helps camouflage the animal, is not con-  
sidered appealing simply because of its superficial appearance. But, the knowledge  
that the cheetah’s small head allows for an aerodynamic advantage and how its head  
“looks fit” in terms of this known function determines an aspect of this animal’s func-  
tional aesthetics. Their notion contrasts with Stephen Davies’s \(2007\) conception of  
functional aesthetics, which considers how a thing’s aesthetic properties contribute to  
and enhance the thing’s function. Even though Parsons and Carlson refer to architec-  
ture, they offer only a limited consideration of how their theory applies to buildings.  
Nevertheless, they do explain that their investigation follows a lineage of examina-  
tions into aesthetics and function with respect to architecture such as Louis Sullivan’s  
\(1896\) “The Tall Office Building Artistically Considered.” While Parsons and Carl-  
son’s analysis of architecture is not applicable to my study of air terminal design, their  
attention to the functional aesthetic of larger arrangements like ecosystems is helpful  
in terms of assessing the aesthetics of the air terminal as a mobility-system. They argue  
that their conception of functional aesthetics can apply to a complex network of enti-  
ties such as an estuary, where each entity contributes to its overall function as a water  
filter. Similarly, I consider the aesthetics of material designs such as skylights with  
regard to their contribution to wayfinding and the organization of passenger flows  
within the air terminal.  
Unlike Parsons and Carlson, I concentrate on architecture as a system of parts that  
support a purpose. I contend that I offer a more thorough investigation of how their  
notion of functional aesthetics can be applied to architecture. Furthermore, I argue  
that my analysis of wayfinding design illustrates an example of why ‘looking fit for its  
purpose’ matters in relation to a design’s function. Moreover, these types of designs  
often operate on a visual register, and they need to look fit for their purpose in order  
to work effectively. Before I address wayfinding designs, I will illustrate how Parsons  
and Carlson’s notion of functional aesthetics can apply to architecture.  
The structural nodes supporting Heathrow Terminal 5’s ceiling are a striking example  
of how architecture can be considered aesthetically with respect to its utility \(Figure 2.3\). 62 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
For Parsons and Carlson, an object’s functional aesthetic is based on whether it  
looks fit in terms of one’s knowledge of its function. In this case, I know that the  
structural node’s function is to support the terminal’s high ceiling, and I can argue  
that it looks fit for this purpose. What I would like to emphasize here is that for  
Figure 2.3 Richard Rogers Partnership, Terminal 5, London Heathrow Airport, United King-  
dom, 2008.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\). Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 63  
Parsons and Carlson the aesthetic of utility is determined by function itself. So,  
if I use their aesthetic theory to consider this structural node, I cannot make the  
following statement\: this node considered with regard to its form alone is an aes-  
thetically appealing object, and it also happens to support the ceiling. Its aesthetic  
depends on my knowledge of its function, which is to support the ceiling. In this  
case, I can state that the structural node, which consists of a hinge that connects  
three pairs of beams attached to the floor and ceiling, looks fit in terms of its role in  
supporting the ceiling.  
Each one of these structural nodes is painted entirely in white with the exception  
of two rings painted grey that are fixed to both sides of the pin running horizontally  
through the node. This slight colour differentiation draws one’s attention to how this  
pin is a pivot point for a hinge connecting three pairs of beams and how it is a key  
element in supporting the roof. Yet, whether these rings are painted grey or white is  
inconsequential in terms of my assessment of their functional aesthetic. This applica-  
tion of paint may draw my attention to the pin and the structural node’s function, but  
it does not contribute to the node’s function and therefore does not impact my assess-  
ment of whether this node looks fit with regard to its function.  
Although my analysis of the structural node at Heathrow Terminal 5 illustrates the  
logic of Parsons and Carlson’s notion of functional aesthetics, they do not include  
a similar type of analysis in their explanation of how their theory applies to archi-  
tecture. Instead, their attention to function focuses on how a building is used by its  
inhabitants. They rightfully note that in many cases a building’s appearance is not nec-  
essarily an indication of how it is used. They argue that earlier analyses that focused  
on “structural honesty” offer only a confused account of how a building’s appearance  
might be considered aesthetically in terms of its function. Parsons and Carlson also  
contend that buildings that point to their function via their overall expression—such  
as Eero Saarinen’s TWA terminal—are not examples of a functional aesthetic since this  
aspect of their form has little to do with their actual function.11  
My analysis of the structural node at Heathrow Terminal 5 illustrates how a con-  
ception of functional aesthetics can be applied to a broader consideration of architec-  
tural function—namely, how individual structural components support the building.  
This attention to the functional aesthetic of the air terminal’s individual components  
with respect to the building’s function resembles Bruno Taut’s \(1929\) explanation of  
efficient design and aesthetics. Taut contends that every architectural detail included  
in a design must serve the overall plan and that their aesthetic derives from their  
ability to fulfil this function. This aesthetic and design principle, which mirrors the  
operation of a collective and cooperative society, is championed by Taut as a means to  
improve social behaviour.12 Although Taut’s assertion that this aspect of architectural  
design directly affects social relations is tenuous, his attention to the overall integra-  
tion of a building’s components offers a concrete example of functional aesthetics and  
architecture.  
My analysis of air terminal wayfinding design also considers the function of a build-  
ing’s individual components—specifically, how they support the building’s primary  
use, which is to move, process, and regulate passengers. Corridors and escalators are  
obvious examples of interior design that function to direct them through the space.  
As I have described previously, architects often deploy more subtle strategies such as  
visual cues to guide passengers. These are examples of design that need to look fit  
for their purpose since they function on a visual level. The thin ceiling slats at Bei-  
jing Terminal 3 look fit for their wayfinding function since they run parallel to the  
direction that travellers need to follow to get to their gate \(Lin-Liu 2008\). Similarly, 64 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
the skylights at Heathrow Terminal 5 visually emphasize the direction towards pre-  
boarding security and the gates \(Figure I.2\).  
The design of HKIA Terminal 1, as noted in the previous chapter, has a near perfect  
symmetrical arrangement of the interior architecture that facilitates the movements of  
passengers, such as the escalators, the security checkpoints, and the bridges connecting  
from the train and the road to the check-in areas. Although this symmetrical design  
can be considered aesthetically comparable to the Classical design ethos as seen in  
Greek temples, it is not a strong example of the type of aesthetic that I have described  
earlier. I consider the architecture’s aesthetic in relation to how it helps manage pas-  
sengers’ movements. Although an awareness of this symmetrical arrangement might  
help with one’s navigation of the terminal space, it is a quality that likely only few of  
the passengers notice. For one to be aware of this symmetrical design, one needs to  
look at the architectural drawings or walk through the space with a careful attention  
as to how these things are deployed in the building. One aspect of HKIA Terminal 1’s  
design that is a strong example of the type of functional aesthetic that I am proposing  
is the linear ceiling vaults that are aligned with the overall direction which passengers  
need to follow to get from the entrance to their gate \(Figure 1.11\). Like the ceiling  
slats at Beijing Terminal 3 and the skylights at Heathrow Terminal 5, the long linear  
vaults draw the traveller’s eye in the direction they need to move. As these vaults are  
all aligned along the same east-west axis, they also help with giving a sense as to which  
direction one is facing in the space. Indeed, the vaults look fit for their purpose as a  
material design that encourages passengers to follow the terminal’s directional flow.  
Heathrow Terminal 5’s colour scheme is a corresponding example of a functional  
aesthetic that operates visually to serve a wayfinding purpose \(see Plate 8\). The red,  
orange, green, purple, and blue ‘mobility-modules’ at Heathrow Terminal 5 help ori-  
ent travellers and visitors within the building’s vast landside space \(Royal Academy  
of Arts 2013\). Although Richard Rogers Partnership designed Terminal 5 with some  
colour, it is a rather limited application compared to Terminal 4 at Madrid-Barajas  
where the firm used a generous amount of colour to aid with passenger navigation \(see  
Plate 11\). The columns and curved beams supporting the wave-like roof are painted in  
a rainbow of colours that gradually shift in hue as one moves along the length of the  
concourse. On the airside facing the apron, there is an unobstructed view of the con-  
course, and one can see the gradation in colour. The terminal’s gates are demarcated  
by H, J, and K, and they correspond with the supports’ hues.13 The signage for the  
H-gates is coloured red, the J-gates yellow, and the K-gates blue. The gate numbers are  
not duplicated across the three different colours. The gates are distributed as follows\:  
H1 to H37, J40 to J59, and K62 to K98.  
The structural supports change from yellow to green to blue as one walks north-  
ward from the J-gates to the K-gates. The struts and beams change from yellow to  
orange to red as one walks southward from the J-gates to the H-gates. The signs for  
the gates do not gradually change colour. For instance, the same yellow hue is used  
for gates J40 through J59. The experience of seeing the terminal’s supports slowly  
change colour while walking through the concourse surely consists of an aesthetic  
and kinaesthetic experience. However, my assessment of this wayfinding strategy as  
a functional aesthetic focuses on a cognitive understanding of how this architectural  
design operates visually to facilitate passenger circulation.  
The structural supports in Madrid-Barajas Terminal 4’s landside area are painted  
in a uniform yellow hue \(see Plate 7\). It encompasses less space lengthwise than the Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 65  
airside concourse. This landside area is adjacent to the J-gates, which are also indi-  
cated by the colour yellow. In this respect, the use of colour for orientation differs  
from Heathrow Terminal 5, which has differently coloured columns and beams on  
the landside. As noted in the previous chapter, the two meeting points at Heathrow  
Terminal 5 are identified by the colours of the adjacent escalator and elevator support  
structures \(see Plate 8\). Madrid-Barajas Terminal 4 only has one meeting point, and  
its signage employs the same yellow colour as the struts and beams supporting the  
ceiling.  
Defining a Design’s Actual Function  
The aforementioned examples illustrate how architects assume that the material aspects  
of their design will facilitate passenger traffic. But, how do we know whether an archi-  
tectural design for wayfinding performs as intended? This is indeed a difficult question  
to answer. Some architects like Kho Liang Ie observe how travellers engage with an air  
terminal’s interior, and they use this information to improve their own designs. Plan-  
ners also rely on passenger feedback to assess whether their changes to improve way-  
finding are actually effective. Every two months, Schiphol conducts surveys consisting  
of questions that address a number of aspects regarding the respondent’s experience  
of the airport \(Hans Bouchier, pers. comm., April 11, 2018\). Schiphol’s planners reuse  
the same questionnaire as this allows them to ascertain whether a recent change to the  
design was effective.  
These types of assessments of a design’s actual operation pertain to my analysis  
of air terminal aesthetics in relation to its function. As noted, I draw upon Parsons  
and Carlson’s \(2008\) conception of functional aesthetics which is predicated on an  
understanding of the object in question’s actual function. They posit that a thing’s  
functional aesthetic derives from its proper function, which is determined by the thing  
itself and not by a functional role that its designer intended it to have.14 Parsons and  
Carlson argue that most things have a multitude of functions; some are proper func-  
tions, which “belong” to the things themselves, and others are accidental functions  
that occur by chance. The “selected effects” theory of function distinguishes a thing’s  
proper function from its accidental functions. This theory, which follows from biolo-  
gists’ investigations into the reproduction of selected traits in organisms, supposes  
that a thing’s proper function is one that has allowed it to survive in nature or within  
the marketplace. For instance, a lung’s proper function is to circulate air, not to make  
breathing sounds. A wire coat hanger’s proper function—the main reason why it has  
survived in the marketplace—is its ability to hang clothes, not its other accidental  
functions such as opening locked car doors.  
What about the inorganic things in nature that function to sustain ecosystems?  
Unlike living beings, these things do not reproduce and do not have selected traits that  
have contributed to their survival. Inorganic things that sustain an ecosystem—such as  
a rock that happens to flood a plain—have causal role functions \(Parsons and Carlson  
2008\). This type of function happens to support a larger system by accident and only  
exists as a function because of this chance contribution. Even though a thing’s causal  
role function is a weaker notion of function than a thing’s ‘selected’ proper functions,  
Parsons and Carlson contend that their theory still applies to these types of inorganic  
things. In their assessment of manufactured artefacts, buildings, and artworks, they 66 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
argue that their functional aesthetic is determined by proper functions rather than  
causal role functions.  
With respect to air terminals, most architectural features that facilitate and organ-  
ize passenger movement have a proper function with respect to the airport’s role as a  
transit hub. In many cases, travellers have no choice in selecting these designs, and it  
is therefore easy to determine these things’ proper functions. For instance, corridors,  
aerobridges, hallways, and stairwells funnel passengers in particular directions; their  
proper function is to organize travellers into directional flows. However, sometimes  
passengers are offered a choice with regard to how they move through these spaces.  
They can elect to use escalators and people movers to expedite their movement \(see  
Plates 5 and 8\).  
Things such as stairwells and escalators tend to function as intended. However,  
not all material designs that planners deploy to direct passenger movement operate  
as envisioned. Airport artworks, which I examine in detail in the following chap-  
ters, are often installed as meeting points and landmarks in the terminal to aid with  
passenger wayfinding. But not all artworks are effective in serving this purpose.  
Shinkichi Tajiri’s Knot \(Figure 2.4\), a large white knot wrapped around a pillar  
in Schiphol’s arrivals level, was designated as a meeting point shortly after it was  
installed in 1974 \(van Hooff 1998\). Even though Knot was the airport’s official  
meeting point, many travellers and visitors chose Kees Franse’s Apple as a place to  
meet instead \(see Plate 12\). It is a sculpture made out of wood slices in the form of  
its title and was installed in 1975. Although it was not intended to function as a  
meeting point, it gradually did acquire this role. Despite the fact that Apple gained  
this role by accident, it is not a causal role function like that of a rock that happens  
to irrigate a flood plain. Passengers chose to use Apple as their designated meeting  
point, and therefore it has gained this as a new proper function. This type of proper  
function is akin to that of the pipe cleaner, which is a thing that has acquired a new  
and more popular proper function that is not the same as the original intended  
function \(Parsons and Carlson 2008\). Pipe cleaners, which are designed to clean  
pipes and are still used to clean pipes, are more often sold as a material to be  
used in children’s craftworks. Like pipe cleaners, Apple’s original intended function  
was not neutralized by its newly acquired proper function. When Apple was first  
installed, it, like other artworks in the terminal, was intended to make people smile,  
and it undoubtedly retained this capacity to elicit this feeling after it started to be  
used as a meeting point \(Berkers and Burgers 2013\). Not only did this artwork’s  
proper function change over time, but its exterior appearance also changed due  
to its effectiveness in capturing the viewers’ attention and actively engaging with  
them. The sculpture has been slowly transformed by countless people marking their  
names on the wooden apple using coloured chalk and inks.  
It is worth noting that this notion of proper function parallels a conception of ontol-  
ogy similar to Latour’s \(2000, 2005, 2009\) philosophical considerations of material  
things as active agents. Moreover, a manufactured artefact’s or building’s proper func-  
tion emerges out of a relation between it and a network of human actors. Like Parsons  
and Carlson, Latour posits that a manufactured object or a building does not neces-  
sarily operate as its designer intended. In his explanations of actor-network theory,  
he draws upon Akrich’s \(1992\) notion of scripts to theorize how a thing’s intended  
function does not always equate with its actual use. Akrich suggests that designed  
artefacts are imbued with a script that defines how the product is expected to perform  
in the real world. Designers establish this script based upon how they expect the user  
to engage with the artefact\: Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 67  
Figure 2.4 Shinkichi Tajiri, Knot, Amsterdam Airport Schiphol, Netherlands, 1974.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\).  
For some time sociologists of technology have argued that when technologists define  
the characteristics of their objects, they necessarily make hypotheses about the entities  
that make up the world into which the object is to be inserted. Designers thus define  
actors with specific tastes, competences, motives, aspirations, political prejudices, and 68 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
the rest, and they assume that morality, technology, science, and economy will evolve  
in particular ways. A large part of the work of innovators is that of ‘inscribing’ this  
vision of \(or prediction about\) the world in the technical content of the new object.  
I will call the end product of this work a ‘script’ or a ‘scenario.’  
\(Akrich 1992, 207–8; italics in original\)  
This script, however, is open to interpretation with regard to the artefact’s users\: “it  
may be that no actors will come forward to play the roles envisaged by the designer.  
Or users may define quite different roles of their own” \(Akrich 1992, 208\). Returning  
to Latour’s \(2000\) analysis of the Berlin key, the object is inscribed with a script to  
prescribe the apartment dwellers’ social relations based on how it operates the lock;  
however, one can override this script by altering the key’s shape.  
Akrich’s \(1992\) conception of an artefact’s script resembles Parsons and Carlson’s  
\(2008\) notion of proper functions since both theories accept that the material artefact  
itself actively mediates its use among its users. However, these theorists differ in terms  
of how they evaluate an artefact’s actual use. Akrich’s conception of the script concen-  
trates on the designer’s intended aim for the artefact’s use, and she offers a framework  
to assess the degree to which the script affects the artefact’s actual use in a number of  
different social contexts. Parsons and Carlson’s conception of proper function, on the  
other hand, seeks to determine one specific actual use—the function that has led to the  
artefact’s repeated selection by the majority of its users.  
Although Parsons and Carlson look to an object’s proper function to assess its func-  
tional aesthetics, I argue that an assessment of air terminal artworks, architecture, and  
design should not discount the airport authorities’, artists’, architects’, and design-  
ers’ intentions—or scripts—for these aesthetic things. Although I do not contend that  
an artefact’s intended function determines its actual function, a consideration of the  
thing’s script may provide some indication of the purpose the item under consideration  
serves. In some cases, the air terminal architects’ and planners’ script for a particular  
building element may draw from existing information about a related element’s actual  
function in other air terminals. At Schiphol, for instance, Marius Duintjer mimicked  
Chicago O’Hare’s overall plan in his design for the new terminal, which opened in  
1967 \(Bosma 2013\). This layout, where the upper departures level is accessed by an  
elevated road and the arrivals area is one storey below, effectively separates the incom-  
ing and outgoing passenger flows. This design has proven to be popular among airport  
architects—most contemporary air terminals organize their departures and arrivals  
areas in a similar fashion. GMR Group, who won the commission to design Delhi  
Indira Gandhi International Airport’s Terminal 3 in 2006, visited a number of the  
world’s busiest airports while they were developing the building \(Vaish 2010; Delhi  
Indira Gandhi International Airport n.d.\). Shortly before Terminal 3 opened in 2010,  
GMR Group’s CEO of Airport Development, Prabhakara Rao, noted that “we had the  
chance of learning from others’ strengths and weaknesses” \(cited in Vaish 2010\). The  
firm visited Heathrow, Frankfurt, Madrid-Barajas, Dubai, Bangkok Suvarnabhumi,  
Singapore Changi, Kuala Lumpur, Tokyo Narita, Hong Kong, and Seoul Incheon dur-  
ing the design process. The latter two were particularly helpful with regard to how  
their designs allowed for a smooth and efficient processing of passenger flows.  
Beth Preston \(2000\) accounts for how an existing product’s actual use influences  
similar subsequent designs in her analysis of proper function. She notes that an artefact  
acquires its proper function through its “lineage” \(2000, 31\).15 Moreover, an artefact’s Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 69  
proper function, a property that ensured its survival, has been replicated in anteced-  
ent artefacts categorically similar to the artefact in question. Kho’s design process for  
Schiphol illustrates an example of lineage with regard to air terminal design. When  
the firm was commissioned to design the terminal’s interior in 1962, they looked to  
other major international airports in Europe and North America for design solutions  
\(Bosma 2013; Berkers and Burgers 2013\). Kho travelled to the airports to see how  
their interior architecture functioned. He watched how passengers interacted with  
the material elements in the terminal, and he used this information to influence his  
design for Schiphol. In some cases, Kho replicated interior design at other airports,  
and in other cases he developed innovative solutions based on what he observed at  
these terminals. At Orly Airport in Paris, he noticed that many travellers were almost  
slipping and falling on the marble flooring as they were rushing through the terminal,  
so he chose a type of tiling that offered more texture and grip. Kho also tried to maxi-  
mize the passengers’ traction by incorporating studded rubber flooring in some of the  
high traffic areas. This design strategy was influential as many other airport planners  
looked to Schiphol’s interior design and began to use this type of flooring as well.  
While this chapter has focused on strategies to direct passenger circulation, these types  
of measures are another type of functional aesthetic as they improve the efficiency and  
safety of passengers’ movements.  
Kho’s design process is also notable with regard to actor-network theory. It resem-  
bles the type of research methodology that Latour and Yaneva \(2008\) propose for  
studying architecture. As noted, Yaneva \(2013, 2017\) conducts ethnographic analyses  
of how people engage with a building’s material aspects. Kho also closely examined  
how architecture mediates passengers’ actions, and he relied on this research to create  
an effective design for Schiphol’s interior.  
Prescribing Movement and Anticipating Consumption in the  
Walkthrough Duty-Free Store  
The aforementioned examples include things like skylights and floor patterns which  
try to coax passengers to move in particular directions. Usually, architects design these  
things in order to provide travellers with clear cues as to how to move efficiently  
towards areas such as pre-boarding security or their gates. The last chapter exam-  
ined walkthrough duty-free store designs that send passengers along serpentine routes  
such that they are exposed to a greater number of goods for sale. According to the  
aviation consulting firm InterVISTAS, this strategy appears to work. In a 2014 report  
on air terminal revenue, Kenneth Currie and Luciano Nunes state\: “Duty-free Shops  
configured in a serpentine walk-through shop achieve sales as much as 60% higher  
than other configurations because 100% of customers are exposed” \(2014, 4\). With  
this in mind, we can establish that increasing passenger spending is an actual func-  
tion for these routes running through duty-free shops in the terminal and that these  
designs constitute a functional aesthetic with respect to passenger circulation and  
consumption.  
The functional aesthetic outlined in this chapter is based on a cognitive understand-  
ing of the design’s function. In this case, it is determined by knowing that this curvi-  
linear route leads to higher sales. This aesthetic, however, is much different from the  
experiential aesthetic of moving through these walkthrough designs. When entering  
these stores for the first time, there is an element of surprise when rounding a corner 70 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
and seeing an array of consumer goods. While this may be pleasurable for passengers  
with ample time before their departure, it undoubtedly causes anxiety for those who  
need to rush to their gate.16  
Indeed, this curvilinear route can elicit a sense of terror in some passengers. This  
response mirrors the type of sensation that late-eighteenth-century tourists sought  
when they walked along serpentine paths in picturesque English gardens such as  
Thomas Johnes’s grounds at Hafod; these gardens were meant to trigger a sense of  
terror, albeit one that was ultimately not an actual negative feeling \(Andrews 1989\).  
Moreover, tourists visited these manufactured spaces for a sublime experience.17 These  
types of picturesque gardens contrast with planned landscapes such as Versailles which  
include vistas across rectilinear pathways and allow for a rational understanding of  
the space. This is notable with regard to Norman Foster’s innovative design for Lon-  
don’s Stansted; its ceiling consists of a Cartesian grid of modules that allows for views  
in all directions, and it serves a wayfinding purpose since passengers can use the ceil-  
ing to orient themselves within the interior \(Figure 1.1\). But, the walkthrough duty-  
free shop and serpentine shopping street, which were installed thirteen years after the  
terminal opened in 1991, block these vistas and obfuscate one’s understanding of the  
terminal’s layout \(Figure 1.12; see Plate 10\).  
Texts and Their Limitations for Wayfinding  
The material designs discussed in this chapter actively enable passenger movement,  
and they do so by operating on a non-textual level. This runs counter to Augé’s \(1995\)  
and Schaberg’s \(2012\) analyses of air terminals as spaces that are predominately  
defined by texts. For Augé, the airport is a quintessential “non-place,” and he argues  
that\: “The link between individuals and their surroundings in the space of non-place  
is established through the mediation of words, or even texts” \(1995, 94\).18 In The Tex-  
tual Life of Airports, Schaberg \(2012\) follows Augé’s line of inquiry and offers a more  
detailed analysis of the airport’s operations and textuality. Schaberg writes\:  
I argue that airports depend on textuality to a great degree, as much for their  
straightforward operations \(such as the daily performances and narratives that play  
out all the way from the check-in stand to the departure gate\), as for their everyday  
mysteries and inoperative moments \(for instance, how a thousand unique stories can  
be contained in and canceled out by phrases like ‘weather delay’ and ‘lost baggage’\).  
\(2012, 2\)19  
I challenge Augé’s and Schaberg’s arguments that texts define the airport’s environ-  
ment. In today’s contemporary international airports, air terminal architects try to  
minimize the use of signage, and they focus on how the building’s material design can  
be shaped such that it is an easy-to-navigate space. Although I am arguing that the  
terminal’s materiality plays a larger role in the airport’s operations than what Augé  
and Schaberg suggest in their respective analyses, I am not suggesting that texts do  
not have an integral function at the air terminal. Even though texts are not the only  
medium used for wayfinding, the careful deployment of signage often helps passengers  
find their way.  
However, signage can be confusing for some travellers, particularly if they are una-  
ble to read the language depicted. In these cases, passengers will usually find their Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 71  
way by either following other travellers or seeking assistance from airport personnel.  
However, passengers occasionally do get lost, and this can lead to tragic outcomes.  
In October 2007, Robert Dziekański, a Polish national, died as a result of misunder-  
standing the process involved to pass through the border zone at Vancouver’s Interna-  
tional Terminal. After spending over six hours in this space, he became agitated and  
his confused mental state ultimately led to his death. Dziekański’s miscomprehension  
of the process likely stemmed from his inability to speak or read English, the main lan-  
guage spoken in Vancouver. I examine the steps taken by the Vancouver International  
Airport Authority \(VIAA\) in response to this event, and I discuss how the authority  
recognized that signage and texts are less effective for wayfinding than clear view-  
points of desired destinations in the terminal.  
Before I address the implications of the Dziekański incident, I will give an account  
of his, his mother’s, and the airport and security staff’s actions on October 13 and 14,  
2007\. This overview helps illustrate the rationale behind the changes at Vancouver’s  
YVR in response to this tragedy. On October 13, 2007, Dziekański flew from Poland  
to Vancouver \(CBC News 2007a, 2007b, 2007c, 2007d, 2008, 2009\). This was his  
first trip out of Poland as well as his first time flying. Dziekański, who only spoke  
Polish, had to pass through airports in Frankfurt and Vancouver and was anxious  
about the process. His mother, Sofia Cisowski, instructed him to meet her at the bag-  
gage carousels. Yet, meeters and greeters are not permitted to access this space in the  
International Terminal’s airside arrivals area. After Dziekański landed, he waited in  
the secured baggage claim area for over six hours before having his visa processed by  
the Canada Border Services Agency \(CBSA\) and exiting this secured zone. Meanwhile,  
his mother, who was waiting in the landside area, made numerous attempts to find out  
whether he had arrived and if they could page him. Even though she explained that he  
was likely by the carousels, she was reassured by airport staff that he would be attended  
to.20 After numerous attempts to find her son, Cisowski was advised to return to her  
home in Kamloops, situated 350 kilometres east of Vancouver. At approximately the  
same time, Dziekański was processed at the exit of the CBSA baggage claim area.  
From there, he was directed to a separate office where CBSA agents approved his visa  
application and allowed him to leave this secured zone. However, about thirty minutes  
later, an officer noticed that Dziekański was sitting in a chair within this space, and he  
was escorted to the final CBSA checkpoint. When Dziekański exited to the landside  
area of the arrivals hall, he became agitated and tried to return back to this secured  
space. Police were called to deal with Dziekański who at one point started throwing  
furniture. Four RCMP officers arrived on the scene shortly afterwards, and they fired  
a Taser at Dziekański several times to subdue him. Dziekański died moments later.21  
The VIAA tried to improve the terminal’s interior design following Dziekański’s  
death. The carpet design and the lighting were changed in an attempt to clarify the  
route from the CBSA zone to the landside arrivals area \(Braidwood 2010\). These  
renovations follow the logic of the functional aesthetic I have outlined in this chapter.  
But they only have a limited effect in terms of wayfinding since there is no direct view  
of the meeters and greeters on the landside. Like most international airports, the bag-  
gage claim and subsequent border screening area at YVR are situated in a contained  
secured space without any windows.  
The VIAA’s more substantial upgrades to this space included increased personnel  
and access to translation services. A new information kiosk was installed next to  
where Dziekański died. This twenty-four-hour information desk is situated beside the 72 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
exit leading from the airside of the arrivals area to the landside of the arrivals hall. It  
can be accessed by passengers before they exit the secured zone and by meeters and  
greeters on the landside. This desk is equipped with a landline connecting to transla-  
tors for 170 different languages. A similar twenty-four-hour information desk was  
installed near the carousels in the baggage claim area where Dziekański had spent  
approximately six hours.22 The changes included new signage that uses pictograms  
to direct passengers to the landside area of the arrivals hall. Furthermore, the VIAA  
deployed information cards throughout the airport that explained in twenty differ-  
ent languages that translation services were available and that travellers would only  
need to point to the language that needed translation. The airport authority increased  
the number of personnel in the baggage claim area and equipped them with mobile  
phones that access the language line and emergency services. What is notable is that  
the VIAA did not simply translate existing text, but they also deployed human agents  
throughout the space; in their aim to improve the navigability of the terminal, the  
VIAA increased the number of staff to help with passenger wayfinding. While there  
was some attention to an improvement in architectural design, planners recognized  
that design cues to order circulation do not have the desired effect on all passengers.  
In short, the functional aesthetic addressed in this chapter was recognized as only a  
partial solution and human agents were hired to provide support.  
These services no doubt help with passengers who are unable to read YVR’s sig-  
nage, which is in Canada’s two official languages, English and French, as well as Chi-  
nese text. YVR is not exceptional in terms of its use of English on its signage since it  
is the lingua franca of air travel. Airports in cities such as Bangkok, Shanghai, Lima,  
and Athens have signage that is in both the country’s official language and English.  
In most cases, the official language appears on top with English below. Schiphol is  
an exception. The airport, which used to follow this formulation, redesigned its sig-  
nage in 2000 such that English would be the sole language used unless it was deemed  
necessary to include Dutch text \(Mijksenaar 2012\). The rationale behind the change  
was that words such as “gate,” “toilet,” “departures,” and “arrivals” were almost  
universally identifiable by international air travellers since English is used in virtually  
every airport across the world. Only in select cases would Dutch text be permitted.  
For example, on signs pointing to First Aid, the Dutch text, “Eerste hulp,” would be  
printed under the English text in a smaller and lighter font.  
Conclusion  
Airport architects designing today’s international terminals incorporate material cues  
that are intended to help travellers navigate and orient themselves within the build-  
ing’s interior. Their design ethos, which mirrors key tenets of actor-network theory,  
assumes that the terminal’s materiality can operate as an active agent to mediate pas-  
senger behaviour. Some of the terminal’s components such as escalators and travel-  
lators prescribe only a limited set of actions for the airport’s user. Other wayfinding  
strategies, such as skylights and flooring patterns that emphasize directional flows,  
are more subtle material cues and often operate on a visual register. These designs  
constitute an aesthetic type determined by their function, and their appeal correlates  
with their usefulness as a design for wayfinding. Moreover, if a ceiling design is going  
to effectively facilitate passenger navigation, then it must look appropriate for this  
purpose. Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 73  
Even though an architect assumes that their design is fit to serve its wayfinding  
function, it may not operate as intended. Indeed, a design cannot determine a spe-  
cific behaviour, and it will likely incite a multitude of responses among its users. The  
renovations at Vancouver’s YVR following Dziekański’s death illustrate how planners  
recognize that material designs may not always operate as envisioned and that human  
agents are sometimes necessary to lead the way.  
In the following chapter, I concentrate on how artworks are expected to serve way-  
finding purposes. I examine how architects deploy artworks for orientation and how  
they often install them near consumer spaces to draw passengers towards shops and  
restaurants.  
Notes  
1\. Parsons and Carlson refer to their theory as “Functional Beauty.” However, their use of the  
word “beauty” requires some clarification. Although they concede that “beauty” is a con-  
tentious term, they note that they decided to refer to their theory as functional beauty since  
no other expression—aside from the lengthy phrase, “aesthetic appreciation involving  
knowledge that concerns function”—could succinctly and properly convey their concept  
\(2008, xii\). Parsons and Carlson explain that they use the term “beauty” in the broader  
sense of its meaning, which is “aesthetic appeal in general,” and that they are not referring  
to “the specific sort of aesthetic appearance it typically suggests” \(2008, xiii\).  
2\. See also Karen A. Cerulo’s \(2009\) discussion of actor-network theory and Erin J. Camp-  
bell’s \(2014\) analysis of how Latour’s and other theorists’ ideas concerning the agency of  
things apply to studies of the decorative arts.  
3\. See also Andrew Pickering’s \(1995\) analysis of scientific knowledge and practice in terms  
of a “performative idiom” as opposed to a “representational idiom”; he argues that both  
humans and non-humans are active agents in the production of knowledge but only  
humans have the capacity to act with intention.  
4\. Latour and Yaneva also propose that actor-network theory can offer a more detailed  
description of the architectural design process\:  
We should finally be able to picture a building as a navigation through a controversial  
datascape\: as an animated series of projects, successful and failing, as a changing and  
criss-crossing trajectory of unstable definitions and expertise, of recalcitrant materials  
and building technologies, of flip-flopping users’ concerns and communities’ appraisals.  
\(2008, 87; italics in original\)  
See Albena Yaneva’s \(2005, 2009, 2016\) and Sophie Houdart’s \(2008\) investigations of  
architectural design practices for these types of analyses.  
5\. This attention to practices and space has influenced some of the theoretical texts on mobili-  
ties such as John Urry’s \(2007\) writing on mobility-systems and Tim Cresswell’s notion of  
mobility as “socially produced motion” \(2006, 3\).  
Nigel Thrift \(2004a\) extrapolates from de Certeau’s chapter “Walking in the City”  
\(1984, 91–110\) and reflects on how people drive through the urban automobile network.  
Although things such as roads, bridges, highways, stop signs, and stop lights are visible and  
people are generally conscious of this built environment, there are a host of other aspects  
of infrastructure and automobiles that influence people’s movements on a non-cognitive  
register. For instance, software is changing how people drive with improvements such as  
anti-lock brakes, traction control, and navigation systems. Thrift \(2004b, 2007\) has also  
theorized how non-cognitive bodily practices are influenced by things such as software,  
urban infrastructures, and ergonomics, which operate as a “technological unconscious.”  
See also Martin Dodge and Rob Kitchin’s \(2004\) analysis of software and practices in  
the air terminal. They examine how passengers’ and airport employees’ practices are influ-  
enced by their interactions with a myriad of computer programs either visible on a screen  
or operating silently in the background. 74 Agency and a Functional Aesthetic  
6\. While Leach \(2002\) suggests that studies of architecture should account for “narratives  
of use,” he does not include a case study where a building’s inhabitants are observed or  
interviewed to ascertain how their practices might point to the building’s identity. His  
notion of architectural identity is based on a theoretical supposition as to how individuals  
might identify with a building. For an analysis of how practices performed within a build-  
ing contrast with its design’s symbolic meaning, see Rhodri Windsor Liscombe’s \(2014\)  
investigation of how the uses of various British and North American buildings designed in  
a classical style belie the ideologies associated with this architectural aesthetic.  
7\. Scholars and critics mostly addressed whether the Postmodern building’s Colosseum-like  
appearance was an inauthentic structure that did not recall the site’s history and whether  
the design was culturally insensitive to Vancouver’s growing Asian population \(Lees 2001;  
Windsor Liscombe 2000\).  
8\. Lees contends that her method of investigating architecture “recognizes that \[a building’s\]  
meaning is not something fixed but is ongoing and continually produced through prac-  
tice, for human action itself is forever unfinished” \(2001, 75\). Lees’s study has inspired a  
number of other cultural geographers \(Kraftl 2006; Rose, Degen, and Basdas 2010; Jacobs  
and Merriman 2011\) to consider buildings in the context of their inhabitants’ practices.  
Like Lees, they employ ethnographic methods—they observe and take accounts of prac-  
tices while they are being performed, which in some cases includes interviews with the  
inhabitants. This contrasts with methods used by architectural historians. Their investiga-  
tions typically do not involve observations of humans in action but focus on the build-  
ing’s material structure, planning documents, and other printed materials such as journals  
and newspapers. However, see Mark Llewellyn’s \(2004\) investigation of Kensal House, a  
modernist housing flat in London, for a study of past lived experiences inspired by Lees’s  
“critical geography of architecture.” Llewellyn examines the experience of living in Kensal  
House during the 1930s; he draws from surveys conducted shortly after the building was  
constructed and his own interviews with former residents.  
9\. In Being and Time, Martin Heidegger \(1962\) grapples with how, as human beings, we exist  
and considers this in terms of how we encounter other entities as either “present-at-hand”  
or “ready-to-hand” entities. A present-at-hand entity is approached via one’s consciousness  
and is considered in relation to its apparent properties. Many of the things we encounter  
in our own worlds, he argues, are ready-to-hand entities—such as his famous example,  
the hammer when used for hammering—which exist as unnoticed entities in everyday life  
until they malfunction. When the carpenter uses the hammer for hammering, he does not  
think of the tool according to its apparent properties; only when the hammer breaks the  
carpenter contemplates the not ready-to-hand broken tool as present-at-hand.  
10\. Parsons and Carlson \(2008\) argue that this type of aesthetic, which derives from the  
knowledge of a thing’s function, contrasts with a major tenet in Immanuel Kant’s tracts on  
the judgement of taste and beauty, disinterestedness. In judging pure beauty, Kant argues,  
the beholder must approach the object without attending to any concepts pertaining to the  
object and without any consideration of how the object might fulfil any needs or desires.  
Drawing from theories that consider an object’s aesthetics in terms of its social, cultural,  
and historical context, Parsons and Carlson reject Kantian disinterestedness and argue  
that concepts pertaining to things can determine the thing’s aesthetic properties. Although  
Kant’s notion of dependent beauty allows for an aesthetic judgment that accounts for the  
thing’s function and use, Parsons and Carlson note that this type of beauty considers how  
an object’s aesthetic properties might be appropriate for its function and is unlike their  
notion of functional beauty, which derives a thing’s aesthetics from its function.  
11\. Saarinen’s curvilinear design for the TWA terminal was intended to evoke the dynamism of  
flight \(Merkel 2005; Hubregtse 2017\).  
12\. Taut writes\: “The architect who achieves this task becomes a creator of an ethical and  
social character; the people who use the building for any purpose, will, through the struc-  
ture of the house, be brought to a better behaviour in their mutual dealings and relation-  
ship with each other” \(1929, 9\).  
13\. These gates are used for flights departing to airports within the Schengen zone. Flights to  
non-Schengen locations depart from the M- and S-gates in Terminal 4’s satellite building.  
It also has support structures that gradually change colour. Agency and a Functional Aesthetic 75  
14\. See Howard Risatti \(2007\) and Nick Zangwill \(2001\) for examples of philosophical con-  
siderations of the functional aesthetics of man-made objects in terms of the designer’s or  
artist’s intentions.  
15\. Like Parsons and Carlson \(2008\), Preston \(2000\) draws her notion of proper function from  
Ruth Garrett Millikan \(1984, 1989\) and discusses it in the context of Robert Cumming’s  
\(1975\) causal role function, which she refers to as a “system function.” She posits that both  
Cummings and Millikan provide equally strong philosophical considerations of function,  
and she argues that these notions complement each other and that theories of function  
should account for both proper functions and system functions.  
16\. While my assessment of walkthrough duty-free stores focuses on a functional aesthetic  
with regard to organizing passenger movement, I do not discount that there are other aes-  
thetic factors belonging to this space. For instance, shopping itself can be an aesthetic and  
pleasurable experience \(Holbrook and Hirschman 1982\).  
17\. Edmund Burke \(1757\) examines this aesthetic type in Philosophical Enquiry into the Ori-  
gin of Our Ideas of the Sublime and Beautiful. Joan Coutu \(2017\) considers the relation-  
ship between time, the sensuous, and Burke’s sublime in her analysis of the architectural  
design and sculptures in the Marquis of Rockingham’s Wentworth Woodhouse and British  
politics in the late eighteenth century.  
18\. According to Augé \(1995\), signage is a distinguishing characteristic of “non-places.” These  
include other transit spaces such as railway stations and highways and large-scale commer-  
cial operations such as supermarkets.  
19\. Schaberg’s \(2012\) analysis also examines the airport in terms of a broader scope of texts;  
particularly, how the airport is experienced by many passengers as a space for reading and  
how the airport is described in literary representations.  
20\. His mother also spoke with CBSA officials at their office near the exit from the airside to  
the landside arrivals area. She was told that he had not been processed, even though by this  
time he had already passed through the passport inspection zone.  
21\. Paul Pritchard, a passenger in the landside area of the arrivals hall, started filming  
Dziekański with a digital camera when he started behaving erratically and kept recording  
until a few minutes after Dziekański became motionless. This video, which was released  
to the press, was seen by millions and caused a public outcry. It would later be scrutinized  
at an inquiry led by Justice Thomas Braidwood \(Bailey 2008, 2010\). During the inquiry,  
which ran between 2008 and 2010, Dziekański’s and the RCMP officers’ practices depicted  
in the video were carefully examined frame-by-frame to ascertain whether the use of a  
Taser was warranted. In his final report on the Dziekański affair, Justice Braidwood praised  
Vancouver’s airport authority for adding services aimed at helping international travellers  
who do not speak English \(Braidwood 2010\).  
22\. The VIAA’s installation of extra information desks and distribution of phones with direct  
access to the language line among personnel is undoubtedly a successful strategy for over-  
coming language barriers faced by some passengers. Within a few months of implementing  
this change, the VIAA’s President, Larry Berg \(2008\), reported that the language line was  
being used four to six times per day and that the languages that most often needed to be  
translated were Spanish and Mandarin. He also noted that the newly installed twenty-  
four-hour information desk in the CBSA baggage claim area received approximately 250  
inquiries per day, which were primarily questions regarding directions.
