edited by Emily Brady and Jerrold Levinson, 123–48. Oxford\: Clarendon Press. 3 Installing Artworks for Wayfinding  
# and Commerce  
In Chapter 2, I examined how architects incorporate material cues into their air termi-  
nal designs for wayfinding purposes. In this chapter, I focus on how architects deploy  
artworks as easily recognizable landmarks for orientation. Like ceiling designs and  
flooring patterns, these installations are active agents that mediate passengers’ actions  
in the terminal. I discuss how air terminal architects and planners deliberately choose  
artworks that will stand out as conspicuous material objects. Moreover, these sculptures  
are typically designed with colours, shapes, and finishes that contrast with the sur-  
rounding interior. Some airports such as Amsterdam’s Schiphol, Vancouver’s YVR, and  
Bangkok’s Suvarnabhumi include these distinctive artworks on their terminals’ maps.  
I argue that these types of artworks, like the architectural designs discussed in the last  
chapter, constitute a functional aesthetic with regard to their wayfinding purpose.  
I also discuss how some airport planners install artworks to draw passengers  
towards retail spaces and why they believe that these works will spur the travellers’  
desire to spend. I examine works at Schiphol, YVR, San Francisco International, Los  
Angeles’s Tom Bradley International Terminal, and Toronto Pearson, and I explain  
how they are intended to help generate revenue. I pay particular attention to artworks  
integrated into the building’s architecture during the terminal’s planning and construc-  
tion phases. Often, these pieces are installed near commercial spaces. Furthermore,  
artists are typically required to adjust these integrated artworks to suit the terminal’s  
interior design. Nevertheless, architects occasionally design a terminal to accord with  
a commissioned work and its placement in the interior. For instance, Bill Reid’s Spirit  
of Haida Gwaii\: The Jade Canoe at Vancouver’s International Terminal and Richard  
Serra’s Tilted Spheres at Toronto Pearson’s Terminal 1 were acquired during these  
buildings’ construction phases and significantly impacted their architectural designs.  
I examine these works in detail with respect to these terminals’ design processes as well  
as how they contribute to these terminals’ functions. Reid’s The Jade Canoe is perhaps  
an ideal example since it acts as a significant landmark and draws passengers towards  
adjacent commercial spaces. Serra’s work also serves these functions when it is viewed  
from the exterior. Yet, its interior space undermines these aims since it shields the  
traveller’s view from the terminal’s retail stores, restaurants, and wayfinding signage.  
Artworks and Orientation  
In many of today’s international terminals, planners have installed artworks to aid  
with passenger navigation and orientation. At YVR and Schiphol, architects and plan-  
ners expect these pieces to operate as active agents, and they have chosen artworks to  
function as way-posts because of their materiality. While they carefully select artworks 80 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
based on their representational content, they also consider how an artwork’s material  
aspects will be grasped in the background of a passenger’s attention. Hans Smits, the  
former director of Schiphol’s art committee, noted that their aim was to install artworks  
that were visible in the corner of the traveller’s eye \(Marck 1995\). This committee  
chose installations such as Dennis Adams’s Coda which passengers would differenti-  
ate from the miles of transparent glass walls and the predominantly white-and-grey-  
coloured floors and ceilings. Coda is notable in regard to wayfinding and colour \(see  
Plate 6\). Its red-and-white checkered pattern contrasts with Schiphol Plaza’s subdued  
tones, and it effectively operates as an eye-catching landmark. Schiphol has designated  
this piece as an official meeting point. Like Heathrow Terminal 5’s skylights and HKIA  
Terminal 1’s longitudinal vaults \(Figure I.2, 1.11\), this artwork looks fit in terms of its  
wayfinding function. Its vibrant colour scheme and monumental size allows it to stand  
out as a noticeable object in a busy space of passenger flows.  
At Vancouver’s airport, Stantec deploys artworks for wayfinding purposes as well, and  
they also choose works with colours that contrast with the surrounding interior design.  
Smith’s Freedom to Move, which the firm installed in the Domestic Terminal in 2009,  
consists of a series of cedar panels adorned with red and black formline representations  
of animals including a raven, an eagle, a grizzly bear, and a killer whale \(see Plate 13\).  
The work stands out among the blue-green carpeting and white ceiling. Stantec placed  
Freedom to Move at a strategic “decision point” \(Noel Best, pers. comm., December 17,  
2018\). It is situated at a three-way intersection in the pier serving the C-gates \(Figure 3.1\).  
Figure 3.1 Stantec, Plan of the Domestic Terminal’s Pier C, Detail, Vancouver International  
Airport.  
Source\: © Stantec. Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 81  
Stantec’s Noel Best notes that the work is not meant to stop passengers in their tracks  
but to “break their pace” and draw their attention to the approaching intersection.  
Smith’s initial conception for Freedom to Move envisioned the cedar pieces fixed  
to the floor without a surrounding structure \(Noel Best, pers. comm., December 17,  
2018\). Stantec suggested that the sculpture should be set in a pool since the wooden  
panels could be easily damaged by baggage trolleys, motorized carts, or careless indi-  
viduals. When it was installed, Freedom to Move was placed on a podium in a pool of  
water enclosed by concrete. Wooden benches are fastened to this low protective wall  
to offer passengers extra seating space. While this concrete wall serves a functional  
purpose with regard to durability and preserving the sculptural work, it does not  
specifically pertain to the functional aesthetic I outlined in the last chapter. Rather,  
this aesthetic concerns the more striking aspects of the work—its cedar panels with  
red and black formline designs allow this piece to operate effectively as a guide for  
wayfinding.  
Schiphol and YVR depict some of their terminals’ artworks on their maps. This  
includes Adams’s Coda and Mark Brusse’s I meet you at Schiphol and Smith’s  
Freedom to Move and Reid’s The Jade Canoe at YVR \(Figure 1.5; see Plates 1, 6,  
and 13\). What is notable is that the artworks on these maps, which are supposed to  
be landmarks, are not identified by their titles or the artists that created them but are  
icons referring to their physical forms and colours—quick identifiers easily grasped by  
passengers who are trying to navigate the space. Coda’s form is parsed down to a cube  
with unbroken edges; the work, however, has a bench for seating and openings that  
lead into the work’s interior space. What’s more, each side is rendered with a 3x3 grid  
of red-and-white checkers rather than the 7x7 grid that actually appears on the sculp-  
ture’s surface. Schiphol includes these simplified renditions of the terminal’s artworks  
on the maps posted throughout the airport, on paper copies available at information  
desks, on its website, and on an app available for mobile phones and tablets. YVR  
uses these icons on the maps placed in the terminal and posted on the airport’s web-  
site. Schiphol’s maps also depict some distinctive architectural features. The map for  
Lounge 3, which is in the post-security area near the F-pier, includes a green-coloured  
icon that refers to the House of Tulips store \(see Plate 14\). It is built out of green glass  
and resembles an Amsterdam canal house \(see Plate 15\). I discuss Studio Tjep’s place-  
themed design for the House of Tulips’ exterior in further detail in Chapter 5.  
Schiphol has commissioned the wayfinding firm, Mijksenaar, to create the maps  
for its interiors. Maps that are fixed to a column or a pier are designed according to  
the passenger’s orientation in the space \(Fenne Roefs, pers. comm., April 11, 2018\).  
The firm only includes an icon of an artwork or a distinctive interior design if it is  
within view of where the map is placed in the terminal. Their intent is to indicate  
artworks strictly for navigation—these maps are not intended to provide a tour of  
the artworks installed in the terminal \(Hans Bouchier, pers. comm., April 11, 2018\).  
Furthermore, an icon is depicted such that it coincides with the passenger’s position  
in the interior. For instance, the map located to the left of the House of Tulips shop  
depicts the icon for the store immediately to the right of where “You” is indicated  
\(see Plate 14\). Mijksenaar’s designers also believe that a view of an aircraft is an  
effective wayfinding tool \(Thoas Hooning van Duyvenbode, pers. comm., April 11,  
2018\).  
1 Their maps include icons of airplanes on the apron, and they situate them  
according to the traveller’s orientation. Aircraft appear at the top of the map if the  
apron is in front of the passenger and at the bottom of the map if the apron is behind  
the passenger. 82 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
Based on my observations from visits to forty-two international airports across the  
world, only a small number of terminals use artworks on their maps. Schiphol and  
YVR have layouts that are hard to discern as a passenger walking through their inte-  
riors, and artworks can certainly help with navigating oneself through these compli-  
cated spaces. These two airports’ landside and airside areas have expanded a number  
of times since their terminals opened in 1967 and 1968 respectively.2 Yet, it is not  
only terminals that have grown by accretion that necessarily use artworks as guides  
for navigation and represent them as icons on their maps. The terminal at Bangkok’s  
Suvarnabhumi Airport, which opened in 2006 and contains large open spaces like  
HKIA’s Terminal 1 and Heathrow Terminal 5, has a series of sculptures that appear  
on maps posted throughout the building.3 This includes Scene of The Churning of The  
Milk Ocean, an elaborate installation comprised of figures depicting Hindu super-  
natural beings \(see Plate 16\). It is situated on the airside of the terminal, which con-  
sists of a concourse over one kilometre in length \(Figure 3.2\). Two clusters of gates are  
situated on each end. This sculpture is in the centre point of this long passageway, which is  
lined with shops, restaurants, and boutiques. At each end of the concourse, planners have  
Figure 3.2 Murphy/Jahn, Plan of Departures Level, Bangkok Suvarnabhumi Airport.  
Source\: © JAHN. Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 83  
installed an open-walled structure with a high-pitched roof in a style akin to the highly  
ornamented gables found on traditional Thai temples \(Figure 3.3\). These buildings are  
included on the airport’s maps. These artworks and structures undoubtedly help pas-  
sengers orient themselves in the space. There are three security checkpoints that lead  
Figure 3.3 Gift Shop, Bangkok Suvarnabhumi Airport, Thailand, 2006.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\). 84 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
from the landside to the airside, and these decision points are distributed symmetri-  
cally along the length of the post-security concourse. The Scene of The Churning of  
The Milk Ocean is situated near the central security checkpoint.  
Artworks and Commerce  
Air terminal artworks are installed to support wayfinding as well as commercial pur-  
poses. Some planners place artworks near shops and restaurants to draw travellers  
towards these consumer spaces, and they often expect that these pieces will increase  
the passengers’ desire to spend. However, this planning principle is not universal to  
all airports. At Stansted, for example, there are no artworks in the terminal. In this  
section, I examine the types of works installed at Schiphol, YVR, Toronto Pearson,  
San Francisco International, and Los Angeles’s Tom Bradley International Terminal,  
and I consider how their placement in the building affects passenger spending. Before  
I address these pieces, I consider the reasons why some airport planners elect to not  
commission pieces for their terminal interiors.  
Some planners choose to not install artworks since they occupy spaces that could be  
exploited commercially \(de Wijn 2013\). Schiphol’s architect Jan Benthem notes that  
those in charge of commerce often challenge proposals for artworks since they are  
constantly seeking more advertising and retail space to boost revenue \(Erdogan 2003\).  
Benthem defends the reasoning behind the installation of Dennis Adams’s Coda in the  
central space in Schiphol Plaza \(see Plate 6\). Benthem explains that “every town square  
needs an artwork, a meeting point, a village pump” and that “commerce should yield to  
functionality” \(cited in Erdogan 2003, 14\).4 As noted, this artwork was commissioned  
to operate as an eye-catching landmark in a space of multiple flows, and it appears on  
the terminal’s maps. Clearly for Benthem, the airport’s primary function is the smooth  
circulation of passengers, and its capacity to generate revenue is secondary.  
In some cases air terminal artworks are moved to a different location to make way  
for shops and restaurants or other renovations. Sometimes they are removed and no  
longer displayed in the terminal. Michel Santry suspects that his aerial installation was  
removed from HKIA’s Terminal 1 in 2003 to create more space for advertising \(Korpo-  
raal 2003\). The artwork was suspended from the ceiling in the landside area of Terminal  
1 when it opened in 1998. It comprised of three separate sections, each made with steel  
tubing and colourful lightweight cloths. Airport officials claimed that it was removed  
because it was too difficult to clean. Santry’s suspicions may have been correct, since  
there are a number of advertisements that hang from the ceiling in the landside area  
of the terminal \(Figure 1.2\). HKIA has also removed Tao Ho’s A Synergy of Dynamic  
Energy. Like Santry’s artwork, it was commissioned for Terminal 1 and suspended from  
the ceiling \(Xinhua News Agency 1997\).5 Ho’s artwork, constructed out of coloured  
aluminium tubes and stainless steel wire, consisted of eighteen icosahedra, which was  
meant to symbolize the relationship between the eighteen districts in Hong Kong.  
A developer’s resistance to including artworks in commercial spaces is unsurprising.  
Architect Victor Gruen, a pioneer of shopping centre design, faced criticism from plan-  
ners when he commissioned artworks for malls during the 1950s \(Wall 2005\). Nonethe-  
less, some developers supported his initiative to include creative works in these spaces.  
His design for the Northland Shopping Center included 200,000 USD worth of artworks  
when it opened in 1954 \(Gruen and Smith 1960\). Gruen did not aim to include art “in the  
form of a museum-like display” \(Gruen and Smith 1960, 153\). Rather, he integrated the  
works into the shopping centre’s architecture and landscaping.6 Like Coda at Schiphol, Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 85  
these pieces acted as landmarks and meeting points within the mall’s public spaces. Gruen  
did not regard these artworks simply as a means to stimulate spending; they contributed  
to his overall aim to design shopping malls as civic centres that met the community’s social  
and cultural needs. In Shopping Towns USA\: The Planning of Shopping Centres, Gruen  
and Larry Smith reported on the reception of Northland’s artworks\:  
After the opening of the shopping centre, the developer, the executives of his  
organization and the tenants declared themselves delighted with the results. The  
publicity value and promotional opportunity were impressive. Even criticism of  
individual pieces and the resulting controversies were valuable as an expression  
of community life.  
\(1960, 157\)  
While air terminal and shopping mall developers may be delighted with artworks  
in commercial spaces, their capacity to increase spending is hard to measure. Dur-  
ing Smits’s tenure as Schiphol’s Chief Executive Officer, the airport, along with the  
Mondriaan Stichting, commissioned a study into the links between marketing strate-  
gies and air terminal artworks. Dorothée van Hooff \(1998\) published the results in  
Tegenbeelden\: Marketingstrategie en Kunstopdrachten.  
7 She contends that Schiphol’s  
selection of artworks reflects its corporate identity and has bolstered the company’s  
image. She argues that installing artworks in the terminal may lead to more passen-  
gers transferring through the airport; like good shopping options and clean toilets,  
artworks might help attract transferring passengers. Although she admits that it is  
not possible to quantify the returns on installing artworks in terminals, she adds that  
neither is the friendly face of a counter attendant.  
Van Hooff’s \(1998\) Tegenbeelden is a remarkable document when considering  
Gruen and Smith’s \(1960\) comments regarding Northland’s artworks. Not only does  
it address the promotional aspect of artworks, but the study was ordered shortly after  
art critics attacked works installed in Schiphol in 1993. Mark Brusse’s I meet you, for  
instance, was derided for being too touristic \(Figure 1.5\). Smits, who commissioned  
Tegenbeelden, was part of an art committee that was formed to address this criti-  
cism. The committee developed a proposal that sought works which critically reflect  
upon the passenger’s experience of time and space in the terminal. In 1995, Schiphol  
installed four works that address this theme. These include Adams’s Coda, Marc Ruy-  
grok’s So go on, Niek Kemps’s Closed View, and Jenny Holzer’s Untitled. I examine  
these artworks in further detail in Chapter 4.  
Van Hooff \(1998\) does not focus solely on this series. She considers the wide array  
of artworks installed at Schiphol since the terminal opened in 1967.8 A number of the  
pieces added during the 1960s and 1970s were sculptural works in a modernist idiom  
consisting of simple formal arrangements. Carel Visser’s Salami, installed in 1967, is  
a layered stack of thin rectangular metal blocks. It is located outdoors in a rectan-  
gular water pool near the entrance to Schiphol Plaza. That same year André Volt-  
en’s Rustcloud was hung from the terminal’s ceiling. It is made out of small slices of  
metal I-beams welded together.9 In 1974, the airport added Tajiri’s Knot \(Figure 2.4\).  
In 1988, the airport decided that it would allocate 0.2 percent of its construction costs  
for new buildings and renovations to commissioning new artworks \(van Hooff 1998\).  
Five years later, it increased funding to 0.5 percent. These funds paid for artworks  
such as Carel Visser’s Flying Fish, Brusse’s I meet you, Adams’s Coda, Ruygrok’s So  
go on, Kemps’s Closed View, and Holzer’s Untitled. Schiphol has installed a number 86 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
of other artworks since Tegenbeelden was published in 1998. In the following chap-  
ters, I address Cor Dera’s Flora of the Low Lands and Daan Roosegaarde’s Beyond,  
installed in 2007 and 2016 respectively.  
The airport has opened a satellite gallery of the Rijksmuseum since van Hooff \(1998\)  
published Tegenbeelden. In 2002, the airport began to show some of the Netherlands’  
most famous artworks to passengers transiting through the airport’s non-Schengen  
area \(Koster 2002\). The museum showcases seventeenth-century paintings by Dutch  
Masters such as Rembrandt van Rijn, Jan Steen, and Jacob Isaacksz van Ruisdael.10  
The gallery’s interior is dimly lit, and it offers travellers a moment of tranquillity away  
from the hustle and bustle outside. This changes when passengers exit the gallery and  
enter the museum’s gift shop, which sells things such as models of Amsterdam’s canal  
houses, Delft Blue cups, plates, and tiles, and purses and umbrellas with patterns taken  
from Vincent van Gogh’s paintings \(Figure 3.4\). The purpose of the Rijksmuseum sat-  
ellite gallery is to offer a glimpse of Dutch culture for passengers transferring through  
Schiphol—approximately forty percent of the total passenger traffic \(de Haan 2002\).  
It is undoubtedly an appealing amenity for travellers who need to spend a layover in  
a European hub. However, the museum was not only deployed to draw transferring  
passengers to Schiphol. It is intended to encourage them to travel to Holland and,  
hopefully, visit the Rijksmuseum in Amsterdam.11  
Like Schiphol, San Francisco International has installed a wide array of artworks in  
its terminals, and it has likewise reserved a percentage of the total construction costs for  
Figure 3.4 Rijksmuseum Museum Shop, Amsterdam Airport Schiphol, Netherlands, 2017.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 87  
art. But the amount set aside is more generous. The airport is beholden to a municipal  
ordinance that requires all public projects to attribute two percent of its development  
costs towards public art \(San Francisco Arts Commission n.d.\). The city’s Arts Commis-  
sion began working with the airport in 1977 when it was developing Terminal 3 \(San  
Francisco International Airport n.d.b\). It has since commissioned works for Terminals  
1 and 2 as well as the airport’s International Terminal, which was completed in 2000.12  
While most are wall-mounted paintings and free-standing sculptures, there are some  
artworks integrated into the buildings’ architecture. The majority of the works installed  
in the International Terminal are on the airside. They consist of two-dimensional pieces  
mounted to a two-storey wall beside each gate. Passengers can view these works from  
the central corridor on the upper level as well as the gate’s waiting area on the lower  
level. Some of these works depict imagery inspired by San Francisco’s community as  
well as more general aviation themes. Juana Alicia and Emmanuel Catarino Montoya’s  
Sanctuary/Sanctuario is a 7.3-metre-long and 6.1-metre-tall fresco mural that depicts  
the city’s diverse population \(see Plate 17\). Rupert Garcia’s Bird Technology is a similar-  
sized work constructed out of hand-painted ceramic tiles \(see Plate 18\). It depicts a sil-  
houette of a bird as well as a grid of coloured squares. The label notes that the bird refers  
to “natural flight” and the “geometric grid . . . symbolizes the technological advances  
that made human flight possible.” On the International Terminal’s landside, there are  
two sculptural works incorporated into the building’s architecture. James Carpenter’s  
Four Sculptural Light Reflectors consists of a translucent material affixed to the ceil-  
ing trusses high above the check-in counters. It is intended to recall the shape of early  
flying machines such as dirigibles and the Wright brothers’ first aircraft. Vito Acconci’s  
Light Beams for the Sky of a Transfer Corridor is incorporated into the design of a pas-  
sageway connecting the International Terminal to Terminals 1, 2, and 3 \(Figure 3.5\).  
Fluorescent lighting encased in plastic laminate criss-crosses the corridor’s walls and  
ceilings. Each light fixture is anchored with a non-functional public telephone.  
Aside from these types of permanent installations, San Francisco International dis-  
plays temporary exhibitions on art, science, history, and culture. SFO Museum is an  
accredited museum with its own team of curators and designers.13 Unlike Schiphol’s  
Rijksmuseum, these exhibitions are located in display cases and dedicated wall spaces  
throughout the landside and airside of all four terminals. San Francisco’s airport does  
not capitalize on these exhibitions in the same fashion as Schiphol. There is no gift  
shop placed at the gallery’s exit since the museum is distributed across the airport. Nor  
does the SFO Museum have its own gift shop. But, there is a San Francisco Museum of  
Modern Art store located in the International Terminal’s pre-security departures area.  
Louis Turpen, San Francisco International’s director from 1981 to 1995, recognized  
how art increases an air terminal’s appeal and how it can be used for commercial  
gain \(Milroy 2003\). He was recognized for overseeing an increase in spending at San  
Francisco’s airport during his tenure, and he was expected to generate an increase in  
revenue at Toronto Pearson when he was hired to direct the Greater Toronto Airports  
Authority \(GTAA\) in 1995 \(Willis 1997\). Turpen’s main achievement as the GTAA’s  
president and CEO was the redevelopment of the airport’s Terminal 1, which opened  
in 2004.14 The firm commissioned Moshe Safdie, Skidmore Owings & Merrill, and  
Adamson Associates to design the building.  
Turpen is largely credited for the GTAA’s decision to install artworks in the terminal  
\(Milroy 2003\). Unlike San Francisco, the airport was not required to set aside funds for  
art. Nonetheless, the GTAA spent approximately nine million CAD on art installations—  
or roughly 0.25 percent of the 3.6 billion CAD construction costs \(Cornell 2004; Rochon 88 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
Figure 3.5 Vito Acconci, Light Beams for the Sky of a Transfer Corridor, San Francisco Inter-  
national Airport, United States, 2000.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
2004\). Furthermore, the GTAA did not need to work with a local arts commission, so it  
was free to choose the types of works and determine their placement within the terminal.  
In 2004, Turpen recalled his experience with the San Francisco Arts Commission\:  
We were spending hundreds of millions of dollars and I’m sitting here twiddling  
my thumbs because somebody that I don’t know, who was hired by somebody  
that I don’t know, is doing something that I’ve never seen, to put someplace I’m  
not sure where.  
\(cited in Blum 2004\)  
For Toronto’s airport, Turpen hired Elsa Cameron, a San Francisco-based art con-  
sultant, to manage the commissioning process. The GTAA chose eight works that  
primarily evoke aviation-related themes, and the artists worked with the building’s  
architects to finalize their designs \(Greater Toronto Airports Authority n.d.\). At the  
time of the terminal’s opening, Safdie noted\: “This is the result of an extraordinary  
collaboration of artists and architects and that is rare. . . . We took risks as architects.  
I mean we gave the artists the best places in the building because we felt we had a  
dialogue with them” \(cited in Hiller 2004, 4\). Two years later, Safdie remarked that  
the terminal’s artworks serve a wayfinding purpose\: “These artworks are landmarks.  
They give specific character to specific places. And they enhance your sense of orienta-  
tion” \(cited in Hume 2006, 4\). Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 89  
Some of these works are located adjacent to commercial spaces. Robert Charles  
Coyle’s Flight Song is suspended from the ceiling next to a number of shops and res-  
taurants in the post-security zone that services domestic flights \(Figure 3.6\). It consists  
of nineteen 1.5-metre-long sheets of lexan which are folded into the shape of paper  
airplanes. This squadron of aircraft is flying through the concourse at an altitude  
Figure 3.6 Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\).  
Robert Charles Coyle, Flight Song, Toronto Pearson International Airport, Canada, 2003. 90 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
just above the store and restaurant signage. This suspended artwork also draws one’s  
eye to advertisements posted in the terminal. The squadron’s lead airplanes pass just  
above advertisements hanging below the clerestory windows adjacent to the D39-  
D41 gates. Katharina Grosse’s Untitled is a multi-coloured spray-painted mural  
that spans approximately twenty-three metres across two perpendicular walls \(see  
Plate 2\). One is situated above an entrance to a passageway leading to the D1-D12  
and D22-D28 gates, and the other is above a commercial space which now houses  
the Mill Street Brewery. The latter wall is capped with a skylight above, and Grosse’s  
paint extends onto the roof’s beams below this opening. During the installation, Safdie  
encouraged Grosse to extend her gestural strokes to the ceiling so that they appear to  
travel into the airspace above the terminal \(Hiller 2004\).  
The placement of these two works contrasts with the installations at San Francisco’s  
International Terminal. The most spectacular pieces on its airside are located in the  
waiting areas near the gates and away from the commercial spaces in the corridor  
leading to the gates. As noted, works such as Alicia and Montoya’s Sanctuary/Sanctu-  
ario and Garcia’s Bird Technology are mounted to a wall spanning two storeys, and  
viewers on the upper level can gaze upon these works from a balcony that also offers  
views of the apron and the gate’s waiting area below \(see Plates 17 and 18\). During my  
visit to the terminal in August 2018, there were movable seats placed by some of these  
balconies. The passengers occupying the seats were spending their dwell-time facing  
the artworks or the apron outside and not the commercial spaces located behind them  
along the corridor. The SFO Museum’s exhibitions also distract the passenger’s atten-  
tion away from advertisements and shopfronts. For example, display cases line the  
post-security corridor leading to Terminal 3’s gates \(Figure 3.7\).15 Even though only  
some of the passers-by are captivated by the artworks or historic objects shown, this  
ultimately results in a net loss of time spent near the airside retail spaces, which are  
located after the corridor.  
The redeveloped Terminal 2 at San Francisco’s airport, on the other hand, does  
include an artwork that was deliberately placed next to a shopping area \(see Plate 19\).  
Janet Echelman’s Every Beating Second was suspended over the ‘Recompose’ area  
when the terminal reopened in 2011 \(Henry 2011\). Passengers encounter this work  
directly upon exiting the pre-boarding security screening area. Gensler, the architec-  
tural firm that renovated the terminal, designed the Recompose area with the intent  
to relax passengers before they pass through the airside’s commercial spaces. It is lit  
by skylights and clerestory windows, and the ceiling is approximately twice as high as  
the security screening zone’s ceiling. The San Francisco Arts Commission collaborated  
with Gensler and the airport’s staff to create an “art program plan,” which called for  
an artwork that integrates with the Recompose area’s interior design.16 The commis-  
sioned work, Every Beating Second, consists of three sets of red and purple netting  
that hang below three skylights. These webs of weaved fibres droop between 5.8 and  
6.7 metres from the ceiling, and each net is suspended from two concentric rings\:  
an inner ring in the centre of the round skylight and an outer lobed metal armature  
attached to the ceiling.17 The nets gently pulse since they are stimulated by fans that  
emit a fluctuating air current. Below these translucent volumes are soft cushioned  
benches. Gensler notes that\: “Here, passengers can put on their shoes and gather their  
belongings in comfort, while scanning the shops and restaurants that line the walk  
toward the departure gates” \(Gensler n.d.\). This walk begins with passing by two Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 91  
Figure 3.7 SFO Museum Display Cases, San Francisco International Airport, United States.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
shops that abut this high-ceilinged space\: Compass Books and Kiehl’s, a cosmetics  
retailer.  
While Echelman’s vibrantly coloured Every Beating Second certainly draws the  
passer-by’s attention, it is relatively muted compared to Time Tower at Los Angeles’s  
Tom Bradley International Terminal \(Figure 3.8\). This twenty-two-metre-tall work  
encases an elevator shaft with a cubic footprint and projects high-resolution video on  
each of its four sides. Like Echelman’s sculpture, Time Tower is surrounded by shops  
including luxury retailers such as Michael Kors, Coach, Gucci, Hermes, and Burberry.  
The screens display metaphoric interpretations of time, including water streaming  
down a translucent surface, a slow panning view of distant galaxies, and a stack of  
suitcases with a clock that changes shape as it slithers downwards. The latter recalls  
the melted timepieces in Salvador Dalí’s The Persistence of Memory, 1931. Every hour  
the tower projects the actual time in the form of a clock with a case that mimics the  
Art Deco sunburst style. The moment is marked with a two-and-half-minute show  
where the clock’s inner mechanism is exposed and dancers perform a kaleidoscopic  
routine alongside the gearwheels supposedly driving the timepiece.18 Time Tower is a  
landmark on the airside, and it is clearly visible in the central hall situated between the  
north and south concourses that contain the terminal’s gates. The work, however, does Figure 3.8 Sardi Design, MRA International, and Moment Factory, Time Tower, Los Angeles  
International Airport, United States, 2013.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 93  
not merely orient the viewer—its slowly changing subject matter draws the viewer’s  
attention and entices them to dwell in the airside’s most concentrated commercial  
space.  
Time Tower is the largest of seven video works installed in 2013. This 40-mil-  
lion-dollar Integrated Environmental Media System was part of a 1.9-billion-dollar  
reconstruction and expansion which doubled the size of the terminal \(Sumers 2013;  
Templeton 2013; Society for Experiential Graphic Design n.d.\). Sardi Design and  
MRA International designed the video works while Fentress Architects was planning  
and renovating the terminal, and all seven works are integrated into the building’s  
architectural design. Montreal-based Moment Factory was commissioned to create  
over forty hours of video content for the installations. Sakchin Bessette, the firm’s  
creative director, noted that the videos were “designed with the sense of the magic of  
travel in mind” \(cited in Koepke 2013, 3\). Aside from Time Tower, there are two other  
sets of screens deployed in the central hall. The Story Board projects videos inspired by  
Los Angeles as well as other places “both real and imagined” \(Moment Factory 2013,  
n.d.\). The Destination Board displays departures information and graphics based on  
live weather data gathered from the flights’ destinations. There are two other screens  
leading in and out of the airside. The Bon Voyage Wall is situated in a passageway  
leading from the pre-boarding security check to the airside, and the Welcome Wall is  
placed in a corridor accessed by arriving passengers.  
There are also two series of screens, Portals, situated at the entrances to the north  
and south concourses adjacent to the central hall. Each portal consists of ten nar-  
row screens standing 8.5 metres tall. They are designed to display interactive videos  
based on live data. The screens project imagery based on destinations of outbound  
flights, and the video morphs in response to passers-by’s motions; there are sensors  
that capture movement in the surrounding area.19 Melora Koepke describes her expe-  
rience walking past this work in December 2013\: “As I walk by the North Concourse  
portal, each screen column is strung with one quivering string of a jazz manouche  
guitar\: The next outgoing flight is to Paris” \(2013, 3\). My encounter with this work  
in August 2018 was markedly different. At both the north and south portals, the ten  
screens displayed a bright pink advertisement for lipstick \(see Plate 20\). The Portals  
were transformed into a usual form of video advertisement one sees in the terminal;  
namely, a series of screens that are evenly distributed along a corridor or travellator  
and that project a synchronized loop of ads \(see Plate 5\).  
While Portals is a rare example of an artwork repurposed as an advertisement,  
there are a number of air terminal advertisements that are designed using analogous  
conventions employed in contemporary art. Heathrow Terminal 5, for instance, has  
light boxes displaying advertisements for the upscale clothing retailer Burberry that  
are installed in the wall and backlit in a similar fashion as Jeff Wall’s photographic  
transparencies such as The Destroyed Room, 1978, and The Vampires’ Picnic, 1991.  
Like the artworks discussed in this chapter, these spectacular advertisements also cap-  
ture the passenger’s attention.  
Vancouver’s YVR\: Place-Themed Artworks and Stimulating Spending  
Planners at Vancouver’s YVR likewise consider air terminal artworks as a potential  
boon for commercial gain. When the Vancouver International Airport Authority  
\(VIAA\) was developing the International Terminal during the 1990s, they believed 94 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
that Northwest Coast First Nations artworks would draw passengers towards retail  
spaces \(O’Neill 1998\). Reid’s The Jade Canoe, for example, was deliberately located  
close to the restaurants and food outlets on the departures level \(see Plate 1\). Its  
nearest neighbour is a Starbucks Coffee stand that abuts the eastern side of a theatre-  
like space that surrounds the work, and a larger food court is situated to the north.  
The VIAA not only installed these types of works to attract travellers to particular  
consumer spaces, but they also anticipated that the works themselves would stimu-  
late passenger spending. Frank O’Neill, chairman of the VIAA’s thematics committee,  
claimed\: “What art can do is create an ambiance and a feeling that puts people in a  
good mood. That, by the way, has a great commercial spinoff” \(cited in Bell 1996, 3\).  
The airport authority noted that this expected revenue would help pay for the five mil-  
lion dollars spent on artworks, which was two percent of the International Terminal’s  
250-million-dollar development costs \(Vancouver Sun 1994\). The airport’s planners  
chose a specific type of artwork to create their desired “ambiance.” They installed  
Northwest Coast First Nations artworks since they believed that these pieces would  
give the terminal a “sense of place,” and they argued that this evocation of a place-  
based identity would lead to higher spending in the terminal. In Chapter 5, I discuss  
the rationale behind this expectation in greater detail.  
YVR is a remarkable example in terms of airport artworks since its architects designed  
the International Terminal’s interior based on where these types of sculptural works would  
be placed. The VIAA purchased Reid’s The Jade Canoe three years before the terminal  
opened in 1996 \(Globe and Mail 1993\). Throughout the design process, the VIAA and  
the architects deliberated on where they would place this 6.05-metre-long, 3.48-metre-  
wide, and 3.89-metre-high bronze cast. In a watercolour from 1994, Rudy Kovach  
depicted The Jade Canoe as a central focal point in the terminal’s airside \(Figure 3.9\).20 It  
appears as though it is in the orchestra of a theatre-like space. A semi-circular arrange-  
ment of steps surrounding the monumental work leads upward to a food court area,  
and the sculpture is situated in front of a floor-to-ceiling glass wall that offers views  
of the coastal mountains. The VIAA ultimately decided to place the sculpture in the  
terminal’s landside on the departures level, since this is a publicly accessible area and  
more people can see the work \(Figure 3.10\).  
Yet, this required a design solution that would obscure the south-facing view of the  
glass wall behind it, as it looked out onto the parking and roadway, but still allowed  
light to shine in on the sculpture. They commissioned Lutz Haufschild to create a  
forty-metre-wide and ten-metre-tall glass mural \(O’Neill 1998\). The planners’ discus-  
sion regarding the mural’s imagery included Reid, who preferred a reference to the  
ocean environment that a Haida canoe would be situated in. These canoes, which  
were used traditionally to travel from Haida Gwaii to the British Columbia mainland,  
often encountered stormy seas in Hecate Strait. Haufschild attempted to capture the  
intensity of its crashing waves and modelled the mural after Hokusai’s famous early-  
1830s woodblock print, The Great Wave off Kanagawa.  
Waisman Dewar Grout Carter Inc.’s final design for the interior space surrounding  
Reid’s sculpture resembles Kovach’s illustration from 1994. The area surrounding the  
work is designed as a place where people can sit before passing through security or  
while waiting for friends and family to arrive \(see Plate 1\). Furthermore, The Jade  
Canoe is an eye-catching object in the terminal due to its shimmering reflective sur-  
face, and it appears to operate as an effective landmark. During my visits to the ter-  
minal, I observed that there is almost always someone engaging with the work; many  
of whom are passers-by who stop to photograph or be photographed with the work. Figure 3.9 Rudy Kovach, Sketch of the International Terminal’s Interior and Bill Reid’s The  
Jade Canoe, 1994. Watercolour.  
Source\: In the collection of the Vancouver International Airport Authority. Published in “Vancouver Inter-  
national Airport Expansion,” SkyTalk 1, no. 5 \(1994\)\: special insert.  
Figure 3.10 Stantec, Plan of the International Terminal’s Departures Level, Detail, Vancouver  
International Airport.  
Source\: © Stantec. Figure 3.11 Marianne Nicolson, The Rivers Monument, Vancouver International Airport,  
Canada, 2015.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2019\). Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 97  
In the International Terminal’s airside, planners have deployed artworks as well as  
pseudo-naturalistic settings that mimic British Columbia’s wilderness to draw passen-  
gers to consumer spaces \(see Plate 21\). The west post-security chevron, an expansion  
which opened in 2007, includes a stream flowing through a manufactured rocky chan-  
nel in the food court \(SkyTalk 2007a, 2007b\).21 A 114,000-litre saltwater aquarium  
containing marine life indigenous to British Columbia’s coast is located at the end of  
the creek.22 Situated directly above the tank’s glass pane is Orca Chief and the Kelp  
Forest, an installation consisting of Haisla artist Lyle Wilson’s formline representation  
of the Orca Chief and John Nutter’s ‘forest’ of etched glass panels. The food court’s  
tables and chairs flank this ‘supernatural’ scene, and restaurants, cafés, and shops  
circle its periphery.23  
YVR has also placed Northwest Coast First Nations artworks as guides for way-  
finding in its Domestic Terminal, and some of these are situated next to shops and  
restaurants. Marianne Nicolson’s The Rivers Monument, which was installed in the  
airside in 2015, is adjacent to a clothing store and a small shop that sells books,  
travel accessories, snacks, and beverages \(Figure 3.11\). The piece consists of two  
8.57-metre-tall blue glass-etched poles with carved cedar eagles balanced on their  
peaks \(YVR n.d.\). Like Steve Smith’s Freedom to Move, it was placed at a key  
intersection. The Rivers Monument is located immediately beyond the pre-boarding  
security and between the A-gates and B-gates. It too sits upon a podium and is sur-  
rounded by an oval-shaped concrete base with wood bench seating. The podium  
supporting Freedom to Move’s cedar panels has a slightly different shape \(see  
Plate 13\). It is in the shape of a boat’s hull and appears to float in a body of water  
encased by the raised concrete pool. The informational panel accompanying Free-  
dom to Move notes that it refers to how the region’s Northwest Coast First Nations  
people would travel by canoe and that “\[f\]or them, travel was as much about the  
journey as it was about the destination.” The panel explains that the artwork  
“encourages travellers to slow down, contemplate and acknowledge their surround-  
ings in order to experience a change of pace both physically and spiritually.” This  
recommendation for passengers to be aware of “their surroundings” is notable as  
the artwork is situated only a few metres away from an Absolute Spa as well as other  
shops and restaurants.  
Shielding the Passenger From the Spectacle\: Richard Serra’s  
Tilted Spheres  
Richard Serra’s Tilted Spheres at Toronto Pearson’s Terminal 1 is comparable to Time  
Tower as it is placed at the centre of a commercial area containing luxury retailers  
\(Figure 3.12\). The 4.35-metre-tall and 13.86-metre-long sculpture also draws passen-  
gers towards the shops, but its interior offers an escape from the sights and sounds of  
the surrounding commercial space. Like Reid’s The Jade Canoe at YVR, it is a flag-  
ship artwork by a well-known artist and commissioned during the building’s planning  
phase \(Milroy 2003\). The GTAA commissioned Serra’s work for a 84,500-square-  
metre expansion that opened in 2006 and services flights to international locations  
\(Mississauga News 2006\). The pier, which is a long finger extending from the main  
terminal building, is capped with a semi-circular “hammerhead” at its south end.  
Tilted Spheres is at the end of the finger in the centre of the hammerhead. Cranes 98 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
Figure 3.12 Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
Richard Serra, Tilted Spheres, Toronto Pearson International Airport, Canada, 2004.  
lowered the fifty-millimetre-thick metal plates onto the foundation before the ter-  
minal’s walls and ceiling were built \(Adams 2005\). When the hammerhead expan-  
sion was revealed to the public in 2006, Safdie championed Serra’s sculpture “for the  
sense of geometry and motion it creates” \(Mississauga News 2006, 1\). Indeed, Tilted  
Spheres mirrors Safdie’s architectural design for the terminal—the hammerhead’s ceil-  
ing, skylights, and south-facing glass-curtain wall have a related curvilinear design.  
Like The Jade Canoe, Tilted Spheres is integrated with the terminal’s architecture. In  
the former case, architects created an interior design in response to Reid’s sculpture.  
In the latter case, Serra’s work not only corresponds with the terminal’s interior design  
but also with the hammerhead’s footprint and external structure.  
All non-US-bound international flights depart from gates in the pier’s hammerhead—  
they are designated with the letter “E.” The F-gates, which are along the corridor  
leading to the hammerhead, only service US-bound flights. In addition, there are some  
US-bound flights that depart from the E-gates in the hammerhead. The pier is designed  
to separate passengers departing from the E- and F-gates. Those departing from the  
hammerhead are directed along a passageway a storey above the corridor that services  
the F-gates for US-bound flights. This passageway ends with stairs and escalators lead-  
ing down to the hammerhead’s central space. Passengers entering this area encounter  
a decision point with three possible routes\: a central trajectory that passes through or  
around Serra’s Tilted Spheres, a route to the left that passes through a duty-free store, Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce 99  
or one to the right that passes along a number of shops and through a food court.  
The duty-free store contains a serpentine path equivalent to those found in the walk-  
through shops at Stansted, Gatwick, and YVR. Passengers walking through Tilted  
Spheres also move along a curvilinear path if they choose to enter the narrow spaces  
between the work’s torqued metal plates. But, this serpentine pathway is devoid of the  
spectacular imagery of products, logos, advertisements, and shop displays.  
Serra’s Tilted Spheres replicates the other steel plate artworks for which he is well  
known. Variations of this type appear at numerous galleries across the world includ-  
ing the Seattle Art Museum’s Olympic Sculpture Park and the Guggenheim Museum  
in Bilbao. Though, this work is remarkable since it appears to operate in the same dis-  
ruptive spirit as his infamous Tilted Arc placed in Federal Plaza in New York in 1981.  
A number of the workers who frequented the square disliked the 36.5-metre-long  
curved wall because it obstructed their path and view, and they successfully lobbied  
to have it removed \(Deutsche 1992; Levine 2002\). Serra’s subsequent appeal and the  
work’s ultimate removal in 1989 generated a fervent controversy among artists and  
elicited discussions regarding copyright.  
In this respect, Serra’s Tilted Spheres is an odd choice for an air terminal. Usu-  
ally, planners do not select artworks that hinder one’s view of the terminal’s shops  
or signage. Instead, they install works like The Jade Canoe and Time Tower, which  
invite people to linger in spaces with clear views of the surrounding shops and restau-  
rants. While some passengers may be drawn to the shops that surround Serra’s Tilted  
Spheres, others may find a tranquil space within the work that shields them from the  
sensory experience that one typically encounters in the terminal’s consumer spaces.  
In addition, Tilted Spheres prevents travellers from seeing flight information screens,  
signs indicating gates, or a clear view of the apron. However, the skylights above are  
visible from within the work. As noted in the previous chapter, Safdie included these  
in the ceiling’s design to orient passengers in the terminal.  
Conclusion  
In many of today’s airports, air terminal artworks serve more than simply a decorative  
purpose. Some architects and planners expect these works to operate as landmarks  
for orientation and as material things that draw passengers towards consumer spaces.  
They consider these pieces in terms of their materiality, and they assume that these  
works operate as active agents that impact the travellers’ actions. The expectation that  
artworks can function as guides for wayfinding is most clearly expressed at Amster-  
dam’s Schiphol, Vancouver’s YVR, and Bangkok’s Suvarnabhumi. These airports  
include eye-catching pieces as icons on their terminals’ maps. Like the ceiling designs  
discussed in Chapter 2, these pieces are defined by a functional aesthetic with regard  
to their wayfinding purpose; these installations operate on a visual register, and they  
need to look fit for their function as conspicuous markers for navigation.  
My attention to artworks in this chapter also addressed how planners expect them  
to stimulate the passengers’ desire to buy food and goods. Even though we cannot  
confirm whether artworks actually perform this purpose, planners at airports such as  
Schiphol, YVR, Toronto Pearson, San Francisco International, and Los Angeles’s Tom  
Bradley International Terminal have installed artworks to create an appealing atmos-  
phere which they hope will encourage travellers to spend. Reid’s The Jade Canoe and  
Serra’s Tilted Spheres are striking examples of air terminal artworks since YVR’s and 100 Artworks for Wayfinding and Commerce  
Pearson’s architects designed the terminals in response to where these pieces would  
be placed in the interior. At both YVR and Pearson, architects surrounded these  
monumental sculptures with commercial operations. The theatre-like space adjacent  
to Reid’s The Jade Canoe offers people a place to sit, and their attention can move  
between the shimmering piece before them and the colourful shopfronts and restau-  
rant logos in the immediate vicinity. Serra’s Tilted Spheres offers a markedly different  
experience. Its interior is a space where passengers can briefly escape the surrounding  
consumer environment.  
This chapter has concentrated on how air terminal artworks are material objects  
that operate as landmarks for navigation or as things that encourage travellers to  
move towards or gather within consumer spaces. The following chapters examine air  
terminal artworks with regard to their representational and symbolic content and how  
this can affect passengers’ moods.  
Notes  
1\. NACO’s architects, as discussed in Chapter 1, aim to include clear sightlines of the apron  
when they are planning the air terminal’s layout. Like Mijksenaar’s designers, they believe  
that a view of aircraft on the tarmac helps with one’s orientation in the terminal.  
2\. Thompson, Berwick, and Pratt’s design for Vancouver’s terminal has changed remarkably  
since it began operating in 1968 \(McGrath 1992\). After the new International Terminal  
opened in 1996, the building became the airport’s Domestic Terminal.  
3\. Bangkok Suvarnabhumi’s terminal contains ca. 560,000 square metres of floor area,  
slightly less than the combined floor area of HKIA’s Terminal 1 and 2 \(Dixon 2007; Jahn  
et al. 2007\).  
4\. My translation from the original text in Dutch\: “Elk plein heeft een kunstwerk nodig, een  
ontmoetingsplek, een dorpspomp”; and “Dan moet commercie wijken voor functionaliteit.”  
5\. A Synergy of Dynamic Energy was suspended from the ceiling in the western side of the  
terminal where the concourse branches into its angled north and south wings.

18\. Schiphol has also installed a video work that functions as a clock. Maarten Baas’s Real  
Time, located in Lounge 2 on the airside, is a three-metre-wide cubic structure with a round  
video screen that depicts a clock with minute and hour hands. Like Time Tower, a human  
resides in the clock and is responsible for relaying the actual time. A diffused image of a  
worker is visible behind the clock’s translucent face. For each passing minute, the worker  
wipes away and repaints the clock’s minute hand. The hour hand is repainted on the hour.  
19\. This work resembles the types of dynamic architectural designs that act as an interface with  
users and transform in response to their actions \(Taylor 2018\).

# Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
The preceding chapter discussed why Gensler, the San Francisco Arts Commission, and  
airport staff decided to hang Janet Echelman’s Every Beating Second above the space  
immediately following the pre-boarding security area at San Francisco International’s  
Terminal 2 \(see Plate 19\). This space is named the ‘Recompose’ area, and Echelman’s  
work is intended to calm travellers before they carry onwards. This supports a pri-  
mary aim for air terminal designers since they believe that a relaxed and unstressed  
passenger is more likely to spend. This chapter considers how these types of artworks  
have the capacity to affect travellers’ moods. Its analysis differs from those in Chap-  
ters 2 and 3. The former chapters concentrate on how air terminal interior design and  
artworks operate as material things that mediate passengers’ actions; in this chapter,  
I examine how an artwork’s representational and symbolic content has the capacity to  
affect passenger behaviour. My investigation, however, does not account for all of the  
possible affective states an artwork can generate; I concentrate on the meanings and  
feelings that airport planners expect these works to stimulate within the passenger. My  
investigation of these intended affective states illustrates how planners try to create a  
specific ambiance within the terminal and why they believe that this leads to increased  
spending.  
My analysis of these artworks draws upon a number of theoretical texts concerning  
affect, including Peter Adey’s investigations of this topic with respect to air terminal  
design. I begin by outlining what affect is, and I examine artworks placed near security  
zones such as Every Beating Second to establish how art can operate on an affective  
register. I then turn my attention to works that convey themes of movement. In many  
cases, these artworks signify a relatively unrestricted type of motion that contrasts  
with the regulated movement that defines commercial flight. I consider how these art-  
works are able to affect passengers and elicit kinaesthesia, the sensation of movement,  
and whether they are installed for functional purposes.  
Richard Wilson’s Slipstream at Heathrow Terminal 2 depicts the trajectory of a  
small stunt plane, and it clearly stimulates ideas of kinaesthesia and flight \(Figure  
I.3\). I argue that its capacity to induce bodily sensations of movement is a type of  
aesthetic. In company with Susan Buck-Morss, I am looking to the Ancient Greek defi-  
nition of aisth*ē*tikos, which means “perceptive by feeling” \(cited in Kester 1997, 43\).  
This conception, which situates aesthetic experiences on the same register as physi-  
ological sensations, has informed other theories such as Edmund Burke’s writing on  
the sublime \(Ryan 2001\). This type of aesthetic differs from the functional aesthetic  
explored in the last two chapters which concerns a cognitive understanding of the air  
terminal’s operation. It concerns a proximal sense—kinaesthesia, the bodily pleasure Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 105  
of movement. Artworks that stimulate this aesthetic experience potentially contribute  
to the airport’s function; some planners expect that movement-themed installations  
will buoy the passenger’s mood which, as noted, correlates with an increased desire  
to spend.  
While I primarily discuss static artworks that represent themes of movement, I also  
examine a few kinetic artworks. Their moving components, however, do not turn,  
sway, or swing in an uncontrolled fashion; instead, they are often synchronized to cre-  
ate harmonious flowing patterns. I contend that these works similarly evoke a sense  
of kinaesthesia that contrasts with the actual movement experienced in the terminal.  
Most air terminal artworks divert the traveller’s attention away from how the air-  
port is filtering and processing them into highly ordered flows. Nevertheless, there are  
a few exceptions. This chapter’s last section considers a few artworks that point to  
the technologies and processes used to facilitate passenger movement. It concentrates  
on a series of artworks installed at Schiphol in 1995 that critically reflect upon the  
experiential aspects of moving through the terminal. I argue that these works not only  
deviate from the usual types of works installed in airports but that they also critique  
the functions that air terminal installations are expected to serve. When viewed in  
contrast to the array of airport artworks, Dennis Adams’s Coda, Jenny Holzer’s Unti-  
tled, Niek Kemps’s Closed View, and Marc Ruygrok’s So go on offer a critical inter-  
pretation of why artworks are installed in the terminal and how they are expected to  
contribute to the airport’s operations.  
What Is Affect?  
The last chapter examined how artworks such as Bill Reid’s Spirit of Haida Gwaii\:  
The Jade Canoe and Dennis Adams’s Coda operate as material things that encourage  
people to move towards them or notice them as eye-catching landmarks \(see Plates 1  
and 6\). This chapter and the following chapter concentrate on what these pieces rep-  
resent and how planners believe that these works’ meanings will affect passengers’  
moods. I rely on theories of affect to consider how these works can evoke particular  
feelings.  
Affect is an elusive concept and difficult to define \(Gregg and Seigworth 2010\). In a  
general sense, the term connotes emotions such as shame, fear, joy, happiness, anger,  
sadness, and jealousy. Nigel Thrift defines affect as a “sense of a push in the world,”  
and he theorizes how urban spaces are designed to operate on an affective register  
\(2004, 64\). His notion of affect is informed by Baruch Spinoza’s affectus and Brian  
Massumi’s \(2002\) theoretical investigation into this concept.1 Spinoza’s monist philos-  
ophy was a reaction to René Descartes’s dualistic philosophy that separated the body,  
part of physical and geometrically defined space, from the mind, which was defined  
by conscious thought. Spinoza postulated that human beings and all objects in the  
universe were composed of the same substance. Unlike the Cartesian mind divorced  
from corporeality, the thinking being for Spinoza is embedded within the materiality  
of the body. Spinoza’s monist definition of affect argues that it is an interaction which  
causes the body and the mind to act.  
Gilles Deleuze’s interpretation of Spinoza’s affectus as a connection that flows  
within and between human and non-human bodies informs Thrift’s theory as well as  
other “affectual geographies” \(Anderson 2006; McCormack 2008; Pile 2010\). Draw-  
ing largely from Massumi’s interpretation of Deleuze’s thoughts on Spinoza, Anderson 106 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
notes that affect is a “transpersonal capacity” that allows a body to be affected and to  
affect other bodies \(2006, 735; italics in original\). This capacity is defined by a move-  
ment situated between entities. Feelings are a corporeal experience of this movement;  
they are a response to flows of affect and are expressed as “proprioceptive and visceral  
shifts” in the body \(Anderson 2006, 736\). Emotions are a personal and subjective  
response to feelings and defined by an individual’s social context. Linguistic expres-  
sions and conscious thoughts are part of the emotional register, while feelings are the  
corporeal expressions such as an increase in bodily heat and the skin’s flushing. My  
analysis considers an idea of affect that accounts for multiple experiences in the termi-  
nal’s spaces. For instance, it considers how material designs that incorporate flowing  
water can calm passengers as well as how aviation and movement-themed imagery can  
generate sensations of kinaesthesia.  
‘Affectual geographies,’ like Thrift’s spatial politics of affect, have been criticized  
as universalizing and deterministic theories that do not account for difference and  
diversity \(Tolia-Kelly 2006\). These criticisms partly result from a notion of affect that  
is synonymous with feeling and emotion. Divya P. Tolia-Kelly charges that “affectual  
geographies” define emotion as “intrinsically embedded in universalist thought rather  
than the geopolitical landscape that constitutes our universal political life” \(2006,  
213\). Her criticism is significant because emotion defined as a “trans-personal” push,  
which is experienced equivocally by all individuals, universalizes how every person  
will respond to a particular stimulus.2 Thrift’s \(2004\) article, which she critiques, uses  
affect, emotion, and feeling interchangeably, and it is difficult to determine how and  
on which register they are operating.3 This is not the case in other geographers’ con-  
siderations of the topic. For instance, Anderson \(2006\) clarifies the difference between  
these three terms.4 He reiterates that emotion is subjective, and he adds that affect  
does not directly determine bodily expressions\: “The relationship between the circula-  
tion and distribution of affects and signification is not, therefore, one of conformity or  
correspondence, but one of resonation or interference” \(Anderson 2009, 9\).  
My analysis does not conflate affect with feeling and emotion. It relies on Ander-  
son’s more strict interpretation of affect\: affect is the capacity to affect or be affected;  
feeling is on a separate register defined by bodily fluctuations that are reacting to the  
flows of affect; and emotion is the conscious expression of feelings. I argue that an  
air terminal artwork’s capacity to affect a viewer is a universal quality, but I am not  
arguing that an artwork has the capacity to provoke the same feelings and emotions  
for every traveller. Nor am I arguing that an installation will repeatedly spur the same  
feelings and emotions each time an individual is affected by the work in question. For  
example, Echelman’s Every Beating Second has the capacity to affect all passengers,  
but the feelings and emotions felt while viewing this installation is different for each  
passenger, and these can change for each passenger over time \(see Plate 19\). We can  
ascertain the variation of these responses based on a series of interviews with travellers  
shortly after the piece was unveiled.5 For some, the suspended work gives a sense of  
freedom or being uplifted. For others, it calls to mind San Francisco’s reputation as a  
welcoming and diverse community.  
Even though artworks have the potential to evoke a range of affective responses,  
my analysis in this chapter and the following chapter concentrates largely on the types  
of feelings and meanings that airport planners expect air terminal artworks to evoke  
among the passengers that view them. Indeed, this does not point to the actual effect  
on travellers; but it does address the planners’ and architects’ rationale for installing Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 107  
these types of works and why they believe that these installations will affect passen-  
gers’ behaviour.  
Affecting Passengers’ Moods With Air Terminal Design  
Adey \(2008\) theorizes how spatial orderings and materials in air terminals might impact  
travellers on a pre-cognitive affective register.6 He investigates how some designs,  
which are based on predicted pre-cognitive responses, are intended to trigger specific  
affects. This does not imply that passengers’ behaviour is determined by airport archi-  
tecture; a terminal’s design affects passengers in variable ways and inspires a multitude  
of contingent responses. He illustrates how planners design commercial spaces with  
the intent of spurring feelings and emotions of joy and excitement. In his study of air-  
port architecture and passenger behaviour conducted during 2003 and 2004, he found  
that airport authorities were trying to increase retail spending to offset profit losses  
due to changes in ownership and a surge in low-cost airlines. Drawing from numer-  
ous studies of consumer behaviour, planners were designing terminal spaces with the  
intent to reduce anxiety and induce feelings associated with higher spending. These  
strategies also included trying to entice passengers to move towards shopping spaces.  
In one airport, which Adey does not name, designers thought that a limestone-tile  
floor’s reflected ambient light would have a positive effect on travellers and encourage  
them to wander past the shops and restaurants. The shopping areas contrast with the  
security spaces. They tend to have lower ceilings, fewer windows, and the paint and  
carpets are usually less colourful. These drab interiors and closed spaces are intended  
to prompt melancholic emotions and to evoke a sense of being controlled—a feeling  
reinforced by the security barriers that limit passengers’ movements.  
As noted, Every Beating Second is intended to improve passengers’ moods once  
they exit the pre-boarding security. The Transportation Security Administration \(TSA\)  
screening zone that precedes the Recompose area with Echelman’s work is similar to  
those described in Adey’s \(2008\) analysis; it has a low ceiling and subdued colour-  
ing. Like Every Beating Second, Daan Roosegaarde’s Beyond at Amsterdam Airport  
Schiphol is adjacent to a pre-boarding screening area \(Figure 4.1\). The one-hundred-  
and-twelve-metre wall panel, installed in Departure Hall 3 in 2016, runs along a corridor  
and two escalators that lead to the screening zone upstairs. Only those with a board-  
ing pass can enter this space. The installation, which Roosegaarde modelled after Dutch  
seventeenth-century landscape paintings, projects a shimmering skyscape with scattered  
clouds that seem to subtly move and change shape \(Studio Roosegaarde n.d.\). The ten-  
centimetre-thick wall contains LED lights and printed lenses that render an illusion where  
the sky has depth, and it appears as though the clouds move as one walks past the work.  
Beyond offers some visual stimulus in what would have otherwise been a bland corridor,  
and its content elicits a sense that one is drifting through the skies above.  
Like Beyond, Ingo Maurer’s Earthbound . . . Unbound at Toronto Pearson’s Termi-  
nal 1 conveys a sense of floating in a peaceful space which contrasts with the surround-  
ing busy environment \(see Plate 22\). This aquarium, containing transparent cubes that  
swirl and glide through blue water, was intended to alleviate passenger anxiety when  
the Greater Toronto Airports Authority installed it in 2004. They placed it next to  
the queue for the E-gates’ pre-boarding security. At the time of the terminal’s open-  
ing, the airport authority’s general manager of design, Sam Dewairy, noted\: “Ingo’s  
idea is to give peace and tranquility to the passengers as they rush about” \(cited in 108 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
Figure 4.1 Daan Roosegaarde, Beyond, Amsterdam Airport Schiphol, Netherlands, 2016.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
Hiller 2004, 4\). Like the seven other works commissioned for the new Moshe Safdie-  
designed terminal, Maurer’s work is intended to convey “the essence of flight in a con-  
ceptual, abstract or realistic manner” \(Greater Toronto Airports Authority n.d., 1\).7  
While the slowly swirling transparent cubes loosely recall aerial imagery such as hot  
air balloons in the sky, one cannot mistake them for anything but objects being gently  
pushed around by the water’s currents.  
Water is generally considered an appealing feature in natural and built environments  
\(R. Kaplan and S. Kaplan 1989\). Placing an installation with water next to a security  
zone is likely an effective strategy to de-stress passengers since it is known to stimulate  
a positive emotional response \(White et al. 2010\). The Musqueam Welcome Area in  
Vancouver’s International Terminal, which travellers encounter on their way to the  
border screening area, includes rushing water that runs down the face of a granite  
wall and along two channels beside a set of stairs and escalators \(Figure 4.2\). Unlike  
the fluid in Maurer’s aquarium, this installation’s moving water is sensed visually as  
well as aurally. Passengers see and hear the rushing water as they walk towards the  
screening area. The following chapter examines this installation’s Northwest Coast  
First Nations artworks and their political significance with regard to their location in  
the terminal.  
At Dubai International’s Terminal 3, planners have installed a similar type of water  
wall adjacent to its security zones \(Figure 4.3\). Arriving passengers encounter the wall Figure 4.2 Susan A. Point, Krista Point, Robyn Sparrow, Debra Sparrow, Gina Grant, and  
Helen Callbreath, Musqueam Welcome Area, Vancouver International Airport,  
Canada, 1996.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). Figure 4.3 Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
Paul Andreu, Terminal 3, Dubai International Airport, United Arab Emirates, 2008. Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 111  
en route to the border screening area, and departing passengers pass by the work  
upon exiting the pre-boarding security. This extensive water wall and fountain is situ-  
ated behind a set of elevators with an unclad support structure that does not block  
the pacifying sound of the falling water. The space in front of the elevators on the  
lowest level is divided by a glass wall in order to separate the arriving and departing  
passenger flows. Arriving international travellers must descend to the lowest level to  
pass through passport control and customs. Departing travellers enter the space on  
the lowest level after having cleared security. The elevators and an adjacent escalator  
lead to the Terminal’s B- and C-gates. Passengers must take a train from here to access  
the A-gates.  
Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Movement-Themed Artworks  
Adey \(2008\) argues that notions of affect need to consider mobility since feelings and  
emotions both impact and are impacted by bodily movements. He relies on Massu-  
mi’s argument that movement and feeling are inseparable and that moving bodies are  
always feeling, and feeling bodies are always moving. Thrift \(2007\) also notes that  
theorists writing about affect need to consider how bodily movements affect emo-  
tions and feelings. Adey illustrates that this topic is relevant to contemporary design;  
airport architects account for this intertwined nature of affect and mobility, and they  
use it as a strategy to encourage specific responses to the space. Like Adey’s analysis,  
my investigation assumes that passengers’ feelings and behaviours are affected by the  
way they move through these carefully designed interiors. This assumption pertains to  
the functional aesthetic that I explored in the last two chapters, which considered how  
air terminal design organizes passenger traffic. For instance, a glass-walled and high-  
ceilinged open concourse that gives a clear sense of directional flow causes less anxiety  
than a terminal consisting of low-ceilinged artificially lit corridors that feel like a maze.  
In the preceding section, I discussed how planners place artworks near security  
zones to calm travellers’ nerves. In this respect, my analysis of these artworks follows  
a similar tack to Adey’s \(2008\) consideration of how air terminals are designed to  
elicit specific affects. My assessment of movement-themed artworks, however, departs  
from his theoretical considerations of affect and mobility in the terminal since I con-  
sider an inverse relationship between affect and movement. Moreover, my analysis  
of artworks that convey movement examines how they induce kinaesthetic sensa-  
tions. Rather than considering how passengers are affected by the regulation of their  
movement in the terminal, I investigate how an artwork’s representational content can  
stimulate sensations of movement.  
My analysis of this affective response concentrates on artworks such as Jonathan  
Borofsky’s I Dreamed I Could Fly \(Figure 4.4\).8 The work’s five figures were sus-  
pended below a large round skylight in the ceiling at Toronto Pearson’s Terminal  
1 when it opened in 2004. Sadly, it has been removed. Each figure was made of  
aluminium and Lexan, a translucent polymer, and was differently coloured\: green,  
yellow, red, orange, and blue. Their arms and legs were outstretched, and these  
figures gave the impression that they could either fly through the oculus above or  
swoop through the terminal’s vast spaces. Like Echelman’s Every Beating Second,  
Roosegaarde’s Beyond, and Maurer’s Earthbound . . . Unbound, this work was  
situated next to a screening area. Passengers departing from the terminal’s D-gates  
encountered I Dreamed I Could Fly upon exiting the pre-boarding security zone. Figure 4.4 Jonathan Borofsky, I Dreamed I Could Fly, Toronto Pearson International Airport,  
Canada, 2003.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\). Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 113  
After passing through a zigzagging pathway formed by rows of retractable cor-  
don barriers and having security personnel check their travel documents, bodies,  
and luggage, travellers were directed to escalators where they had an unobstructed  
view of Borofsky’s five figures flying freely in the concourse’s expansive space. This  
.  
representation of unrestricted movement clearly contrasts with their experience of  
passing through the screening area. It also differs markedly from the experience of  
flying commercially—passengers typically spend their time aboard an airliner con-  
fined and buckled into their allotted seat.  
9  
I argue that these types of movement-themed works operate on an affective register  
to elicit sensations of kinaesthesia. I draw upon the aforementioned definitions of  
affect as well as Sigmund Freud’s conception of the term. With respect to psycho-  
therapy, he describes affect as an electric charge that covers the surface of a memory.  
Freud writes,  
the concept that in mental functions something is to be distinguished—a quota of  
affect or sum of excitation—which possesses all the characteristics of a quantity  
\(though we have no means of measuring it\), which is capable of increase, diminu-  
tion, displacement, and discharge, and which is spread over the memory-traces of  
ideas somewhat as an electric charge is spread over the surface of a body.  
\(cited in Green 1999, 15\)10  
This definition illustrates how a recalled experience of movement can elicit sensations  
associated with the actual movement. For example, a recollection of free-falling when  
jumping from a diving board is loaded with a type of energy that is comparable to what  
is felt during the actual experience of this kinaesthesia. This type of electric charge also  
accompanies imagined experiences of movement such as flight simulation video games  
played from the pilot’s point of view. This affective quality of virtual kinaesthesia is per-  
haps most amplified during dreams of flight—an experience that Borofsky recreates in  
I Dreamed I Could Fly. Indeed, this installation as well as other movement-themed art-  
works draw our attention to our experiences of kinaesthesia, whether real or imagined,  
and bring forth a “sum of excitation” associated with these experiences.  
Aerial-Themed Imagery\: Signifying Flight and Eliciting Kinaesthesia  
I Dreamed I Could Fly conveys a human experience of flight without the aid of wings  
or jet turbines. It is an exception among aerial-themed air terminal artworks since  
most convey non-human things such as winged aircraft and birds. For instance, Ber-  
nard Séguin-Poirier’s Si j’avais des ailes \(If I had Wings\), installed at Montreal’s Pierre  
Elliott Trudeau International in 2008, is a mobile in the shape of an airplane con-  
structed out of painted panels and thin metal tubing \(Figure 4.5\).11 It is suspended in  
the pre-security departures area adjacent to the check-in counters.  
My aforementioned assessment of I Dreamed I Could Fly considered how a direct  
representation of human movement arouses sensations of kinaesthesia. How can a  
depiction of an aircraft such as Si j’avais des ailes \(If I had Wings\) instigate the same  
type of response? I contend that a theoretical framework based on Charles Sand-  
ers Peirce’s semiotics offers a way of understanding how representations of flying  
machines can stir sensations of movement. Unlike Ferdinand de Saussure’s semiol-  
ogy, Peirce’s semiotics transcends the rigid conception of spirit and matter, ideas and 114 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
Figure 4.5 Bernard Séguin-Poirier, Si j’avais des ailes \(If I had Wings\), Montreal-Pierre Elliott  
Trudeau International Airport, Canada, 2008.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2017\).  
things, and subject and object as separate entities occupying two unbridgeable spheres  
\(Gottdiener 1995; Keane 2005\).12 Even though post-structuralists such as Jacques  
Derrida, Jean Baudrillard, and Roland Barthes have deconstructed many of the under-  
lying assumptions of Saussure’s structuralism, these theorists have all maintained this Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 115
