dualistic split and retained the sign within the realm of ideas and outside of matter.  
Peirce’s pragmatic theory addresses how the actual object a sign refers to affects the  
sign’s overall meaning. Saussure’s notion of the sign, consisting of a signifier and a sig-  
nified, pertains to the vehicle that transmits the sign \(the signifier\) and the concept it  
prompts in the mind of the receiver \(the signified\). Peirce, on the other hand, analyzes  
a sign in terms of\: the “representamen,” the means by which the sign is conveyed; the  
“interpretant,” the idea that the receiver uses cognitively to understand the sign; and  
the actual material object the sign is referring to. While Saussure was primarily con-  
cerned with how the relations and differences between signs informed the structures of  
systems of signification, Peirce sought to examine the logic of sign systems—namely,  
how language transmits information, and its possibilities for conveying truth claims.  
Peirce’s semiotics classifies signs into a number of categories depending on the  
characteristics of the object, representamen, and intepretant \(Gottdiener 1995; Atkin  
2010\). My analysis considers only one of his triadic classifications of signs\: the sym-  
bol, the icon, and the index. Peirce notes that the first is “a sign which would lose the  
character which renders it a sign if there was no interpretant” \(cited in Gottdiener  
1995, 12\). A symbol’s meaning, such as the written word ‘cat,’ is determined by cul-  
ture and social relations, and this meaning can only be ascertained by individuals with  
access to this cultural and social knowledge. An icon resembles the object it repre-  
sents. The spoken word ‘meow’ is an iconic representation of the sound of a cat, and  
an image of a lit cigarette with a red circle inscribed around it and a red straight line  
drawn through the diameter of the circle and overtop of the lit cigarette is an icon that  
transmits ‘no smoking permitted here.’ An index is a sign that is not determined by a  
convention shared by a particular social group but by the interpreter’s own experience  
of the world, and it points to a causal connection between the representamen and the  
object.13 As an index, a lightning flash is an indication of thunder and an impending  
storm, and smoke is an indication of fire and something burning. For Peirce, these  
three classifications are abstractions, and some signs cannot be strictly differentiated  
along these terms but are iconic, indexical, or symbolic to varying degrees. Further-  
more, the same sign can be interpreted as an index, an icon, or a symbol depending on  
the context and the intentions of the receiver interpreting the sign.  
In Webb Keane’s \(2005\) analysis of how materiality is intrinsic to systems of signifi-  
cation, he addresses how an iconic sign such as the quality ‘red’ cannot be experienced  
simply on its own but through its embodiment in an actual material thing. An inter-  
preter experiences redness along with a number of other contingent qualities which  
are “bundled” with the interpreter’s experience of the colour\: “redness in an apple  
comes along with spherical shape, light weight, sweet flavor, a tendency to rot, and so  
forth” \(Keane 2005, 188\).  
As a sign Séguin-Poirier’s Si j’avais des ailes \(If I had Wings\) is an icon signifying an  
aircraft, which as an actual flying machine is an index that points to the interpreter’s  
experience of flight and movement \(Figure 4.5\). Following Keane’s \(2005\) argument,  
the interpreter’s acknowledgement of its resemblance to a flying machine is bundled  
with a number of other contingent qualities. As an icon of an aircraft, Si j’avais des  
ailes \(If I had Wings\) may elicit the bundled qualities of watching a real airplane in  
flight. This visual experience of a flying thing’s movement conveys the quality ‘move-  
ment,’ which, like the quality ‘redness,’ is bundled with a number of other qualities  
that are contingent and dependent on each individual’s own kinaesthetic experiences.  
This may include embodied experiences of flight itself, other motion machines such  
as automobiles, boats, and bicycles, or bodily movements such as running, dancing, 116 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
and swimming. The sign’s capacity to transmit the quality ‘movement’ to the receiver  
occurs on an affective register; it is essentially a current of affects between two bod-  
ies. In short, an icon of an aircraft has the capacity to affect the viewer, and it gener-  
ates sensations of movement that are unique and particular to the viewer affected by  
this icon.  
Air terminal artworks that refer to airplanes are often similar to Séguin-Poirier’s  
Si j’avais des ailes \(If I had Wings\)—they are constructed out of lightweight materi-  
als and strung from the ceiling. When Kansai International Airport opened in 1994,  
planners suspended Susumu Shingu’s Boundless Sky in the concourse. It consists of  
multiple sculptures constructed out of colourful cloths and lightweight armatures, and  
they recall the shape of flying machines \(Buchanan 1994\). It parallels Michel Santry’s  
aerial installation which hung from HKIA Terminal 1’s ceiling from 1998 until 2003.  
It comprised of three separate installations and was constructed out of stainless steel  
tubing, painted steel tubing, thin colourful cloths, and rigging for yachts \(Xinhua  
News Agency 1997\). Santry maintained that his installation, which resembled winged  
craft and sails, was a place-themed reference as well since it referred to the airplanes at  
HKIA and the boats in Hong Kong’s harbour \(Michel Santry Sculptor n.d.\).14 Robert  
Charles Coyle’s Flight Song, discussed in Chapter 3, is also a flight-themed artwork  
suspended in the concourse and is made of thin plastic \(Figure 3.6\). These installa-  
tions, which all hang from the ceiling and employ lightweight materials, remind the  
viewer that aeronautical technology has allowed us to move through airspace and  
overcome gravitational forces, a barrier that otherwise restricts us from leaving the  
ground. Yi-Chih Lai’s Multiple Dimension, installed at Taipei Taoyuan Terminal 2  
in 2011, is an aviation-themed work suspended over the landside departures area  
\(Figure 4.6\). It, however, consists of voluminous forms and is more massive than the  
lightweight works described previously. The largest of the series, which measures 13.2  
metres long, 11.3 metres wide, and 4.7 metres high, hangs above the airline check-  
in counters. It too refers to advancements in aviation design—it consists of propel-  
lers attached to an assemblage of bulbous forms that recall an aircraft’s aerodynamic  
styling.15  
Some air terminals hang models of actual aircraft from their ceilings. At HKIA  
Terminal 1, a replica 1910 Farman biplane is suspended in the landside area of the  
terminal \(Figure 4.7\).16 Arriving passengers exiting the airside on the south side of  
the terminal can see the biplane as they enter the space where meeters and greet-  
ers are waiting. Departing passengers entering the terminal on the south side can  
view the plane up close as it hangs between two of the sets of angled ramps that  
lead to the check-in area. For some passengers, the biplane may foster a romantic  
notion of the early days of flight when aviation was characterized by innovation,  
adventure, and far less regulation than air travel today. A plaque on the departures  
level explains that the first aircraft to take flight in Hong Kong was a Farman  
biplane that Belgian aviator Charles van den Born flew on March 18, 1911, at Sha  
Tin. It states that this replica, which is a fully functional aircraft, flew at HKIA on  
November 15, 1997, and that\: “The flight and this display are lasting tributes to  
the birth of aviation in Hong Kong and the pioneering spirit that built our avia-  
tion industry.”17 Unlike the aforementioned aerial-themed artworks, the Farman  
biplane is not an icon of an aircraft but an actual flying machine. Therefore, with  
regard to Peirce’s semiotics, it is an index that points to embodied experiences of  
flight. Figure 4.6 Yi-Chih Lai, Multiple Dimension, Taipei Taoyuan International Airport, Taiwan, 2011.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
Figure 4.7 Roger Freeman, 1910 Farman Biplane Replica, Hong Kong International Airport,  
Hong Kong, 1997.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2014\). 118 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
Robert Byers’s Da Vinci Flying Machine is another full-scale model of an aircraft.  
It hung above the baggage claim in the arrivals area of Vancouver’s International Ter-  
minal until it was removed a few years ago \(O’Neill 1999; SkyTalk 1999\). Like the  
Farman biplane, this reproduction of Leonardo da Vinci’s design is a flying machine  
that is an index signifying kinaesthesia. Byers’s installation replicates da Vinci’s plans  
for a set of wings that act as an extension of the human body, where each individual  
pilots their own craft. If this rudimentary flying machine were to function as da Vinci  
had imagined, then this machine would allow the aviator to soar and glide in any  
direction in airspace.  
As installations situated in an international air terminal, replicas of aircraft and art-  
works resembling airplanes may recall the bodily sensations that air travellers experi-  
ence while flying. They experience their flight in the cabin of a jet aircraft and spend  
much of the time securely fastened by a seatbelt to their allotted seat by the window, the  
aisle, or between two other passengers. This relative immobility, however, is accompa-  
nied by feelings of movement—the seat presses against one’s back and the seatbelt pulls  
against one’s waist when the jet accelerates, decelerates, and encounters turbulence.18  
The view from the aircraft window accompanies these kinaesthetic sensations with a  
visual reference of the jet’s movement relative to the ground and the clouds in the sky.19  
For some passengers, particularly the ‘ideal’ passengers in the eyes of the air transporta-  
tion industry, this kinaesthesia—the push felt as the jet takes off and the pull of the jet  
as it drops in an area of turbulence—literally is the pleasure of movement. Though for  
many others, those who experience motion sickness or are anxious about flying, the  
experience of these movements is undoubtedly bundled with negative qualities.  
The Farman biplane replica and some these artworks hardly resemble today’s com-  
mercial aircraft or recall the types of flight paths taken by these passenger jets. For  
instance, van den Born’s first flight in Hong Kong contrasts markedly with the numer-  
ous flights arriving and departing in the highly organized airspace and massive airport  
complex at HKIA.20 Shingu’s Boundless Sky and Santry’s aerial installation, made  
from lightweight tubing and colourful cloths, resemble less so jet aircraft and more so  
the materials used to construct a hang glider. Like the Farman biplane, a hang glider  
usually carries one person, its pilot, and it enables this aviator to fly a relatively unre-  
strained flight path limited only by weather conditions, air currents, and the topogra-  
phy below. Perhaps for some passengers these installations do not elicit the embodied  
experiences of flying commercially but evoke sensations of kinaesthesia associated  
with the modes of flying represented in these works. Modes of flying that involve far  
less restricted movements than the controlled mobilities experienced by passengers  
transiting through the terminal and flying aboard today’s commercial aircraft.  
At Heathrow Terminal 2—reopened in June 2014 after being demolished and  
rebuilt—designers also installed a flight-themed artwork that points to types of move-  
ments freer than those typically experienced while flying commercially. Richard Wil-  
son’s Slipstream is situated in a large covered space between the terminal and the  
parking garage \(Figure I.3\). This seventy-eight-metre-long sculpture of twisted forms  
clad in aluminium is attached to four columns that support the entranceway’s ceiling  
\(O. Wainwright 2014; J. Wainwright 2014\). A set of escalators is next to the artwork.  
They lead from the ground level to the terminal’s arrivals level and departures level.  
Passengers are able to view Slipstream from a number of different angles as they move  
from level to level on the escalators and on the raised walkways that access the park-  
ing garage, the escalators, and the terminal. Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 119  
While the Farman biplane at HKIA recalls the early days of flight, Slipstream points  
to today’s aircraft due to the sculpture’s riveted aluminium panels.21 Furthermore, Wil-  
son modelled the artwork after a contemporary aircraft’s supposed trajectory; he notes\:  
“Everything leaves an invisible trail and I wanted to make that tangible, expressing the  
velocity and acceleration of flight” \(cited in O. Wainwright 2014, 16\). Yet, Slipstream is  
not a rendition of the smooth flight path of contemporary passenger aircraft such as the  
Airbus A380 or the Boeing 787. Instead, it is a recreation of a wild trajectory taken by  
a Zivko Edge 540 stunt plane—a small agile aircraft capable of aerobatic manoeuvres  
such as tight twisting turns, spins, steep dives, rolls, and loop the loops.  
In Oliver Wainwright’s \(2014\) review of the work in The Guardian, he notes that  
Slipstream is markedly different from Wilson’s Butterfly, an earlier artwork that also  
pertains to aircraft. Butterfly is a Cessna that was crumpled into a small ball and then  
gradually bent back into its initial shape, leaving an aircraft that is battered, bent, and  
beyond repair \(Sotiriadi 2003\). In comparison to Butterfly, “the sculpted twists of  
Slipstream seem almost too well-behaved” \(O. Wainwright 2014, 16\). What’s more,  
Wilson admitted that he wanted to create a work that would not “upset the passen-  
gers” \(cited in O. Wainwright 2014, 16\).  
Even if Wilson had wanted to create a work that resembles Butterfly, it seems unlikely  
that Heathrow’s planners would have installed it in Terminal 2 since Butterfly looks  
like a crashed aircraft pieced back together by airplane accident investigators. While  
Slipstream exemplifies an unrestricted and pleasurable form of kinaesthesia, Butterfly  
is the inverse of this aesthetic. It represents a type of movement that is violently inter-  
rupted, and it undoubtedly operates affectively to prompt emotions of fear and terror  
in passengers—even those with the utmost confidence in aviation technology.  
In a press release for Slipstream, Heathrow’s Development Director, John Holland-  
Kaye, explains that the airport’s management anticipates that passengers transiting  
through the terminal will “enjoy” the artwork \(cited in Heathrow Airport Media Centre  
2014\). The press release includes Wilson’s comments on Slipstream’s capacity to induce  
kinaesthesia\: “Sensations of velocity, acceleration and deceleration follow us at every  
undulation of the form” \(cited in Heathrow Airport Media Centre 2014\). Heathrow  
Airport’s decision to install an artwork that implies a wild airplane ride runs counter to  
the design strategies used by advertisers in the early days of commercial flight. Airlines  
tried to convey an image of a smooth trajectory—flying aboard a passenger aircraft was  
depicted as a comfortable experience, akin to that of travelling in a luxury railcar cabin  
\(Dierikx and Bouwens 1997\). The actual experience of these flights included little com-  
fort. Those who flew aboard the early commercial aircraft, which were typically refur-  
bished warplanes, experienced loud engine noises, noxious fumes, and often airsickness.  
Another artwork that points to a sense of free movement is Carel Visser’s Flying Fish;  
although unlike the works above, it combines both air and nautical themes \(see Plate 23\).  
Schiphol installed the massive sculpture made of uncoloured, red, and black metal panels  
in the Departures 3 area in 1993. It is suspended over a space where passengers queue  
in line for the airlines’ check-in counters. The evocation of movement was part of Vis-  
ser’s intent for the installation as he sought to create an air terminal artwork that was  
“directly associated with an element that ‘glides, hovers or swims’” \(Schiphol Group n.d.,  
n.p.\). In its description of the work, Schiphol Group notes that Flying Fish, “in a poetical  
way, makes a fascinating connection with aviation” \(n.d., n.p.\). Cedric Le Borgne’s Les  
Oiseaux \(The Birds\), installed at Singapore Changi’s Terminal 4 when it opened in 2017,  
is a more realistic depiction of animals with the capacity to fly \(Figure 4.8\). The work 120 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
Figure 4.8 Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
Cedric Le Borgne, Les Oiseaux \(The Birds\), Singapore Changi Airport, Singapore, 2017.  
consists of three birds constructed out of a stainless steel mesh, and they are next to  
the floor-to-ceiling windows that face the access road. Two are suspended from the  
ceiling and appear to be in mid-flight. They hover over a space between two bridges  
that connect the upper departures level to entrances for those arriving by automobile. Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 121  
The other is perched directly below on the ground level in a garden adjacent to the  
arrivals area and doors that exit to the lower roadway.  
The works described in this section convey types of unrestricted movement. Most  
of these pieces have another quality in common—they do not include passengers.  
Slipstream’s Zivko Edge 540 stunt plane and Da Vinci Flying Machine only allow for  
one crew member, the pilot. Flying Fish and Les Oiseaux \(The Birds\) point to moving  
things, animals, who are the masters of their own movements. Although HKIA’s Far-  
man biplane has the capacity to carry an extra passenger, it is only occupied by one  
dummy, which is at the controls of the aircraft. It is unclear whether Shingu’s Bound-  
less Sky or Santry’s aerial installation point to a flying machine with only the pilot on  
board. However, these sculptures’ materials resemble those of a hang glider, which  
often only has the pilot strapped to the apparatus; though, the pilot may choose to  
bring a passenger in a second harness. These artworks point to a type of movement  
that is freer than those typically experienced by passengers, and they also counter  
the aspect that truly defines what a passenger is. Unlike the pilot, the driver, and the  
steamship captain who are actively controlling their movement-machines, the passen-  
ger is passive in terms of the vessel’s navigation and is being transported at the hands  
of another person or organization. This quality of being transported by another agent  
also applies to the passengers’ experience in the terminal and the functional aesthetic  
outlined in Chapter 2. Even though they have some agency over their movements, pas-  
sengers are directed to move in a directional flow and are led through the building by  
the way of interior design and signage.  
What Purpose Do These Representations of Movement  
Serve in the Terminal?  
The Farman biplane, I Dreamed I Could Fly, Slipstream, and Les Oiseaux are illus-  
trative examples of air terminal installations that evoke sensations of relatively unre-  
stricted movement. The Farman biplane and Slipstream convey a form of aeromobility  
that is far less restrained than the stop-and-go movements experienced by the masses  
of passengers who fly commercially and transit through major airport hubs such as  
HKIA and Heathrow. Like all air terminal artworks, these pieces are installed in an  
interior that operates as a consumer space. As noted in Chapter 3, planners deploy  
place-themed artworks such as the Indigenous installations at Vancouver’s YVR in  
order to entice passenger spending. Do artworks that represent unrestricted move-  
ment serve an equivalent purpose in the terminal?  
Artworks that convey themes of unconstrained movement parallel a related type of  
aesthetic that is used in the automotive industry for commercial profit. Automobile  
advertisements often refer to less restricted modes of mobility than those usually expe-  
rienced by the majority of motorists. These include luxury sedans racing alone along  
twisting highways, pickup trucks crawling up rocky ascents in open mountain ranges,  
sport utility vehicles splashing through puddles along remote jungle roads, or sport  
coupes drifting through racetrack corners.  
These types of advertisements depend on the assumption that an elicitation of kin-  
aesthesia stimulates the consumers’ desire for their advertised vehicles. It is not clear  
whether planners presume that air terminal artworks that generate a sense of free and  
unrestricted kinaesthesia have an analogous functional role with respect to commerce  
at the terminal. Thus far, I have not found any planners that have explicitly stated that  
this is the case. Nevertheless, they do believe that artworks such as Slipstream, which 122 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
trigger a dynamic sense of movement, will add to the passengers’ enjoyment of transiting  
through the terminal, and their attention to the travellers’ enjoyment is often driven by  
commercial imperatives. As noted, airport planners try to put passengers in a good mood  
in the terminal, as positive emotions are associated with an increased desire to spend.  
Actual Movement\: Interactive and Kinetic Artworks  
The aforementioned aviation-themed artworks, which convey relatively free forms  
of movement, are all installations consisting of motionless objects. While most air  
terminal artworks are static constructions, there are some that include moving parts.  
Jeff de Boer’s When Aviation Was Young, which was installed at Calgary International  
Airport in 2002, is an interactive piece that consists of two towers, each supporting  
a carousel that has three airplanes attached to it \(Figure 4.9\). When the viewer winds  
a large toy-like key at the base of one of these towers, its carousel starts to spin, and  
the three aircraft come to life. Although these aircraft spur sensations of kinaesthesia  
when they are standing still \(since they are indexical signs of movement\), the sensa-  
tion of movement is notably amplified when they start to spin through the air in the  
concourse. However, the aircrafts’ movement is relatively controlled since they are  
tethered to a tower, and their travel path is far less jarring than Slipstream’s twisting  
trajectory. This engaging piece is situated next to the waiting areas for gates A15 to  
A20. It is also located near a consumer space—a Starbucks Coffee stand is adjacent  
to the work.  
Singapore’s Changi airport has also commissioned an interactive artwork, but it  
moves at a far slower speed. Christian Moeller’s Daisy, installed in Terminal 3’s land-  
side area in 2007, consists of a large fibreglass propeller attached to a steel column  
\(Figure 4.10\). The work’s round base is on the lower arrivals level near the exit to the  
roadway. Its column rises in a space between two bridges that link the departures area  
with the entrances from the upper access road. Moeller incorporated sensors into its  
design such that the propeller responds to people moving past the work. A robotic arm  
slowly angles the propeller up, down, left, and right. While Daisy is unpredictable with  
regard to its movement, the slow pace at which it changes direction hardly connotes  
the type of unrestricted movement conveyed by static artworks such as Slipstream or  
Les Oiseaux. Dynamic artworks moving in such a fashion would be unpopular with  
passengers; a model aircraft engaging in high-speed twisting turns in the concourse or  
a propeller swinging violently from side to side is undoubtedly frightening for those  
about to embark on a flight.  
Singapore Changi has commissioned a number of other kinetic artworks for its  
terminals. Humans since 1982’s A Million Times at Changi and ART+COM’s Kinetic  
Rain and Petalclouds include multiple objects that are synchronized to form changing  
patterns and morphing forms. These movements are not uncontrolled but are choreo-  
graphed to appear as smooth harmonious flows. Like many of the artworks discussed  
in this chapter, they are meant to affect the passengers’ moods. The airport’s website  
states that these works are “all part of the continuous drive to enhance the experience  
for our passengers and offer them a personalised, stress-free and positively surprising  
experience whenever they are at Changi Airport” \(Changi Airport n.d.a\).  
Planners installed Kinetic Rain in 2012 in Terminal 1 when the building was being  
renovated \(Shetty 2012; Changi Airport n.d.b\). This work consists of two assem-  
blages that each consist of 608 bronze forms shaped like water droplets \(Figure 4.11\). Figure 4.9 Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2019\).  
Jeff de Boer, When Aviation Was Young, Calgary International Airport, Canada, 2002. Figure 4.10 Christian Moeller, Daisy, Singapore Changi Airport, Singapore, 2007.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). Figure 4.11 ART+COM, Kinetic Rain, Singapore Changi Airport, Singapore, 2012.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). 126 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
The two 9.8-metre-long and 4-metre-wide ceiling panels have a motor and a thin cable  
attached to each droplet. The motors wind the cables up and down. The droplets are pro-  
grammed to move in unison and appear as falling rain or other three-dimensional forms  
in space. Kinetic Rain’s accompanying panel states that some of these are meant to convey  
birds and airplanes. But they largely appear as multiple variations of undulating waves.  
The work is also supposed to refer to Singapore’s tropical rain showers, and its large  
droplets certainly do call to mind this meaning. Passengers encounter Kinetic Rain in the  
landside departures area. The two assemblages are adjacent to the windows that face the  
access road and are near entrances into the building. They are also next to angled travella-  
tors that move people between the departures level and the arrivals level below.  
The airport also commissioned ART+COM to create a kinetic work for its new  
Terminal 4 which opened on October 31, 2017 \(Lee 2017\). Petalclouds consists of six  
assemblages of sixteen aluminium triangular-shaped rings that hang from the ceiling  
above the baggage claim area \(Figure 4.12\). Purple-hued light emanates from a band  
on the inner surface of each ring. Departing passengers also encounter this work.  
Glass windows on the landside and airside of the upper departures level allow one  
to see Petalclouds and look out over the baggage claim below. The assemblages are  
meant to convey clouds, and their triangular-shaped rings are inspired by orchid pet-  
als. Each of the six sections’ rings move up and down. The work’s informational  
panel explains that they are synchronized to create three types of motions\: circular,  
waves, or morph, where “individual clouds gradually morph to smaller fractions,  
reveal their own character before being consolidated to the bigger structure and being  
divided again.” Music composed by Ólafur Arnalds accompanies Petalclouds’ cho-  
reographed movement, and it is projected from speakers at various points in the ter-  
minal.22 ART+COM states that the “artwork’s harmonic choreography creates a calm  
atmosphere levelling the high activity in the other parts of the terminal” \(n.d.\).  
A Million Times at Changi, installed in Terminal 2’s landside departures area in  
2018, is inspired by a tool that underpins nearly all of the terminal’s functions—the  
clock \(Figure 4.13\).23 Humans since 1982’s 7.5-metre-wide and 3.4-metre-high white  
panel consists of 504 small circular analogue clocks with moving hands.24 The hands  
on each of the work’s clocks change to form different patterns. Their movement is  
continuous, and the ever-changing design is mesmerising. Occasionally, the grid of  
clock hands is configured to display the actual time as a numerical readout, and they  
momentarily stand still. A Million Times at Changi is situated near the airline check-  
in counters. The artwork, which is mounted to a wall facing the entrances from the  
access road, is installed in a similar fashion as the terminal’s electronic screens and  
mechanical split-flap displays that project departures information.  
Kinetic Rain, Petalclouds, and A Million Times at Changi all use software to coor-  
dinate their assemblages’ moving parts. This is notable in terms of their location in  
an airport since computer programs are integral to its operations. They facilitate the  
passengers’ purchase of airline tickets, direct their movements through the airport’s  
spaces, and form the complex algorithms required to propel their jet plane through  
the air. Martin Dodge and Rob Kitchin argue that air spaces are “code/spaces,” where  
“code dominates the production of space” \(2004, 198; italics in original\). They note  
that in the airport this mediation of code and space is used to observe and discipline  
both passengers and staff, and they consider this code/space in relation to Michel  
Foucault’s \(1977\) analysis of Jeremy Bentham’s Panopticon.  
25 Like Bentham’s nine-  
teenth-century penitentiary designs, the airport places everyone in the space under the Figure 4.12 ART+COM, Petalclouds, Singapore Changi Airport, Singapore, 2017.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). 128 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
Figure 4.13 Humans Since 1982, A Million Times at Changi, Singapore Changi Airport, Sin-  
gapore, 2018.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
watchful eye of databases, computer codes, surveillance cameras, and security checks;  
the terminal’s panopticism attempts to discipline these subjects into “docile bodies”  
that move through the space in an organized fashion \(Dodge and Kitchin 2004, 199\).26  
While Kinetic Rain’s, Petalclouds’, and A Million Times at Changi’s moving parts are  
all controlled via computer programs, I suspect that few passengers would think about  
software while viewing these works and even fewer would ponder how it is used to dis-  
cipline passengers and manage their movements. These works’ choreographed routines  
convey smooth flowing motions and are more likely to be perceived as an aesthetic piece  
that resembles a dance. In their artist statement for A Million Times at Changi, Humans  
since 1982 state\: “Metaphorically speaking, we liberated the clock from its sole function  
of measuring and reporting the time by taking the clock hands out of their ‘administrative’  
roles and turning them into dancers” \(Bischoff and Emanuelsson 2018\). Even though danc-  
ing in unison as a group requires order and timing, harmonious movements are generally  
more appealing than the monotonous and predicable ticking of a clock’s second hand.  
Time Tower at Los Angeles’s Tom Bradley International Terminal, discussed in Chap-  
ter 2, also incorporates a choreographed piece into the design of a mechanical clock;  
every hour, its screens depict a kaleidoscopic dance routine performed inside a gear-  
wheel-driven timepiece \(Figure 3.8\). A clock’s mechanical action is arguably similar to  
the airport’s mode of organizing passenger movement. Just as a clock’s cogs churn for-  
ward one at a time, each traveller is processed one after another in the various security  
zones, check-in counters, and boarding gates. Time Tower as well as Changi’s kinetic Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 129  
artworks depict smooth and harmonious flows that contrast with the stop-and-go pro-  
cess that defines the airport’s regulation of movement, and these pieces draw the pas-  
senger’s attention away from their experience of this type of ordered mobility.  
Troika’s Cloud at Heathrow Terminal 5 is a kinetic sculpture that incorporates a  
tool used during the 1970s and 1980s to direct passenger traffic \(Figure 4.14\). It is  
Figure 4.14 Troika, Cloud, London Heathrow Airport, United Kingdom, 2008.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\). 130 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
enveloped in flip dots, a system of alternating silver and black flaps used in airport signage.  
This technology is now largely replaced by digital readouts. The design brief asked for an  
artwork that responded to its location in the terminal \(McKeough 2008; MediaArtTube  
2010\). It is suspended between two escalators that move passengers between a busy air-  
side departures area and a quieter first-class lounge.27 Troika looked to an aviation-related  
theme to convey the transition from the bustling transit space to the “serene” lounge. Eva  
Rucki, one of Troika’s founding members, noted that Cloud is “a metaphor for a plane’s  
takeoff, traveling through a layer of clouds to reach calm skies above” \(cited in McKeough  
2008, 271\). The sculpture, which was installed in 2008, is a black flattened capsule-like  
shape that is 4.9 metres long and 2.1 metres wide. The 4,638 flip dots on its exterior are  
programmed to create patterns that change intermittently. Troika wanted Cloud, which  
recreates the sounds and the visual imagery of a now all but phased out technology, to  
prompt romantic notions of travel.28 Although this work is unlike the aforementioned  
aerial-themed artworks, it nonetheless has a kinaesthetic aspect in terms of its nostalgic  
reminder of earlier experiences of travel for some passengers.  
Like Kinetic Rain, Petalclouds, and A Million Times at Changi, Troika’s Cloud  
synchronizes its flip dots’ movements into flowing patterns. These types of kinetic  
works are analogous to the aerial-themed works that trigger sensations of unrestricted  
kinaesthesia—they stimulate an aesthetic of movement that differs from the type of  
movement experienced in the terminal. Moreover, they display a type of motion that is  
harmonious and smooth, and it contrasts with the airport’s filtering of passenger flows  
into ordered, stop-and-go movements.  
Artworks That Refer to Contemporary Technologies Used to  
Organize Movement  
Troika’s Cloud is notable as an aerial-themed artwork since there are few installations  
of this type that refer to tools and processes used to manage passenger flows. This  
section focuses on works that call the traveller’s attention to the technologies used  
to regulate movement in today’s airports. Unlike Cloud, they do not intend to evoke  
nostalgic memories of an earlier age of flight. Instead, they critically reflect on the  
contemporary experience of life in the terminal. These types of air terminal artworks  
differ from the norm since they clearly illustrate how the airport is designed to control  
a host of aeromobilities. After discussing two artworks at two US airports that address  
these themes, I concentrate on a series of artworks installed at Schiphol. These four  
site-specific works call attention to their role in ordering passenger movement. I argue  
that these sculptures deviate from the usual kinds of air terminal artworks and critique  
the functions they are intended to serve.  
Larry Kirkland’s Flight Patterns at San Francisco International’s Terminal 1 refers to  
the infrastructure that directs airplanes in and out of the airport’s airspace \(Figure 4.15\).  
Air traffic control imagery inspired the shape of the 1,500 thin aluminium objects that  
hang from the ceiling \(SFO Museum n.d.\). The X shapes denote aircraft, the triangles  
represent landmarks, and the dotted lines refer to boundaries. The work’s theme is  
remarkable with regard to its placement; it is adjacent to the entrance leading to the  
TSA’s pre-boarding security zone. Flight Patterns recalls an imagery of control in a  
space that immediately precedes one of the air terminal’s most secured zones.  
Mark Bradford’s Bell Tower, which hangs over the TSA’s pre-boarding screening  
area at Los Angeles’s Tom Bradley International Terminal, refers to the airport’s regu-  
lation of movement in a more abstract fashion \(Figure 4.16\). The 9.14-metre-tall and Figure 4.15 Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\).  
Larry Kirkland, Flight Patterns, San Francisco International Airport, United States, 1987.  
Figure 4.16 Mark Bradford, Bell Tower, Los Angeles International Airport, United States, 2014.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2018\). 132 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
7.93-metre-wide sculpture is constructed out of 712 panels and was installed in 2014  
\(Michno 2013; Hill, Smee, and Butler 2018\). The title refers to early modern bell  
towers which, as Bradford notes, were used for surveillance. But the work’s form is  
modelled after a ‘Jumbo Tron,’ a large-scale assemblage of screens that often hangs  
over a sports stadium’s arena. Bradford chose this shape since it refers to how screens  
and technology control people’s behaviours in contemporary society. At sports games,  
the Jumbo Tron directs the fans’ gaze to what is deemed important. Air terminals, as  
Bradford rightfully notes, employ screens for a similar purpose\: “You look up and it  
says gate number 37, and you just go to gate number 37. We’re so comfortable with  
being controlled” \(cited in Michno 2013\). Yet, this intended meaning is not immedi-  
ately apparent since Bell Tower does not include direct representations of surveillance  
such as security cameras.29 Nonetheless, as an air terminal artwork, Bell Tower is  
a notable exception from the norm since it is meant to provoke a critical reflection  
of how air terminals regulate passenger behaviour and movement. Bradford states\:  
“Whether you like it or not, it’s contemporary art in an airport, and it does exactly  
what I wanted it to do—makes people think” \(cited in Tomkins 2015\).  
Schiphol is decidedly the greatest outlier in this respect. In 1995, it installed a series  
of artworks that are meant to evoke thoughts about the experiential aspects of transit-  
ing through the terminal and flying commercially. Adams’s Coda, Holzer’s Untitled,  
Kemps’s Closed View, and Ruygrok’s So go on shed light on the restricted stop-and-go  
flows that passengers normally undergo in the airport and aboard commercial air-  
craft. An art committee consisting of Saskia Bos, director of the contemporary art gal-  
lery De Appel in Amsterdam, Wim Crouwel, the former director of Museum Boijmans  
Van Beuningen in Rotterdam, and Hans Smits, Schiphol’s president, commissioned the  
works. This committee was formed in response to the criticism levelled at some of the  
artworks installed in the airport in 1993 \(van Hooff 1998; Kloos and de Maar 1999\).  
Art critics panned works such as Mark Brusse’s I meet you and Hugo Kaagman’s  
Nice Trip, a series of Delft Blue tiles that combined traditional Dutch imagery such as  
windmills and tulips with patterns found in Islamic art \(Figure 1.5\). Critics considered  
these works, which portray overtly obvious Dutch themes, to be too one-dimensional,  
and the art committee agreed. Shortly after, they formulated a master plan that defined  
the types of artworks to be commissioned and their location in the terminal. The com-  
mittee sought to move away from pieces that they deemed to be simply decorative or  
touristic. Instead, they favoured artworks that engaged with themes that responded  
to the passengers’ experience of time and space. In addition, they stipulated that their  
location and signified meaning should respond to how they might be perceived by pas-  
sengers moving past or around these works.  
Ruygrok’s So go on, installed in the D-pier, consists of three rectangular stacks com-  
posed of two lit letters that rise to the ceiling \(Schiphol Group n.d.; van Hooff 1998\).  
It is intended to be read by passengers as they walk by the work. The three stacks spell  
out the three words of the work’s title, and they were positioned at different angles so  
travellers must move around the work to grasp its meaning. The phrase ‘so go on’ is  
meant to refer to the passengers’ passage through the D-pier—they need to go to their  
gate or the baggage claim. However, the work’s message, its title, can only be read if  
passengers interrupt their trajectory and move around the ceiling-high blocks.  
The committee also commissioned Adams’s Coda \(see Plate 6\). As noted, planners  
placed this piece in Schiphol Plaza, the entrance to the airport, and labelled it as an  
official meeting point. This cube-like prism, approximately four metres tall, has a Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 133  
red-and-white-checkered pattern that is used in aviation to indicate structures next to  
the runway.30 Due to its size and vibrant colouring, Coda is a distinctive object in the  
terminal’s interior, a space enclosed by glass windows and neutrally coloured floors,  
walls, and ceilings. This cube not only draws one’s attention to a sign used in avia-  
tion, but it also refers to how Coda functions as an obstacle that interrupts people  
moving in and out of the airport. It is a thing intended to be noticed and to be read  
as an impediment to these flows as it is the designated meeting point where some pas-  
sengers and visitors will choose to stop and wait.31 In this regard, Coda is a conceptual  
artwork that critically reflects upon its role as a distinctive landmark. This artwork  
is remarkable with regard to this book’s analyses. Coda, which looks appropriate for  
its purpose as a conspicuous thing for orientation, exemplifies the functional aesthetic  
outlined in Chapters 2 and 3; and its red-and-white checkered pattern is a symbolic  
connotation that reflects upon how its design effectively allows it to operate as an eye-  
catching landmark for wayfinding.  
Kemps’s Closed View likewise referred to the systems that order aircraft movement.  
It consisted of thirteen monitors laid in the floor of Departures 2 that reproduced  
the types of images seen on radar screens by air traffic control \(Koelemeijer 1995;  
Den Hartog Jager 1996\). Unfortunately this work has been removed. Closed View  
reminded air travellers that the air space their jet was about to enter was a highly  
controlled envelope of air, where jets are required to fly at specific altitudes, follow  
precise flight paths, and are under the constant watch of air traffic control. Like Kirk-  
land’s Flight Patterns, this depiction of a surveillance system recalled the extent of the  
regulation that the airport has over the passenger \(Figure 4.15\). It called the traveller’s  
attention to thoughts of control in an area where they were required to queue into a  
line that led to a check-in counter attendant who would examine their identity docu-  
ments and process their air tickets.  
Holzer’s Untitled is a long thin rectangular illuminated text box that hangs  
vertically from the ceiling \(Figure 4.17\). It is situated next to a stairwell between  
Departures 1 and 2. The work is visible from the pre-security landside as well as  
the airside. On the display, a wide range of sentences, mostly general statements  
that ask for some sort of philosophical reflection, continuously scroll down the  
text box. Unlike Ruygrok’s work which is a static presentation of text intended  
to be read by passengers moving around the work, Holzer’s stream of vertically  
rolling text demands the viewer to stand still to be able to read and comprehend  
the projected sentences. It pulls one’s attention, no matter how little or how unfo-  
cused, from moving through the terminal and entices one to pause and reflect on  
statements such as\: “a solid home base builds a sense of self; a strong sense of  
duty imprisons you; absolute submission can be a form of freedom; abstraction  
is a type of decadence; abuse of power comes as no surprise; action causes more  
trouble than thought.” At the time of its installation, the work pointed to an  
additional aspect of the traveller’s experience in the terminal. Judith Koelemeijer  
\(1995\) noted in de Volkskrant that Holzer’s work uses the same technology that  
commercial operations use to project advertisements. But, instead of reading a  
commercial advertisement as one would expect from such a sign, the passenger is  
presented with a philosophical statement.  
Even if the passenger only briefly pays attention to this work, it has the capacity  
to interrupt an otherwise ‘typical’ transit through this consumer space and mobility-  
system. As noted in Chapter 3, artworks are often deployed to interrupt the passenger’s Figure 4.17 Jenny Holzer, Untitled, Amsterdam Airport Schiphol, Netherlands, 1995.  
Source\: Menno Hubregtse \(2013\). Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 135  
trajectory through the terminal, and usually the intent is to slow them down such that  
they notice neighbouring shops and restaurants. Unlike de Boer’s When Aviation Was  
Young, which is located next to a café, and Time Tower, which is surrounded by duty-  
free stores and other retail outlets, Holzer’s Untitled invites passengers to spend time  
in a stairwell where there are no shops and restaurants nearby \(Figures 3.8 and 4.9\).  
The work’s placement in a location removed from consumption opportunities ampli-  
fies its critique of the terminal’s use as a shopping centre and a space that contains a  
captive audience for numerous advertisements.  
Schiphol has commissioned a number of artworks since this series was installed  
in 1995. But, these pieces do not offer a critical interpretation of one’s experience in  
the terminal. Tom Claassen’s Two Incredible Sitting Black Snowmen, commissioned  
in 2000, consists of two large male figures seated back to back on the floor in the  
D-pier. Their extended legs act as seats that passers-by can sit upon. During the same  
year, Schiphol commissioned Rob Birza’s Four Seasons. This series of four round discs  
shaped in the form of faces hang on the wall in a corridor leading to the B-pier. They  
are constructed out of a translucent material and backlit using coloured light.  
Adams’s Coda, Holzer’s Untitled, Kemps’s Closed View, and Ruygrok’s So go on  
differ from the usual types of air terminal artworks. Indeed, these installations resem-  
ble other flight-themed pieces since they refer to aspects of air transportation. How-  
ever, they depart from conventional flight-themed artworks that celebrate air travel.  
Coda, Untitled, Closed View, and So go on do not extol the ‘beauty’ of flight, cham-  
pion the innovations that have allowed people to fly, recall nostalgic memories of air  
travel in past times, or convey flight as an unrestrained form of movement. Instead,  
they offer a critical reflection of how passengers experience the actual passage through  
the air terminal—an interior space where their movements are managed, their identity  
is vetted, their body is scanned, and their attention is directed towards a plethora of  
consumption opportunities.  
While these four works are notable in that they address the realistic conditions of  
contemporary air travel, they are equally intriguing when they are considered as a  
counterpoint to the conventional practice of installing artworks in airports. Moreover,  
these four installations are conceptual artworks that are critically self-reflexive of their  
purpose as air terminal artworks. Rather than operating as a pleasurable distraction  
while one waits in the queue before being vetted at the check-in counter, Closed View  
reminds the viewer of the various forms of surveillance employed at the airport. So go  
on refers to how it, as an art installation, is meant to capture the passenger’s attention,  
though not for too long since it urges the traveller to move onwards. Coda’s checkered  
cladding not only refers to its own role as an intended distinctive landmark but also  
to how it, as an air terminal artwork, has been carefully deployed to organize passen-  
ger movement. Holzer’s Untitled, which interrupts the passenger’s trajectory using a  
medium typically used for advertising, makes light of the usual formula for deploying  
art in the terminal—place it near a consumer space to slow travellers down and, hope-  
fully, draw them into a store or restaurant.  
Conclusion  
This chapter has examined how air terminal design operates on an affective regis-  
ter. Air terminal architects carefully design these spaces such that they might trig-  
ger specific feelings or emotions in the air passenger. My analysis has focused on 136 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
artworks installed in air terminals and considered what types of sensations they might  
elicit, particularly those associated with kinaesthesia. Airport artworks often repre-  
sent themes pertaining to movement, and in many cases they depict types of move-  
ment that contrast with those typically experienced by the air traveller. Installations  
such as Wilson’s Slipstream and the Farman biplane at HKIA call to mind a freedom  
of movement that contrasts with the design intention of air terminals, which seeks  
to push passengers into particular directions and to hold them in various spaces of  
containment. Kinetic artworks such as Petalclouds and Kinetic Rain display harmoni-  
ous synchronized flows that contrast with the more mundane experience of moving  
through the terminal. Movement-themed artworks are placed in the terminal for the  
travellers’ enjoyment. In short, they offer an affective and aesthetic experience that  
shifts the passenger’s attention away, even if only momentarily, from their regulated  
passage through the airport.  
Bradford’s Bell Tower, Adams’s Coda, Holzer’s Untitled, Kemps’s Closed View, and  
Ruygrok’s So go on are defined by an entirely different aesthetic. These works are  
meant to draw the passenger’s attention towards their experience within the airport,  
and they are intended to operate on a cognitive level. Furthermore, their placement  
amplifies their meaning; they are site-specific conceptual artworks that are intended  
to provoke thoughts regarding the viewer’s and the work’s location in the terminal.  
The following chapter examines another type of work that is also meant to inspire  
thoughts regarding one’s location—specifically, the city, region, and country within  
which the airport is situated.  
Notes  
1\. Thrift’s \(2004\) conception of affect also relies on theories of embodied knowledge and  
physiological studies such as Charles Darwin’s writing on emotion and animals.  
2\. Tolia-Kelly argues that different “racialized, gendered and sexualized” bodies will have  
markedly different emotional responses to environmental conditions \(2006, 215\). Hayden  
Lorimer \(2008\) notes that criticisms such as Tolia-Kelly’s have helped geographers refine  
their notions of affect.  
3\. Tolia-Kelly \(2006\) argues that scholars need to be wary of Thrift’s \(2004\) article on affect  
and urban space since “there is a singularity of registers of affect and emotion declared in  
this extremely important call” \(2006, 214; italics in original\).  
4\. Anderson \(2006\) draws from Massumi who explains the difference between emotion and  
affect\:  
An emotion is a subjective content, the sociolinguistic fixing of the quality of an experience  
which is from that point onward defined as personal. Emotion is qualified intensity, the  
conventional, consensual point of insertion of intensity into semantically and semiotically  
formed progressions, into narrativizable action-reaction circuits, into function and mean-  
ing. It is intensity owned and recognized. It is crucial to theorize the difference between  
affect and emotion. If some have the impression that affect has waned, it is because affect  
is unqualified. As such, it is not ownable or recognizable and is thus resistant to critique.  
\(2002, 28\)  
5\. The artist’s YouTube channel includes a video where passengers are interviewed about their  
response to the work \(Studio Echelman 2011\).  
6\. See also Peter Kraftl and Peter Adey’s \(2008\) investigation of building interiors and affect.  
7\. The seven other works include Robert Charles Coyle’s Flight Song, Jonathan Borofsky’s I  
Dreamed I Could Fly, and Katharina Grosse’s Untitled \(Figures 3.6 and 4.4; see Plate 2\).  
8\. Aside from my analyses of air terminal design \(Hubregtse 2016, 2017\), Christopher  
Schaberg’s \(2012\) Textual Life of Airports is one of the few scholarly examinations of air  
terminal artworks. In his assessment of installations that represent forms of avian imagery, Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control 137  
he examines how Seyed Alavi’s Flying Carpet at Sacramento airport recreates the aerial  
view seen by a passenger or a pilot in an aircraft or by a bird in flight. This work, which is  
situated in a corridor that connects the parking garage to Terminal A, consists of a wool  
carpet that represents an eighty-kilometre section of the Sacramento River valley as seen  
from above. Schaberg notes that this aerial view allows the passenger walking along this  
corridor to adopt the “subject positions” of either a bird or an aircraft in flight \(2012, 137\).  
Although Schaberg suggests that the “human becomes animal,” he does not follow this line  
of thought into considering the kinaesthetic sensations associated with flying free over this  
valley \(2012, 137\). He compares this capacity for a bird’s eye view to a textual analogy of  
Internet browsing\: “Googling is a kind of species behavior, another form of flitting about”  
\(2012, 138\). And, he notes that passengers are able to engage in this type of reading at  
terminals that offer complimentary Wi-Fi.  
9\. Air France \(2015\) aired an advertisement that depicts an experience of flight that contrasts  
with the typical trip aboard a commercial airliner. Rather than being buckled into a seat  
in a small tube, young passengers sway back and forth harmoniously on swings in a large  
barrel vault lined with oval shapes that recall aircraft windows.  
10\. This definition is from Freud’s January 1894 article, “The Neuro-psychoses of Defence,”  
and it derives from his early investigations of how psychotherapy can treat hysteria.  
11\. The informational panel accompanying the work notes that it is a collaborative piece that  
incorporates paintings made by the airport employees’ children.  
12\. Gottdiener contends that “Peircian semiotics allows for the understanding of behaviorly  
oriented stimuli that are encoded in material forms as manipulative environments which  
operate through the power of the index and its experiential basis” \(1995, 13\). He uses  
Peirce’s semiotics to analyze “manipulative environments” such as shopping centres and  
amusement parks, two types of urban spaces which, like airport terminals, are carefully  
designed to facilitate people’s movements and stimulate consumption.  
13\. See also Carl Knappett’s \(2005\) discussion of how some indices are only known because of  
prior knowledge and other indices are signs dependent their performative nature.  
14\. The shapes for the central section of Santry’s work are “inspired by the flying figures” that  
are depicted in paintings found at China’s Dunhuang Caves \(Michel Santry Sculptor n.d.\).  
In Chapter 2, I explain why this installation was removed in 2003.  
15\. A panel applied to a pillar below includes Lai’s interpretation\: “‘Multiple Dimension’ pre-  
sents the aircraft in the future as the creation concept. As the advancement in aeronautic  
technology and intelligent transportation system, multiple links among time and space  
bring out the various dimensions and the unbounded imagination.”  
16\. Roger Freeman and his team at Vintage Aviation Services in Texas built the replica \(Clarke  
1997\). Freeman had to reconstruct the aircraft from photographs as there were no existing  
plans for the biplane. The original was destroyed at a riot after one of van den Born’s flights  
in Canton in 1911. Freeman’s replica has a 13.1-metre wing span, is 14.3 metres long, weighs  
544 kilograms, and has a top speed of 61 km/h. Freeman piloted the replica when it made  
its inaugural flight at Chek Lap Kok in November 1997 \(Smith 1997\). In April the following  
year, the biplane was moved into the terminal and suspended from the ceiling \(So 1998\).  
17\. The plaque notes that the Hong Kong Historical Aircraft Association commissioned the  
replica and was funded by Shell Hong Kong Limited.  
18\. For an insightful analysis of kinaesthetic feelings with respect to a different mobility-sys-  
tem, see Mimi Sheller’s \(2004\) discussion of how the embodied experiences of driving are  
tied to emotions and how this relates to the social and cultural aspects of automobilities.  
19\. See David Bissell’s \(2009\) discussion of train passengers’ kinaesthetic sensations and the  
accompanying views of passing landscapes and how the materiality of railway car interiors  
affects the passengers’ visual practices.  
20\. The Belgian aviator departed and landed at a long beach at Sha Tin at low tide \(Grindrod  
1993\). His inaugural flight had been advertised in the press and drew a number of specta-  
tors. Many left before van den Born took to the air as he had to delay his flight by three  
hours because of strong winds.  
21\. For a broader discussion of aircraft and aesthetics, see Rhodri Windsor Liscombe’s \(2004\)  
analysis of the jet engine’s impact on culture, social formations, and the Modern Movement.  
22\. For example, speakers are incorporated into the informational panels adjacent to the glass  
windows that offer views of the work from the departures level. 138 Affect, Kinaesthesia, and Control  
23\. The airport’s website notes that “the artwork elegantly mastered the dimension of time, a  
quality at the core of Changi Airport’s operations” \(Changi Airport n.d.a\).  
24\. Humans since 1982 consists of Bastian Bischoff and Per Emanuelsson.  
25\. In his prison designs, Bentham added a central tower from where the inspector could  
observe each prisoner in his cell. The inmates could not see the inspector in the tower or  
whether they were being observed. This panoptic architecture disciplined the prisoner by  
placing him under what appeared to be constant surveillance \(Foucault 1977\).  
26\. Dodge and Kitchin \(2004\) argue that the airport’s code/space encourages subjects to engage  
in what Foucault calls a “technology of the self”; passengers and staff are persuaded to  
discipline themselves when they encounter the technological structures of a disciplinary  
apparatus that is justified by airport authorities as a necessary tool to combat against secu-  
rity threats. See also Mark B. Salter’s \(2007\) analysis of how passengers reveal personal  
information to airport security in terms of Foucault’s “confessionary complex.” See Lyon  
\(2003\) and Adey \(2004\) for other analyses of air terminals and surveillance.  
27\. Heathrow has commissioned only two artworks for the spaces accessible to all passengers  
at Terminal 5\: Troika’s Cloud and Langlands and Bell’s Moving World \(Night & Day\).  
Neither is situated along a main passenger route. Cloud hangs between two escalators that  
lead to and from one of the British Airways \(BA\) lounges on the airside. Moving World,  
which I examine in Chapter 5, consists of two glass walls located outside at the opposite  
ends of the boulevard running between the main terminal building and the parking garage  
\(Figure 5.4\). Most of the artworks commissioned for the terminal hang in the BA lounges,  
which are reserved for business- and first-class passengers.  
28\. Troika’s Sebastien Noel contends that the sound the flip dots make as they change is  
“instantly reminiscent of travel” \(cited in Lucas 2008, 50\).  
29\. Bell Tower refers to the airport’s region as well. It is made of old plywood panels with worn  
posters and faded graffiti scavenged from construction sites in Los Angeles. A number  
of Bradford’s works are constructed out of found materials and mixed media \(Hill et al.  
2018\).  
30\. Coda has an opening on each of its four sides. Inside are twenty-eight backlit photographs  
of objects painted with the same red-and-white checkered pattern such as a windsock or a  
tower located along a runway or on an apron. These photographs were taken at airports  
in the former Dutch colonies; the place’s name appears at the bottom of each image. These  
pictures call to mind how Schiphol had facilitated the Netherlands’ reach into its colonies  
prior to decolonization.  
31\. Although Ruygrok’s So go on and Adams’s Coda refer to the actual experience of move-  
ment in the terminal, they are not blatant expressions of this experience but subtle point-  
ers to this kinaesthetic feeling. At Vancouver’s YVR, Patrick Amiot and Brigitte Laurent’s  
Flying Traveller clearly expresses the bodily movements that some passengers occasionally  
experience in the terminal. The sculpture, which depicts a man running with two suitcases,  
is a humorous rendition of one’s actual movements while rushing to catch a departing  
airplane. But, this was not the figure YVR’s Frank O’Neill \(1997\) had in mind when he  
approached Amiot and Laurent with a commission to create an acrylic-painted fibreglass  
sculpture for the terminal; he had asked for a sculpture depicting either one of coastal  
British Columbia’s two major resource industries—forestry and fishing. However, in his  
column on YVR’s artworks in the airport’s newsletter, O’Neill reports that Amiot and  
Laurent’s finished sculpture is “perfect for the airport” and that Flying Traveller has been  
popular with passengers transiting through the International Terminal \(1997, 5\).

Terminal.” October 20, 1997. LexisNexis Academic. 5 Place-Themed Designs  
# and Mobility  
Place-themed installations appear in numerous contemporary international airports.  
These types of designs include signifiers that point to the region’s cultural groups, built  
environment, and physical landscape. Planners usually install place-themed artworks  
and interior designs in order to stimulate passenger spending. This chapter examines  
how planners and architects incorporate these types of installations into terminals at  
airports such as Vancouver’s YVR, Amsterdam’s Schiphol, Bangkok’s Suvarnabhumi,  
Delhi’s Indira Gandhi, and Taipei’s Taoyuan International. I consider how these rep-  
resentations of place operate as markers for arrival and departure, and I address why  
planners believe that these installations will appeal to travellers. My investigation also  
addresses political aspects pertaining to these designs. In many cases, airport authori-  
ties choose installations that refer to only one or some of the cultural groups living  
within the region. I discuss the types of identities represented by designs placed near  
the terminal’s commercial areas as well as its border zones. While my analysis concen-  
trates on place-themed installations that refer to cultural groups, I also examine a few  
airports that include natural and pseudo-naturalistic settings to evoke a ‘sense of place.’  
Before addressing what motivates planners to install place-themed installations, I dis-  
cuss how these designs operate as symbols to convey meaning. This chapter’s analysis  
resembles the previous chapter’s investigation; it concentrates on air terminal design with  
regard to its symbolic connotations. Moreover, I do not examine how these designs are  
material things that mediate passengers’ actions, but I consider why representations of  
place are significant in terms of their meaning, especially with regard to their location in  
the terminal. Like my analysis of movement-themed artworks in Chapter 4, I assume that  
a place-themed installation will elicit variable responses among its viewers. However, in  
order to understand these representations, travellers usually require some knowledge of  
the region’s social and cultural contexts. My investigation addresses the meanings that  
planners expect to convey via these place-themed designs. They often explain their cul-  
tural significance via informational panels placed beside these installations. My analysis  
critically assesses these types of narratives; I examine how planners tend to highlight  
cultural groups traditionally associated with the airport’s region, and I discuss why these  
representations of identity are significant with regard to their location in a gateway to  
global destinations and a border zone that filters and screens passengers.  
Conveying Themes of Place Via Imagery and Design  
The last chapter employed Charles Sanders Peirce’s semiotics in order to analyze how  
movement-themed artworks can affect passengers. I illustrated how some of these Place-Themed Designs and Mobility 143  
works operate as indices, icons, and symbols with regard to Peirce’s theory. Architec-  
ture, artworks, and interior designs that refer to the airport’s region primarily func-  
tion as symbols when they convey place-themed connotations. For Peirce, a symbol’s  
meaning depends on information pertaining to its social and cultural context \(Gottdi-  
ener 1995\). Usually, the degree to which one can understand an artwork’s or design’s  
place-themed representational content is contingent upon one’s familiarity with the  
place’s social and cultural relations.  
Kang HeeRa’s Hello at Seoul Incheon’s Terminal 2 is a place-themed work that is  
literally composed of symbols \(see Plate 24\). It consists of one thousand Hangeul char-  
acters of the Korean alphabet suspended from a rectangular panel that hangs from the  
ceiling. Hello was installed in the landside area near the airline check-in counters when  
the terminal opened in 2018. The consonants and vowels contain LED lights, and the  
installation periodically lights up and changes colours; the emitted light reflects off of  
the terminal’s stone flooring. In this case, the passenger can only grasp that the piece  
connotes Korean culture if they recognize that the one thousand suspended objects  
are used as characters in Korean texts. What’s more, Hello’s representational content  
is remarkable with regard to international air terminal design. This jumble of Korean  
characters contrasts with the terminal’s wayfinding signage which uses a minimal  
amount of text. Like many of the terminals discussed in the previous chapters, Seoul  
Incheon’s Terminal 2 is a large open concourse where people can use the ceiling to ori-  
ent themselves in the space, and its planners have installed a limited amount of signage.  
Some of the signs, such as those indicating the zones for the airline check-in counters,  
are comprised of only one capitalized Latin letter. A sign indicating the D-section hangs  
from the ceiling near HeeRa’s installation. Indeed, Hello asserts a Korean cultural iden-  
tity in an international node for air travel that uses Latin letters for wayfinding.  
Steve Smith’s Freedom to Move at Vancouver’s YVR also operates as a symbol in  
order to convey its place-themed meaning \(see Plate 13\). Instead of using text, this  
installation employs a distinct visual language to signify the airport’s region. Smith’s  
cedar panels depict a raven, an eagle, a grizzly bear, and a killer whale in formline, a  
Northwest Coast Indigenous style that is characterized by ovoid forms and U-shapes.  
Similar to HeeRa’s Hello, only those familiar with this mode of depicting subject  
matter will recognize that this piece points to the region within which the airport is  
located. This information, however, can be garnered via the adjacent panel. It explains  
which animals are represented by Smith’s formline designs.  
Unlike Freedom to Move which depicts animals on a two-dimensional surface, Bill  
Reid’s Spirit of Haida Gwaii\: The Jade Canoe renders animal and human figures in  
three dimensions \(see Plate 1\). Passengers unfamiliar with Northwest Coast artworks  
can recognize that this sculpture consists of a boat packed with a number of figures  
who are paddling and steering the watercraft. In this respect, Reid’s piece operates as  
an icon in terms of Peirce’s semiotics since it is a sign that resembles the object it is  
intended to signify. The Jade Canoe, however, also operates as a symbol since the work  
connotes a number of meanings pertaining to the Haida’s culture. Its informational  
panel includes a quote by Reid which explains that this is a traditional Haida dugout  
canoe with “thirteen supernatural creatures, each related in some way to the Haida’s  
mythical past.” They include the Mouse Woman, the Beaver, the Dogfish Woman, the  
Raven, the Wolf, the Frog, the Eagle, the Ancient Reluctant Conscript, and the Bear  
with his human wife and their twin cubs. Reid states that “the tall figure in the middle  
of it all . . . may or may not be the Spirit of the Haida Gwaii.” 144 Place-Themed Designs and Mobility  
Some air terminals signify themes of place via the building’s architectural design.  
At Jakarta’s Soekarno-Hatta International Airport, Paul Andreu modelled the termi-  
nals’ structures after local building types \(Jodidio and Andreu 2004\). He designed  
Terminals 1 and 2, which opened in 1985 and 1991 respectively, to recall Indonesian  
villages with red-tiled roofed houses separated by greenspace. Both terminals have  
semi-circular footprints with three piers radiating outwards. Each pier extends into  
a natural setting which contains seven separate square-shaped buildings that house  
the gates’ waiting areas. These structures have a hipped roof similar to a Javanese  
joglo house, and they are connected to the pier via passageways that cross through  
the greenery. The terminals’ structural supports also signify traditional building styles.  
The tubular steel posts and beams are visible within the interior, and their structural  
form is based on Indonesian bamboo-framed buildings.  
Skidmore, Owings & Merrill’s Hajj Terminal at the King Abdulaziz International  
Airport in Jeddah also connotes a local traditional architectural form. The building’s  
roof, which recalls a Bedouin tent, is constructed out of fabric and supported by cables  
\(Skidmore, Owings & Merrill n.d.\). The terminal opened in 1981, and it accom-  
modates Muslim pilgrims travelling to Mecca, which is situated approximately sev-  
enty-five kilometres southwest of the airport. Fentress Bradburn Architects’ Denver  
International Airport, which opened in 1995, also employs a fabric roof that refers to  
local forms. The terminal’s numerous steep-pitched white peaks evoke the surrounding  
Rocky Mountain range as well as Native American tepees \(Albrecht 2012\). Fentress  
Bradburn, however, did not choose this type of roof strictly for its place-themed con-  
notations. Rather, the thin membrane ceiling was a cost-effective and efficient design  
that met the airport’s purposes.  
When Beijing Capital Airport’s Terminal 3 is seen from the sky, it conveys a sym-  
bolic meaning pertaining to the People’s Republic of China. The building’s footprint  
consists of two triangular shapes on the northern and southern sides of the terminal.  
These two triangles’ sides curve inwards, and they loosely recall the Chinese char-  
acter 人 \(rén\) which means ‘people’ and ‘person.’ Netherlands Airport Consultants  
\(NACO\) designed the building’s footprint in collaboration with Foster + Partners and  
ARUP when they were competing for the building’s contract. NACO devised its shape  
based on the terminal’s placement between the airport’s runways and the number of  
gates on the airside \(Hein Baijer and Piet Ringersma, pers. comm., April 10, 2018\).  
The architects and planners realized the symbolic connotation after they had deter-  
mined the footprint, and they decided to highlight it as a place-themed quality in their  
competition documents.1 NACO, Foster + Partners, and ARUP won the contract in  
2003 and completed the terminal in time for the 2008 Olympic Games in Beijing \(Fos-  
ter + Partners 2003\). In its press release that announces the terminal’s official opening  
in February 2008, Foster + Partners states that its design is “\[a\] symbol of place, its  
soaring aerodynamic roof and its dragon-like form celebrate the thrill and poetry  
of flight. Its gold roof resonates with the Forbidden City, while the striking interior  
palette of red through orange to yellow evokes traditional Chinese colours” \(2008\).  
Deploying Place-Themed Interior Designs and Artworks to  
Stimulate Spending  
While airports such as Jakarta’s Soekarno-Hatta, Jeddah’s King Abdulaziz Interna-  
tional Airport, Denver International Airport, and Beijing Capital Airport convey Place-Themed Designs and Mobility 145  
symbolic place-themed meanings via their terminals’ structural supports and ceil-  
ings, planners tend to evoke a sense of place via the terminal’s interior design and  
artworks. As discussed in Chapter 1, the air terminal’s architectural design \(e.g., its  
footprint, elevation, and ceiling\) is determined by the runways’ location, the number  
of gates, ground transportation access, and passenger circulation. Interior designs and  
artworks are less cumbersome to renovate and replace than an air terminal’s exterior  
shell, and they can be easily changed to accord with passengers’ tastes. At Schiphol,  
for instance, planners have renovated its Holland Boulevard and moved the Rijksmu-  
seum to a different location in this Dutch-themed corridor. The refurbished Holland  
Boulevard and Rijksmuseum reopened in 2017, fifteen years after the museum space  
was first installed in the terminal. This section concentrates on why planners deploy  
these types of place-themed interior designs and artworks to stimulate spending and  
what types of symbolic imagery they choose for this purpose.  
Robbie Gill \(2012\), the director of a firm specializing in the design of air terminal  
consumer spaces, notes that even though there is little evidence that points to the com-  
mercial gains of place-themed designs, most airports aspire to design a terminal or at  
least a duty-free store that evokes a “sense of place” to entice passenger spending.2  
Vancouver’s YVR, which is pictured on the title page of Gill’s article, is perhaps the  
quintessential example of an airport that tries to establish a sense of place.3 Its plan-  
ners and architects have installed numerous Northwest Coast First Nations artworks  
to stimulate spending.4  
The Vancouver International Airport Authority \(VIAA\) began to almost exclu-  
sively commission these types of artworks while it was planning its new International  
Terminal during the early 1990s. This was part of its “North Star” strategic plan,  
established in 1993, that sought to profit from British Columbia’s appeal as a tourist  
location \(Vancouver International Airport Authority 1995\). During this same year, the  
VIAA purchased Reid’s The Jade Canoe for three million Canadian dollars \(CAD\). In  
1994, the VIAA’s director, Peter Newman, defended this expenditure and stated that  
“the sculpture will eventually pay for itself through the sale of souvenir replicas, and  
as a tourist attraction” \(cited in Vancouver Sun 1994\).  
The “North Star” plan also aimed to transform the airport into a significant North  
American destination and transit hub. In the VIAA’s \(1995\) update on this plan, the  
airport included a picture of a globe with Vancouver’s YVR as a central hub between  
Asian, Australian, European, and South American destinations. Aside from Vancou-  
ver and Honolulu, only Asian cities are marked on the globe. They include Singapore,  
Hong Kong, Seoul, and Osaka. Indeed, the VIAA was trying to attract Asian travellers  
to fly to Vancouver as a destination or as a transfer point for flights heading to other  
North American locations.  
As noted in Chapter 3, the VIAA placed Reid’s The Jade Canoe next to the termi-  
nal’s food court \(Figure 3.10; see Plate 1\). YVR’s architects and planners expected  
that this material thing would draw people towards this consumer space, and they  
also believed that the work’s representational content would stimulate retail sales \(Bell  
1996\). They estimated that The Jade Canoe’s symbolic connotation of British Colum-  
bia’s coastal Indigenous peoples would entice passenger spending. Like most of the  
works installed at YVR, its accompanying panel includes a brief artist biography and  
a description of the installation’s meaning. As discussed previously, it explains the  
work’s significance with regard to the Haida’s culture. This information undoubtedly  
amplifies the traveller’s notion that they are in a novel place. Reid’s The Jade Canoe 146 Place-Themed Designs and Mobility  
illustrates how this sense of place is used for commercial purposes. Not only is it situ-  
ated near a café and a number of restaurants, it is also just a few steps away from the  
Lattimer Gallery which sells carvings by Northwest Coast First Nations artists as well  
as blankets, pepper mills, mugs, and water bottles illustrated with Northwest Coast  
First Nations designs.5  
Not all of the terminal’s Indigenous artworks are situated beside stores that sell  
locally crafted goods. Some of these installations are placed beside stores that carry a  
variety of global brands. For instance, Lyle Wilson and John Nutter’s Orca Chief and  
the Kelp Forest and Dempsey Bob’s The Story of Fog Woman and Raven are located  
in the International Terminal’s airside near luxury retailers such as Burberry, Omega,  
Cartier, and Gucci \(see Plate 21\).  
Vancouver’s airport has been successful in terms of generating spending and increas-  
ing its passenger traffic. In a 2012 survey, the airport ranked second in North America  
with regard to dollars spent per passenger \(Lee 2012\). The average amount spent was  
20.11 CAD. The North American airport with the highest per capita spending was  
New York’s John F. Kennedy International where passengers spent on average 24.07  
CAD. Asian tourists were YVR’s highest spenders, especially those from mainland  
China. In 2013, the airport reported that it received its greatest single transaction—a  
70,000 CAD purchase by a Chinese customer \(Luke 2013\). Three years earlier, the  
airport began to cater its wares to the increasing number of Chinese travellers. Susan  
Steine, the VIAA’s director of commercial services, stated that  
we were looking at the Chinese market because we knew we were growing at a  
huge rate from a Chinese perspective. . . . We actually will take a look at the diver-  
sification of passengers and where the growth is in the various markets. When  
we see that there is a specific nationality that is going to be growing, we will go  
out and interview the existing \[passengers\] who are coming through and find out  
what we are missing here at the airport.  
\(cited in Lee 2012, 1\)  
The VIAA found that this demographic preferred to purchase expensive whiskys as  
souvenirs, and it began selling bottles with prices in the tens of thousands of dollars.  
In November 2018, YVR’s worlddutyfree store was selling a bottle of 1937 Macallan
