art/musqueam-welcome-area. Conclusion  
I have illustrated how architects and planners design air terminal interiors to affect  
passenger behaviour and to actively contribute to the airport’s operation. These inte-  
riors, which often include artworks, support the terminal’s primary function which is  
to move air travellers from the curb to the plane and to encourage them to spend their  
money while they spend their time waiting. Ceiling designs, flooring patterns, lighting,  
artworks, and many other aspects of the building’s interior are designed to direct pas-  
sengers to walk along routes in the terminal that are lined with numerous stores and  
restaurants. Most new terminals are large open concourses with floor-to-ceiling glass  
walls, and architects incorporate sightlines facing the apron since views of the aircraft  
help with orientation. Architects and planners use these strategies to make it easier for  
passengers to navigate the terminal and to limit the amount of signage in the space.  
Moreover, they attempt to create an interior where travellers follow intuitive cues and  
do not need to rely on signs for every step of the way.  
These planning principles, which assume that the material aspects of air terminal  
design can facilitate passengers’ actions, resemble one of actor-network theory’s cen-  
tral concerns—non-human things have agency and can affect social relations. I have  
concentrated on designs such as skylights and flooring patterns that predominately  
operate on a visual register to direct passenger movement. I argue that these designs  
constitute an aesthetic type determined by their purpose and that this functional aes-  
thetic pertains to their effectiveness for wayfinding. My analysis, however, has not  
concentrated on judging air terminal designs for their value. But, by theorizing air ter-  
minal design in terms of a functional aesthetic, I have outlined some of the parameters  
for those who do wish to cast a judgement on these things’ aesthetics and efficacy.  
In some cases, architects and planners install air terminal artworks to aid with  
passenger navigation. These pieces are material and visual cues that support wayfind-  
ing, and they constitute a similar functional aesthetic. Architects and planners often  
choose conspicuous installations as eye-catching landmarks for orientation, and they  
usually place them near food courts and stores to draw travellers into these consumer  
spaces. They also place artworks and designs such as water walls near the airport’s  
screening zones in order to calm passengers. Indeed, architects and planners select  
artworks with particular material qualities based on their purpose as a landmark or  
as a pacifying installation. Yet, they also choose pieces with specific types of symbolic  
imagery. Airport authorities often commission artworks and other interior designs  
that convey themes related to the region in which the airport is located. They presume  
that these installations, which lend to the terminal’s sense of place, will positively  
affect passengers’ moods and spur them to spend. Conclusion 179  
Another prevailing theme among air terminal artworks is movement. These installa-  
tions often connote types of movement that are relatively free and unrestricted. I argue  
that this type of imagery is able to affect passengers and generate sensations of kin-  
aesthesia. The tendency to install artworks defined by an aesthetic of unregulated  
movement is notable when considering the environment they are located in—one that  
is purposely designed to organize passengers into directional flows. These representa-  
tions of unrestricted movement are conveyed by works consisting of static compo-  
nents. There are some kinetic artworks that also depict movement, but these works  
tend to replicate smooth harmonic flows. Singapore Changi has installed these types  
of pieces to create a calming ambiance that alleviates passenger anxiety. Like repre-  
sentations of unconstrained movement, these depictions of harmonic motions contrast  
with the stop-and-go processes that airports employ to direct passenger streams, and  
they divert the traveller’s attention away from their experience of regulated mobility  
in the terminal.  
In this book, I have examined what architects and planners expect passengers to  
feel when they interact with these installations. I have also speculated on how these  
artworks might affect passengers, and I have considered how this experience can be  
different for each traveller. However, this analysis is based on the designers’ inten-  
tions and my perspective and not on the experiences of the millions of passengers that  
view these works each year. Even though this analysis of the affective potential of  
air terminal artworks is based on an assumption of how these things might affect air  
travellers, it illustrates the types of effects air terminal planners believe artworks will  
have on passengers. In short, airport authorities commission and install artworks that  
they believe will buoy one’s mood, and they hope that this will stimulate consumption.  
Yet, the co-relation between increased passenger spending and artworks has not been  
proven. The actual effect that these artworks have on air travellers is indeed a topic  
for future study.  
This investigation also demonstrates how air terminal artworks constitute a unique  
form of public artwork. Other commercial spaces such as shopping centres and down-  
town retail streets incorporate artworks; however, these places do not contain their  
publics for extended periods of time. Air terminals, on the other hand, hold pas-  
sengers in commercial areas such as post-security departure lounges. In many cases,  
travellers are required to walk past an artwork or wait in a space next to an instal-  
lation. Passengers departing from Schiphol’s Departure Hall 3 must walk past Daan  
Roosegaarde’s Beyond on their way to the pre-boarding security area, and travellers  
arriving on international flights at Delhi Indira Gandhi’s Terminal 3 must stand below  
Ayush Kasliwal’s oversized mudras while they wait in the border screening area \(Fig-  
ure 4.1; see Plate 3\). The latter artwork illustrates how air terminal installations are  
a noteworthy and distinctive type of public art due to their placement in a node that  
filters flows of people across borders. For many travellers, their first actual impression  
of a country occurs within the air terminal. Artworks, particularly those that convey  
themes of place, suggest what is culturally significant for the nation. Furthermore, air  
terminal installations are steadily becoming more meaningful for more people; with  
the rise of low-cost airlines, more people are choosing to fly and passenger traffic  
keeps increasing in today’s terminals.  
International airports are part of a global infrastructure that assists the flows of peo-  
ple and capital. This book demonstrates that their terminal buildings—designed largely  
in terms of utilitarian purposes—should not be dismissed as entirely characterless 180 Conclusion  
‘non-places’ lacking aesthetic consideration. Air terminals are designed by architects  
and planners who make aesthetic choices that are particular to each location and are  
meant to fulfil the functional aims of these carefully engineered spaces, which are  
experienced materially, affectively, aesthetically, and kinaesthetically by the passengers  
transiting through them. Index
